THE RED PAGE.
Far in the Eastern passage-way a sudden light ;
The stone that blocked the sepulchre is backward
rolled ;
And down into the foetid, stifling vault of Night
The naked corse of Dawn is lowered, grey and
cold.
A.H.A.
Tiib twelfth of May, 1796, in North Italy, at Tavazzano,
on the road from Lodi to Milan. . . . Two days before,
at Lodi, the Austrians tried to ‘prevent the French from
crossing the river by the narrow bridg'd there ; but the
French, commanded by a general aged 21, Napoleon
Bonaparte, who does not understand the art of war,
rushed the fire-swept bridge, supported by a tremendous
cannonade in which the young general assisted with his
own hands. Cannonading is his technical speciality : he
has been trained in the artillery under the old regime, and
made perfect in the military arts of shirking his duties,
swindling the paymaster over travelling expenses, and
dignifying war with the noise and smoke of cannon, as
depicted in all military portraits. He is, however, an
original observer, and has perceived, for the first time
since the invention of gunpowder, that a cannon ball, if it
strikes a man, will kill him. To a thorough grasp of this
remarkable discovery, he adds a highly evolved faculty for
physical geography and for the calculation of times and
distances. He- has prodigious powers of work, and a clear,
realistic knowledge of human nature in public affairs,
having seen it exhaustively tested in that department
during the French Revolution. He is imaginative with-
illusions, and creative without religion', loyalty,
patriotism, or any of the common ideals. Not that he is
incapable of these ideals ; on f,lie contrary, he has swal-
lowed them all in his boyhood, and now, having a keen
dramatic faculty, is extremely clever at playing upon
them by the arts of the actor and stage-manager. Withal,
he is no spoiled child. Poverty, ill-luck, the shifts of im-
pecunious shabbv-geritility, repeated failure as a would-
be author, humiliation as a rebuffed time-server, reproof
and punishment as an incompetent and dishonest officer,
an escape from dismissal from the service so narrow that
if the emigration of the nobles had not raised the value of
even the most rascally lieutenant to the famine price of a
general, he would have been swept contemptuously from
the army ; these trials have ground the conceit out of
hiin'l and forced him to be self sufficient and to understand
that to such men as he is the world will give nothing that
he cannot take from it by force. In this the world is not
free from cowardice and folly ; for Napoleon, as a merci-
less cannonader of political rubbish, is making himself
useful; indeed, it is even now impossible to live in Eng-
land without,sometimes feeling how much that country
lost in not being conquered by him as well as by Julius
Caesar.
* * » *
However, on this May afternoon in 1796, it is early days
with him. He is only 26, and has but recently become a
general, partly by using his wife to seduce the Directory
(then governing France); partly bv the scarcity of officers
caused by the emigration as aforesaid ; partly by his
faculty of knowing a country, with all its roads, rivers,
hills and valleys, as he knows the palm of his hand ; and
largely by that new faith of his in the efficacy of firing
cannons at, people. His army is, as to discipline, in a
state which has so greatly shocked some modern writers
before whom the following story has been enacted, that
they, impressed with the later glory of “ L’Empereur,”
have altogether refused to credit, it. But Napoleon is
not “ L’Empereur” yet; he has only just been dubbed
“Le Petit Oaporal,” and is in the stage of gaining influ-
ence over his men by display of pluck. He is not in a
position to force his will on them, in orthodox military
fashion, by the cat o’ nine tails. The French Revolution,
which has escaped suppression solely through the
monarchy’s habit of being at least four years in arrear
with its soldiers in the matter of pay, has substituted for
that habit, as far as possible, the habit of not paying at
all, except in promises and patriotic flatteries 'which are
not compatible with martial law of the Prussian type.
Napoleon has therefore approached the- Alps in command
of men without money, in rags, and consequently indis-
posed to stand much discipline, especially from upstart
generals. This circumstance, which would have embar-
rassed an idealist soldier, has been worth a thousand
cannon to Napoleon. He has said to his army, “You
have patriotism and courage ; but, you have no money, no
clothes, and hardly anything to eat. In Italy there are
all these things, and glory as well, to be gained by a
devoted army led by a general who regards loot as the
natural right of the soldier. I am such a general. En
avant, m es enfants!" The result has entirely justified
him. The army conquers Italy as the locusts conquered
Cyprus. They fight all day and march all night, covering
impossible distances and appearing in incredible places,
not because every soldier carries a field marshal’s baton
in his knapsack, but because he hopes to carry at least
half-a-dozen silver forks there next day.
« * * *
It must be understood, by the way, that the French
army does not make war on the Italians. It is there to
rescue them from the tyranny of their Austrian con-
querors, and confer republican institutions on them ; so
that in incidentally looting them it merely makes free
with the properiy of its friends, who ought t.o be grateful
to it, anil perhaps would be if ingratitude were not the
proverbial failing of their country. The Austrians, whom
it fights, are a thoroughly respectable regular army, well-
disciplined, commanded by gentlemen versed in orthodox
campaigning at the head of them Beaulieu, practising
the classic art of. war under orders from Vienna, and
getting horribly beaten bj r Napoleon, who acts on his own
responsibility in defiance of professional precedents or
orders from Paris. Even when the Austrians ,win a bat-
tle, all that is necessary is to wait until their routine
obliges them to return to their quarters for afternoon tea,
so to speak, and win it back again from them ; a course
pursued later on with brilliant success at Marengo.
On the‘whole, with his foe handicapped by Austrian
statesmanship, classic generalship, and the exigencies of
the aristocratic social structure of Viennese society,
Napoleon finds it possible to be irresistible without work-
ing heroic miracles. The world, however, likes miracles
and heroes, and is quite incapable of conceiving the action
of such forces as academic militarism or Viennese draw-
iugmomism. Hence it has already begun to manufacture
“ L’Empereur.”
— •».
is a passage from “ The Man of Destiny,”
a one-ar;t play by Bernard Shaw. It is quoted to
show | Shaw’s breadth and strength at his best
period, his rare suggestive force of mind and
phrase. No journalist in our time has. sown his
work more thickly with original ideas ; none has
, expressed his ideas in 'more luminous language.
Men like Stead and Steevens have described
people and envirhnments as well or better ; but
neither Stead nor Steevens has Shaw’s fundamen-
tal brain-power, or his peculiar instinct for gener-
alisation, his talent for squeezing from evanes-
cent facts the juice of the essential law.
Steevens’s “With Kitchener to Khartum,”
considered as a piece of literature, has no
perspective. It is a brilliant account of
a military episode of 1898; discussed separ-
ately and superficially. Shaw’s description
of the episode would probably have been less
picturesque, less vivid, but far more vital. He
would have created thoughts where Steevens, has
been content to image tilings. Steevens takes for
granted England and the Soudan, the English and
the Soudanese, the status quo and its results
possible and actual. Shaw would have asked why
these countries, men, events, should develop in
this way, and asked justification of the develop-
ment by ethical and aesthetic standards. Steevens
considers Kitchener as a cardinal fact : to
Shaw he is a cipher in the sum of human
destiny. Steevens sees trees; Shaw, the whole
landscape. Behind Steevens’s picture there is
nothing; behind Shaw’s would be the back-
ground of Life and Art, and Kitchener’s campaign
would appear in due relation and proportion to its
significance in Life and Art. The men’s methods
are a consequence of their minds, and there can
be no question that the generalising mind is the
more valuable factor in human progress.
* * * *
As Shaw’s mental sight subtends a wider angle
than most men’s, he is necessarily heterodox. That
is his special merit: his judgments are illuminated
by a light from beyond the common horizon.
It seems partly a freakish exultation in Lis
strength which carries him past heterodox into
paradox ;. yet the paradox of to-day is the
orthodox of to-morrow, and Shaw’s controver-
sions of accepted theories may prove to hold his
best titles to esteem. For illustration : R. G-.
Ingersoil has done splendid work as an advo-
cate of free thought and free speech ; but he is rhe-
torician rather than thinker: he readily accepts con-
ventional views and standpqints. (The conventions
of atheism and agnosticism are nowadays almost
as well-marked as those of deism and fetichism.)
So, in discussing Napoleon in a passage often bor-
rowed by , predatory ‘ ‘ orators, ” he reviews in
imagination the incidents of Napoleon's career,
compares N’s life with that of a peasant living in a
hut with a vine growing over the door and the
grapes growing purple in the amorous kisses of the
autumn sun, and declares that ‘ ‘ I would rather
have, been that poor peasant, with my loving wife
by my side, knitting as the day died out of the sky
—with my children upon my knees and their arms
about me—l would rather have been that man,
and gone down to the tongueless silence of the
dreamless dust, than to have been that imperial
impersonation of force and murder, known as
Napoleon the Great.” Ingersoil, though Ameri-
can, has adopted the. conventional British idea
of the Corsican bogey, the monster who deluged
Europe with futile blood. Shaw, an Irishman,
hints in the above-quoted excerpt some com-
prehension of Fyffe’s contrary view (History
of Modern Europe, chap, xi.) that Napoleon con-
tinued and concentrated the social regenerative
force of the French Revolution; that the ele-
ments of violence, and disturbance in the
Napoleonic age will come to fill less space
in the eyes of mankind, its permanent crea-
tions more ; and that “it is not now easy to
suppress the doubt whether the permanent interest
of mankind would not have been best served by
Napoleon’s success in 1812.” That is, Shaw has
clutched the skirts of the historical paradox
—which next century will accept as a histori-
cal truth —that Napoleon was the greatest social
regenerator of modern times, one of the greatest
forces ever devoted to the welfare and progress of
humanity, while the league against him was a
league of stagnating barbarism and hereditary
tyranny whose additions to the sum of human
misery infinitely outweighed the bloodshed caused
in the accomplishment of Napoleon’s enlightened
ambitions.
« • * • ■
Shaw’s seven plays, divided into four “pleas-
ant ” and three “unpleasant,” are clever in them-
selves and interesting as manifestations of the
man. In a sense, they are the weightiest plays
which the England of Shaw’s generation has pro-
duced : their force and earnestness approxi-
mate them to Ibsen’s. The method and results of
Shaw, in fact, might be roughly held to place him
midway between Ibsen and Pinero, while H. A.
Jones stands midway between Shaw and Pinero.
There are so many exceptions and reservations,
however, that the comparison is given only for its
suggestion. Wilde has affinities with both Pinero
and Shaw, but he moves on a different plane.
Shaw’s method is as clearly literary as Pinero’s
is dramatic. While Pinero puts stage representa-
tion first, Shaw is first concerned with the repre-
sentation of his idea. He seems grudging every
concession made to the inadequacy of the stage to
develop and display ideas in the literary way.
The result is that while Pinero’s plays double or
treble their force when acted, Shaw’s seem to a
reader so built that acting would aid them but
little. Here again passages and whole plays must
be . excepted from the dictum. But it may stand
to indicate the totality of comparative effect.
* * * *
Shaw prefaces his plays with introductions
containing, observations on the drama; Some-
times he is amusing ; often his conclusions are
debatable. The printed plays are much more
fully annotated with stage directions than is
usual; and for the most part the notes help more
than they hinder, Shaw gives in words, in fact,
the impression one would receive through sis eyes
if se saw the characters on a stage. A minor
novelty is the dropping of apostrophes in words
like “dont,” “youll,” “isnt”; but Shaw does not
venture to write “ Im ” for “I’m,” or “111” for
“ I’ll, ” though he passes “itll” —which does not
look seducing.
The author calls his first three plays “ unplea-
sant ” because “their dramatic power is used to
force the spectator to face unpleasant facts.. . . .
In ‘Widowers’ Houses,’ I have shown middle-
class respectability and younger-son gentility
fattening on the poverty of the slums as flies fatten
on filth.” It is a strong play, very well written,
but the moral is too obvious—the writer lias too
much the air of an advocate arguing from a client’s
brief. Instead of suggesting the climax he would
bring you to, he takes you by the scruff cj>f the
neck and throws you at the climax. His art is
too downright, provoking revulsion. So you
admire the play as a clever play, irrespective of
purport; and the moral goes hang.
“The Philanderer” attacks current marriage
compacts and customs ; but just as ineffectively as
“Widowers’ Houses” attacks slum landlords, and
—strangely enough—for the very opposite reason :
the moral'is not obvious enough. “ The Philan-
derer ” leaves the impression of an amusing
extravaganza.
In “Mrs. Warren’s Profession,” the third “un-
pleasant ” play, Shaw comes nearer his desired
effect. - Mrs. Warren belongs to a very ancient
woman’s profession, and undertakes to justify her
profession. Her daughter, well brought-up out .of
the profession’s profits, to a certain extent admits
the justification ; but, being a particularly hard-
headed and self-reliant girl, -she decides that she
can do without the mother and the profession and
an ineffectual young man who hankers after her,
and is left at the close of the play immersed in her
own insurance-calculations and tobacco-smoke.
“Mrs. Warren’s Profession” looks like the best
stage-play of the three ; and ’tis pity there doesn’t
seem the remotest chance of seeing it staged.
* * *
What Sfiaw calls the “four pleasant plays”
seem much better suited for acting. “ Candida,’’the
best of all his plays, resolves itself into a struggle
for a keen-brained, fine-emotioned, self-respecting
woman between her husband —a cheery Christian
socialist of the Charles Kingsley type, a little sel-
fish, a little pompous, a little spotted, with cant—-
and a stray poet, physically weak, flawed in char-
acter, but with high ideals, unsatisfied yearnings,
and despairing self-knowledge in strong contrast
to the other man’s smug satisfaction with himself
and liis spiritually-iriean formulas. Husband and
poet bid against each other; and the woman
cleaves to her husband. The play closes with a
dash of mystery—
CandidA. Good-bye. [S/ie takes Marchbanks’ face in
hir hands; and as he divines her intention and bends his
knee, she kisses liis forehead. 'Then he flies out into the
night. She turns to MoreU, holding out her arms to him.]
All. .tunes ! [They embrace:. But they do not know the
se r t in the poet s heart. ]
“ The secret in the poet’s heart.” That Candida
loves him, if unconsciously ; that the best of her
clings to him ; and that if she does not go to him
it is only because her weaker husband, whom she
thinks she loves, appeals to her pity—he needs her
more; or because she is a woman—a, creature
moulded by a thousand centuries of habit and
tradition, who sees the dawn, but whose dawn is
not yet day ?
“Arms and the Man ” is really fitted for the
stage; it is as “brilliant” as its reputation ; and
its American performance by Richard Mansfield
has brought Shaw some thousands of pounds.
“You Never Can Tell ” is even more sparkling:
its effervescence sends one back to Sheridan for
comparisons. If that is playable, and could be
added to the Brough repertoire, and were played
with gusto and gay spirits, Australia would have
reason to be and would be grateful. “The Man
of Destiny ” is an uncommonly clever glimpse of
Napoleon. The dramatic effect is frequently
dominated by jfche literary effect—almost sub-
merged. A speech like the following is a dramatic
excrescence, yet Shaw cannot find it in his heart
to cut it off -
Napoleon : There are three sorts of people in the
world, the low people, the middle people, and the high
people. The low people and the high people are alike in
one thing: they have no scruples, no morality. The low
are beneath morality, the high above it. I am not afraid
of either of them ; for the low are unscrupulous without
knowledge, so that they make an idol of rne ; whilst the
high are unscrupulous without purpose, so that they go
down before my will. Look you : I shall go overall the
mobs and all the courts of Europe as a plough goes over
a field. It is the middle people who are dangerous : they
have both knowledge and purpose. But they, toh, have
their weak point. They are full of scruples—chained
hand and foot by their morality and respectability.
Lady : Then you will beat the English ; for all shop-
keepers are middle people.
Napoleon : No, because the English are a race apart.
No Englishman is too low to have scruples : no English-
man is high enough to be free from their tyranny. But.
every Englishman is born with a certain miraculous
power that makes him master of the world. When he
wants a tiling, he never tells himself that he wants it. He
waits patiently until there comes into his mind, no one
knows how, a burning conviction that it is his moral and
religious duty to conquer those who have got the thing
he wants. Then he becomes, irresistible. Like the
aristocrat, he does what pleases him and grabs what
he covets : like the shopkeeper, he pursues his
purpose with the industry and steadfastness
that come from strong religious conviction and deep
sense of moral responsibility. H ft is never at a
loss for an effective moral attitude. As the great cham-
pion of-freedom and national independence, he bouquet's
and annexes half the world, and calls it Colonization.
When he wants anew market for his adulterated Man-
chester goods, he sends a missionary to teach the natives
the Gospel of Peace. The natives kill the missionary : he
flit;, to arms in defence of--Christianity; fights for it;
conquers for it ; and takes the market as a reward from
heaven. In defence of his island shores, he puts a chap-
lain on board his ship; nails a flag with a cross on it to
his top-gallant mast; and sails to the end of the earth,
sinking, burning and destroying all who dispute the
empire of the seas with him. He boasts that a slave is
free Lite moment his foot touches British soil,; and ho
sells the children of his poor at six years of age to work
under the lash in his factories for sixteen hours a day.
He makes two revolutions, and then declares war on our
one in the name of law and order. There is nothing so
bad or so good that you will not find Englishmen doing’
it; but you will never find an Englishman in the wrong.
He does everything on principle. He fights you on pat-
riotic principles ; he robs you on business principles ; he
enslaves you on imperial principles ; he bullies you on
manly principles; he supports his king on loyal principles
and cuts off his king’s head on republican principles.
His watchword is always Duty ; and he never forgets that
the nation which lets its duty get on the opposite side to
its interest is lost.
» * * *
Bernard Shaw’s plays are his most important
published work : he has been content to stimulate
rather than accomplish. In week-to-week journal-
ism, in socialist essays and lectures, in practical
municipal work, he has spent the energy which
might have permanently signalised his name.
Oliver Holmes has. a story of a rebuke ad-
ministered by a brilliant talker to one who
wondered at his wasting in society the jewels
of phrase and thought which could be
hoarded for fame and a book. The clever man
pointed out o’ window to a water-cart scattering
largesse in dusty paths, and intimated that it
would be a poor world if good things were not
occasionally showered round freely. Shaw has
been a wonderfully refreshing' water-cart in the
arid highway of his time. Asa critic of art,
mu.siq, and drama, he is one of the best-equipped
in England: and though his seeds sown through
twenty years' of newspapers have perished, the
crop is harvested in thousands of illumined heads.
His work is no less real because it is not collected
in a dozen stout volumes of consecutive philosophy.
Now, Shaw says' he finds that' the water is
getting lower in his mental well ; he has to pump
harder, and gets drops instead of. bucketsful.
Life is such; and Shaw is 42. So he is capitu-
lating to Fortune. Max Beerbohm doesjiis articles
in the Saturday Review ; he has married ; he is
even said to have eaten meat. Unluckily, his
present health is not good, and he is advertised
as waiting a second operation on account of his
bicycle-fractured bone, which obstinately refuses
to kuit on artichoke soup. But he bears his
troubles brightly.
O’er the Eastern hills of light,
While the dim world slept,
Dawn the sculptor stepped ;
And the shapeless block of Night-
• Chiselled into form
Morning-lit and warm.
A.H.A.

Various Verses.
At Onawe.
Peaceful it is : the long light glows and glistens
On English grass;
There falls no sound upon the ear that listens
But winds that pass,
Bustling the tussock, and the sea-birds’ calling,
And far below
The murmur of a calm sea rising, falling,
Soft, soft, and slow.
All undisturbed the Pakeha’s herds are creeping
Along the hill;
On lazy tides the Pakeha’s sails are sleeping,
And all is still.
There once in state the Atua had his dwelling,
“Te Nui o Ra,”
And thence mysterious sounds were heard at
night-time swelling,
And borne afar.
Here once the haka sounded, and din of battle
Shook the grey crags,
Triumphant shout and agonised death-rattle
Startled the shags.
And now ? such peace upon this isthmus narrow,
With Maori blood
Once red 1 These heaps of stones—a greenstone
arrow —
An adze, rough, rude.
Gone is the Atua, and his dwelling lonely,
His glory dead,
No sense, no sound ! The weird, wild wailing only
Of gull instead.
Come not the Rangatiras hither roaming
As once of yore,
To dance a ghostly haka in the gloaming,
And feast once more ?
Tenakoe Pakeha! within this fortification
Grows English grass;
Tenakoe! subtler conqueror of a nation
Doomed, doomed to pass.
Doka Wilcox..
“B”: Thebeauty and talentof Mrs. Gilbert
Wilson, as described in The Bulletin (Dec.. 3},
suggests memory of her grandfather, Carandluni.
Writer remembers him in Hobart, where he used
to teach dancing. Nobody would have dreamed
that the little, bewigged man, whose smooth
cheeks often bore a touch of rouge, and who looked
a type of the Neapolitan or Florentine dude—had
ever earned a halter for high treason. So it was,
however. Carandini, the dancing-master, had
been one of the most prominent and dangerous
eonsoirators against King Bomba’s Government.
He married Maria Burgess, a Tasmanian beauty
with a voice. I have seen Carandini caper on the
ul^o-p—not a pleasant sight. On one occasion his
wig fell off, and revealed that he was as bald as a
pumpkin. And he a conspirator, under sentence
of death—so close is tragedy to farce.
Atra Cura.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Black Care behind the horseman sits,
Her hand in his the while,
Not all the joy of jangling bits
Can charm her to a smile ;
She wraps him in her mantle grim,
And when the pace is slow
She leans across and kisses him
With colder lips than snow.
But when the mountain brumbies take
The tracks no man can trust,
Or when the restless scrubbers break
In roaring clouds of dust,
Or when hard-held below the stand
A good horse leads them all,
Care has been known to lose her hand,
Sway in her seat, and fall.
Ride fast! Ride fast! A reckless race,
All ye who carry Care,
And let the plain winds lash her face,
The hill winds lift her hair !
Let greenhide curl and rowel sting,
No speed can be amiss
That makes those arms too numb to cling,
Those lips too cold to kiss !
N.S.W.
WILL H. OGILVIE.
When Eden Groves.
When Eden groves are gulfed in fire abysmal
And one-time smiling lands are black and bare ;
Soon, soon it fades, the fury cataelysmal,
And Nature weaves again her garment fair;
On fierce volcano falls her evening calm —
Her riven islands breathe again in balm.
When spirits proud are rent in storm asunder,
When all our dreams are draped with awful
dread,
When Heav’n hath smit us with her vengeful
thunder
And hurled her flashing bolts about our head,
What healing hath she when her hand is stayed,
What balsam gives she for the wound she made ?
What Voice shall bid, from yonder blue environ,
The broken hero-heart again “ Be whole 1 ”
What hand shall clear the moody brow of Byron,
Or pour the cooling dews on Dante’s soul ?
What potent touch shall ban the prophet s pain
Or quench the deathless fire in Bruno s brain ?
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

HISTORY of
YANKEE DOODLE
'v see:
DIXSON’S TOBACCO HOARDINGS.
ID America.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
General, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight
aroused them. Marching with drum
and fife was an old man, Jeremiah Wyn-
ton, and his son and grandson. The
old father, with silvery flowing locks,
sturdily strode through the field. The
son with bandaged head fifing lustily,
the youth, catching the spirit of his
grandsire, rolled out with him the soul-
inspiring rattle of the drum. Father, son,
and grandson had accompanied the
troops as volunteers, and were ever in
the forefront of the fight. The fierce
light of battle was in their eyes, they
knew no fear, and many a wild cheer
arose, as on that fateful day Yankee
Doodle was heard as never was heard
before or since.
10 Australia.
YANKEE Doodle as a Trade Mark
is admitted by all to have no equal
on this side of the World, and, as
a Tobacco, Dixson and Sons determined
that it should enjoy an equal reputation,
consequently the resources of their great
establishment, and patient trials and ex-
periments (involving several visits to
America in search of suitable leaf with
which to produce the tobacco sought
after) resulted in a Dark and Bright
Tobacco, which to-day CHALLENGES
THE WHOLE WORLD IN COMPE-
TITION, and that it is appreciated may
be gathered from the fact that the sales
for New South Wales alone now reach
Tons per Day.
The objection of many smokers to
Dark Tobacco is its strength. Dixson’s
have overcome this, and Dark Yankee
Doodle, while possessing a delightful
flavor, is not strong. Then again, Light
or Bright Tobacco, although mild, burns
the tongue. Bright Yankee Doodle is
equally mild, richer than other tobaccos
in aroma, and DOES NOT PARCH
THE TONGUE. Every smoker should
TRY FOR HIMSELF.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 24, 1898.
The New Federal Situation.
Now and then, in a small, accidental way,
the Nominee Council of New South Wales
gets an immortal truth off its chest. It con-
tributed one during the recent debate on the
Federal resolutions, the particular contri-
butor being H. C. Dangar. That gentleman
thus defined the present Federal situation :
I also thank the Attorney-General, the hon. and learned
member Dr. MacLaurin, and the patriots who put us in
our present position, that we are not in the same position
as we were in before in consequence of the blunder made
in the Federal Enabling Act. Whatever Constitution
may be recommended to ms by the impending conference
will be subject to the approval of the Parliament of this
colony. We are to that extent in a better position than
we were formerly, for according to the dreadful Enabling
Act we were to be deprived of the power of saying either
yes or no to the constitution. It would not have been
submitted to US, but we should have had to swallow it
whether we liked it or not.
Which is a very complete and carefully
boiled-down statement of the present Federal
situation, as distinguished from the one which
perished a few months ago.
Under the original Federal Enabling Act,
the people elected the delegates who con-
structed the Bill, and the power of saying
yes or no was left to the people. The
common, beastly nation was the last court of
appeal, and its destiny was in its own hands,
barring certain limitations. The community’s
servants in Parliament intimated to their
alleged master that the majority could decide
provided it mustered 50,000 votes—if there
were 49,999 votes for Federation and one
against it the minority was to win. But,
barring that small trifle, the people practi-
cally held the power of final decision. Then
the community’s servants in Parliament fur-
ther disfranchised their alleged master by de-
creeing that not only must there be a majority
before Federation could be carried, but it must
consist of at least 80,000 votes. If there
were 79,999 votes for Federation and only
one against it the one was to carry the day.
Still, even with this further restriction, the
people had still some direct voice in the
matter. But when they accepted the Feder-
ation Bill by a majority of over 5300, and
Parliament and the Reid Government
jumped on their decision with all its feet,
and Attorney-General Jack Want delivered
his famous oration in which Tie told the
people how the accepted measure had been
rejected, that aspect of the situation came to
an end. The disfranchised nation that had
just had its ukase thrown back at its head
by its own servants, went meekly home to
bed, and a new procession of things started.
* * * *
In the first place, it is a noteworthy cir-
cumstance that there is now no N.S.W.
Federal Enabling Act existing. It was care-
fully provided by law that if the enormous
number of 80,000 affirmative votes was not
forthcoming, “no further action shall be
taken pursuant to this Act.” The Enabling
Act simply dropped out of existence
on the spot. The right of the people
to elect their own Federal constitution-
makers, or to have any direct voice in the
acceptance or rejection of the next Constitu-
tion, went out of existence also, and the law
authorising the creation of a Commonwealth
disappeared from the statute-book. And
the New South Wales Council was so very
sorry indeed that it ever gave the people the
right to decide anything concerning the Con-
stitution that it isn’t at all likely to concede
any such right again. Some other Legisla-
tive Councils feel much the same way.
■ * • ■ •
When the next Federal Constitution comes
along the people will be allowed to vote
upon it if both Houses of New South Wales
Parliament pass a law to that effect, and the
Council isn’t at all likely to pass any such
law. And if the people-are allowed to vote
at all they wfill be allowed to vote, as they
were last time, in the way, and under such
limitations, as their servants in Parliament
may allow, but in no other way whatsoever.
If the next Bill is sufficiently Tory to please
the Upper House, it may let the people have
a voice in the matter and enact that a simple
majority will suffice to carry it. If it is too
democratic to please that institution it will
probably enact (supposing it lets the people
have any vote at all) that the affirmative
votes must reach an impossible minimum
(perhaps 100,000 or 150,000) before the
measure shall be accepted. If the people
are allowed a vote in the matter, a,nd
they accept the next sample Constitution
despite all Parliamentary obstacles, even
that doesn’t necessarily bring union any
nearer. This time Parliament keeps the
power of acceptance or rejection absolutely
in its own hands, and Parliament consists of
two Houses, only one of which is respon-
sible to the people. Therefore the next
Constitution won’t be submitted to the
people at all, and they won’t have any voice
whatever in the matter —unless the Council
consents. If they have a voice they will
only have as much as the Council likes to
give them. They will only be allowed to
vote on the Bill which the Council likes to
present to them—it may alter the new Con-
stitution into any shape it darned w'ell
pleases before it is submitted to the people
at all. And if they do get a vote and do
accept the Constitution offered them it seems
that after that they won’t get it unless the
Council pleases.
* * * •
This is no exaggerated statement of the
case. Where the assent of two Houses is
required before anything can be done the
one which doesn’t care a straw' whether any-
thing is done or not is master of the situa-
tion. The New South Wales Assembly
wants Federation a little—a very little ; the
Upper House is indifferent, or worse. If
the Assembly tries to pass a law enacting
that the new Bill shall be submitted to the
people, the Council can simply say, “Submit
the Bill with our amendments or not at all.”
If it wants to load the dice it can say, “ Sub-
mit the Bill with a 150,000 minimum, or let
it drop right here.” It can also reject the
Bill bodily and unconditionally ; under the
present position it must be accepted by both
Houses before anything can be done, and in
that case one Chamber is as much boss of the
situation as another. The fact that the
Council’ has cast out of its amended resolu-
tions with contumely every mention of the
Referendum makes it eminently probable
that the idea of having any popular vote on
the subject will meet with its enthusiastic
disapproval.
« * * »
It is a notable circumstance that the alleged
democratic party in the Assembly has never
once asked for a new Federal Enabling Bill
to provide definitely for giving the people a
voice in the acceptance or rejection of the
next draft Constitution. Not a single voice
has been raised to demand from the Reid-
Want Government a legal provision that the
next scheme shall be submitted to the
people ; that it shall be fairly submitted this
time, and that the Referendum shall not be
again faked with a minimum provision ; that
the Bill shall be submitted -as it comes from
the Conference, and not after being mangled
out of all democratic shape by the Legisla-
tive Council ; or that the popular vote shall
be decisive—in other words, that the national
decision shall not be thrown overboard after-
wards, at the caprice of the nominee bigots of
J. Want’s Upper House. And nobody has
even seriously demanded that the Council
shall be uromptly reformed so that the House
which has the last word in the matter of
Federation shall be responsible to the people
and reasonably in touch with the popular
sentiment. Even the crowd which hollers so
loudly for the Referendum in theory has
kept absolute silence.
• • • •
Last time the people’s representatives
drafted the Federal Constitution, and the
people accepted or rejected it. There was
one judge and one final court of appeal ;
and the arrangement was plain and work-
able—until the brutish ' treachery of the
Reid Government in assenting to the propo-
sal that the majority should not decide un-
less it was an anti-Federal majority, upset
everything. This time the people who draft
the Constitution will elect themselves. The
Assembly demands one set of amendments
and the Council demands a different set.
That part of the Reid Government which is
in the Assembly says it wants the Referen-
dum included in the Bill, and that part (J.
Want) which is in the Council says it doesn’t.
The other provinces can’t agree to both sets
of amendments, for they are contradictory,
and each House can reject the Bill if Its own
amendments are not included. There is no
one final court of appeal—the people —as
there was on June 3 ; the undemocratic Reid
Government has so fooled things that the
last decision is left to two hostile judges with
no referee or bottle-holder to arbitrate be-
tween them. And when it is considered that
all this chaos arose mainly through the gross
treachery of Reid and some of his colleagues
in insisting on the insertion of the fatal and
undemocratic three-fifths majority idea in
the original Constitution ; and rejecting the
Referendum idea proposed by Kingston ;
and afterwards appealing to the people of
New South Wales to throw out the Bill
because of the undemocratic clauses they
had done their best to put in it ; and can-
celling the popular acceptance of the Federal
Constitution on June 3 —considering aH
these things, the Reid Ministry deserves to
live in Australian history as a thing of shame
and disgrace and execration for all time.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Turner on the Federal Situation.
The straightforward explanation of his Federal
policy which the Victorian Premier gave the
other day was in strong contrast to the dead
silence of the New South Wales Govern-
ment on the same subject. Up to time of
waiting no one had succeeded in extract-
ing any definite information from the Reid-
Want Ministry as to whether it intends
submitting the new Bill (if there is one) to the
people, and, if so, on what basis, whether with
a possible or impossible minimum, or with no
minimum at all, or whether the people’s elected
delegates will be consulted in any way, or
whether Parliament will simply decide the
matter on its own responsibility, or what will
happen. George Turner was clear and definite
on all these points. He is prepared to meet the
Premiers of the other provinces in conference as
soon as the other Premiers are ready, and con-
sider the proposed amendments of the N.S.W.
Parliament. At the same time, he claims for
himself and for S.A., Westralia, and Tasmania
the same right to submit amendments as
is claimed by N.S. Wales, and the same
right to demand concessions. N.S. Wales,
Victoria, S.A., and Tasmania all gave a
majority for the old Bill as it stood, and
Turner very reasonably maintains that no one
Government has any more right to demand
special concessions than another. He proposes
to act only with the advice of the other nine
popularly elected Victorian delegates, so that
the Victorian people will have the same voice
in framing the new Bill as they had with regard
to the old one. If the Conference agrees upon
a new Bill it is to be submitted to Parliament
as a matter of necessity, and the Legislature is
to be asked to authorise its further submission
to a popular vote, as was done with the original
draft Constitution.. But this time, Turner
says with emphasis, he will only move after
N.S.W. has passed its Referendum Act. Vic-
toria is not going to spend a large sum on
holding another national vote unless it is assured
that N.S.W. will not again go back on the
agreement, and insert a second impossible mini-
mum at the last moment. If there is again a
practically impossible minimum in the N.S.W.
Act, the Victorian Premier intends to wait
until the people of N.S.W. have actually
accepted the new scheme before he goes to the
cost of another Referendum. Last time pro-
ceedings started with an Enabling Act which
provided that the Draft Constitution should be
sent direct to the people whether Parliament
approved of it or not. This time there is
nothing of the sort, and it will be necessary to
submit the new Bill to all the Parliaments and
ask them to accept it and to authorise its
reference to the people. If any of the Parlia-
ments object, the only process, as Turner
explains, is to hold another Conference
of Premiers, and perhaps a dozen more Con-
ferences, and try to arrive at some compromise
on the points in dispute. Turner’s own atti-
tude is straight and honest enough, but his ex-
position of the case shows the hopelessness of
the situation which the mean treachery of the
Reid Govt, has brought about. The collapse of
tlieEnabling Act through thedefection of N.S.W.
has dropped the matter back into the hands of
Parliament, and, unless some Constitution can
be devised which will suit equally the demo-
cratic Assembly of S.A. and the bigoted House
of Landlords in Victoria, there is nothing in
view save deadlock. And, on top of every-
thing else, by reason of the delays and evasions
and shufflings that have filled the long interim,
the enthusiasm of last June is as dead and flat
now as the ditch where the late lamented cat
takes its rest, awaiting the day of resurrection.
Concessions in Return for Support.
The new Industrial Disputes Arbitration Bill,
introduced by N.S.W. Premier Reid, is merely
Dibbs’s old futile law, with an unimportant
difference. Dibbs’s law provided a court of
arbitration which could deal with industrial
disputes—if both employers and employes were
agreeable. They hardly ever were both agree-
able at the same time, and Dibbs’s court of con-
ciliation is now a lone skeleton perished in the
wilderness. Reid provides that his court may
deal with a dispute on the application of only
one party, and give a decision —and neither
party need take the least notice of the decision
if it doesn’t want to. To all intents and pur-
poses the decision might as well be given by
the washerwoman. Reid’s conciliation scheme
is pure sham. His device has already had a
long trial in Maoriland, and the result of ,that
trial is that a court whose verdict needn’t be
attended to by any body whodoesn’t feel that way
isn’t very much more useful than a law which pro-
vides for the arrest of criminals if they don’t ob-
ject. So Maoriland is taking the logical course of
giving the Conciliation Court power to enforce
its awards. And N.S. VV., under Reid’s guid-
ance, is leaving things practically as they are.
The present Cornstalk statute provides that
the disputing parties needn’t arbitrate unless
they are both willing. The proposed new law
is that they needn’t take any notice of the
award of the Arbitration Court unless they
are both willing—which distinction shows the
subtlety of the legal intelligence.
North. Queensland’s Grievance.
Twenty-one North and Central Queensland
members have sent a memorial to the local
Excellency, praying him to be so kind as to
pray the British Government to grant them
Separation. And his Excellency has under-
taken to pray as requested—in a purely formal
fashion. For all practical purposes these 21
members might as well have saved themselves a
grievous waste of good ink. According to the
unlucky and iniquitous Colonial Boundaries
Act no authority whatever has power to sub-
divide any province, except with the con-
sent of the local Legislature, and the Queens-
land Legislature isn’t taking any. Neither is
any other Legislature. The only party that
ever wants Separation is the minority, which
reckons that its interests are being sacrificed to
those of the majority in and around the capital.
If that minority was a majority, or if it com-
manded a majority in Parliament, it wouldn’t
want Separation, for then its interests wouldn’t
be sacrificed —it would take all manner of care of
that. And under the Colonial Boundaries Act it
can’t get Separation till it becomes a political
majority and doesn’t want it. Therefore the
only prayer that is worth praying at present is
that the Imperial Government will kindly see
its way to repeal that very foolish piece of
legislation. As things are, if Central Queens-
land lands are being sold wholesale and the
money spent in the South, and it and the
North are compelled to pay extravagant rail-
way freights to cover the loss incurred by
giving cheap freights to the South, and they
don’t get their fair share of the loan swindle,
the remedy, under the present system, is to
wait till they own the biggest share of the
population and the Parliamentary representa-
tion, and then they can settle the Separation
matter for themselves. But then Separation
will probably be the very last thing that the
proposed new province will want. What it
will desire then will be to shift the capital of
all Queensland to Townsville, or Cairns, or
somewhere thereabouts; and to have cheap
freights itself and raise the Southern freights
to cover the loss ; and to spend all the loan
money in the North while the South pays its
share of the interest. In short, if that time
ever comes, the North will be Pharaoh
and the South will be Pharaoh’s Israelite, and
Pharaoh won’t see the philosophy of letting his
Israelite go by any means. At present, of
course, things are reversed, and North Queens-
land is the Israelite. The Colonial Boundaries
Act is a cul de sac. Its chief result is that,
when any district has been hitched on without
its consent to a province to which it doesn’t
naturally belong, and with which it has no
interests in common, it must simply remain
there, and let the animosities accumulate till it
finds its own way out. It can’t settle the
matter by insurrection, because then the British
Government is bound to interfere in the in-
terests of injustice and the majority. In the
geographical order of things, the Barrier and
western and central Riverina don’t naturally
go with Sydney, nor Eastern Gippsland and the
two or three far western Vic. counties with
Melbourne, nor the Northern Territory with
Adelaide, nor Northern Westralia with Perth,
nor North Queensland with Brisbane—in fact,
all the boundaries are drawn practically any-
how. And one of Premier Reid’s Federal
amendments is that this unprofitable and curse-
ful state of things shall be maintained in-
definitely.

Rofe and Suffield.
The public of N.S.W. will, or ought to be,
much interested in hearing that ex-attorney
Rofe’s case is being, or has just been, inquired
into. Roee was convicted, in 1895, of con-
spiracy in connection with the infamous
Butler divorce-case. The principal in that
case, Butler, a gentleman whose character is
best deposited in the ash-lieap, wanted to get
rid of his wife, of whom he had grown weary,
and having no case against her he manufactured
one with such elaborate perjury that the
divorce was granted, and Mrs. Butler had
severe remarks made about her character by
the all-seeing and infallible Judge YVindeyer.
Mrs. Butler’s brother, who had given evidence
on her behalf, got three years for perjury.
Later on, however, solely through the persist-
ent exertions of Mrs. Butler, the case was un-
ravelled, and the law decided that it was built
solely on premeditated Ananias. It decided
this point so strongly that Butler and certain
of his accomplices were sent to a well-earned
gaol. Further, the law was (rightly or wrongly)
so definitely convinced that Rofe, solici-
tor in the case, conspired to help Butler
that it sentenced him -to five years in the same
valuable public institution. Nobody seemed to
notice any conspicuous doubt in Rofe’s case,
and his legal representative, Heydon, asked for
mercy partly on the ground that Rofe had been
“led astray,” which looked like a tolerably
definite throwing up of the sponge, and partly
on the ground that he belonged to a “good


family,” and partly on the ground that
Attorney-General Want would be glad if he
got off. Thereupon Judge Backhouse a
subordinate Judge dependent for promotion
upon the good opinion of the Ministry—released
Rofe under the First Offenders’ Act, and he
didn’t serve even a single day of his purely
nominal sentence, though his fellow-prisoners
ajl went to gaol in the usual way. And now
Rofe, the influential ex-solicitor, is allowed
to have a new trial (that is what an inquiry
practically amounts to), while the uninfluential
.Suffield, convicted on no evidence worth call-
ing such, and forced to serve out his sentence
to the bitter end has always been per-
sistently denied one. Rofe gets his new trial
under the same clause in the Act which the
N.S.W. Government emphatically declined to
let Suffield avail himself of. The clause in
question states that any convicted person may
get a re-hearing “ whenever any doubt or ques-
tion arises as to his guilt,” but the N.S.W,
Government persistently broke this law in
Suffield’s case by refusing him a rehearing,
no matter how many doubts existed as to his
guilt, unless he produced new evidence.
Whether the same ruling was applied in Rofe’s
case is a mystery, for Rofe got his inquiry
with no publicity or conspicuous fuss ; and the
reason why he got it is unknown to the public;
and hardly anyone knew that lie had got it;
and how it progressed has been a strict
secret up to time of writing, no evi-
dence whatever having been allowed to
appear in print. Also, it isn’t known whether
the Reid-Want Government (Attorney-General
Want being the gentleman who was quoted in
court as a person who would be very glad if
the Judge could see his way clear to deal with
Rofe under the First Offenders’ Act) was con-
nected in any way with Rofe getting his new
trial ; it was at least very much connected
with Suffield not getting his. In the absence
of all published evidence there is, of course,
nothing to show that Rofe may not have been
wrongly convicted. But there seems abundant
evidence to show that a possibly wrongly-con-
victed solicitor finds it very easy to get his case
re-opened in N.S.W. while a mere laborer in
the same circumstances, even if his case is a lot
better than the solicitor’s, doesn’t.

More of the 48 Rich Landlords.
By 23 votes to 17 Victoria’s precious Leg. Coun-
cil has rejected the One Man One Vote Bill, and
this for the third time. On the two previous
occasions the Bill was frankly and honestly
thrown out in much the same style as a dis-
orderly person is removed from a pub. The
Assembly’s third attempt to abolish plural
voting was defeated by strategy. In the first
place, Sargood, the uncrowned king of the
Council, fooled with the Bill from week t o week,
and, when his fooling finally collapsed under
the weight of its own absurdity, Henry
Wrixon came along with a proposal which pro-
fessed a desire to put an end to plural voting,
but was quite certain not to do it. The Coun-
cil, at Wrixon’s suggestion, “approved” of
the one - man - one - vote principle (as re-
gards elections for the Assembly only), but
sent the Bill back to Premier Turner with an
impossible amendment attached. The amend-
ment would allow 85 members of the Assembly
to be elected on the 0. M.O.V. system pure and
simple, with the proviso that the other 10
members, representing the districts where the
Labor-party has been all - powerful of late,
should be chosen on the discredited “Hare
system ” of proportional voting. The Wrixon
proposal was to merge the eight radical consti-
tuencies into one with the hope that the pro-
portional representation system would wipe
the Labor-party right out of the Assembly,
and put the Tory minority on top. And
the 48 Rich Landlords accepted this proposal
because they had just enough brains to
understand that the Lower House would re-
fuse to entertain it. As a matter of fact,
the Govt, didn’t ask the Lower House to enter-
tain it. The idea of changing the Parlia-
mentary constitution in this casual way, at the
suggestion of Fatman, and without reference
to public opinion, was not worth discussing.
The One-man-one-vote was promptly returned
to the Council in its original form. Last week
the landlords had to say what they would do
in the matter, and the majority coolly decided
to insist upon Wrixon’s impossible amend-
ment, notwithstanding that Wrixon himself
voted in favor of its withdrawal, after Sargood
had asked him, in a pained tone of voice,
whether he would not prefer to “ leave things
as they are.” Wrixon was one of the 17 who
recommended cutting his fatal amendment off
the bill, whilst Sargood and the others, who
had all along been anxious to leave plural
voting where it is, decided to leave Wrixon’s
amendment where it is, which amounts to the
same thing. There are more ways than one
of killing a Democratic measure.
Slavery in New South Wales.
It is about time that agreements under which
Asiatics come to N.S. Wales to work were
overhauled. The other day, in the Far North-
West (N.S.W.), two Afghans, who had been
working for a reputedly wealthy carrying co.,
waited till their camel-train had got well into
the desert, and then silently stole away.
They were arrested after doing a “perish”;
but then the beak, to the apparent consterna-
tion of the “ boss,” insisted on seeing the agree-
ment under which it was alleged that the ac-
cused had bound themselves for six years, at
£24 a-j'ear. The “ boss ” swore he hadn’t gone
outside the bond; the accused swore that,
though they didn’t know what was in the bond
(they had put their marks to something), they
had certainly been beaten and cuffed and half-
starved, living mostly on flour and tea, and
rarely seeing meat. Further, one of them
showed scars on his body, and stated that
that was where a pannikin of boiling water
had been thrown at him. Adjournments
were granted for the production of the agree-
ment, which was promised ; but it didn’t put
in an appearance, and at length the poor
wretches, who could not speak a word of Eng-
lish, were discharged. (The Court here ad-
journs for two hours to admire the cheek of a
person who prosecutes for breach of agreement
and apparently expects to get a conviction on
his own bare word that there is an agreement
and his own nude assertion as to the contents
thereof). Further, apropos of Afghans and
their kind : Some time ago a couple of aliens
belonging to a camel train in the same district
deserted. They were passing through country
strewn with stones, and made away as soon as
ever they had prevailed on the “ boss ” to pro-
vide them with a pair of boots apiece to bolt
in—their own were worn clean through ! After-
wards a third Afghan sought a pair of boots,
too. He had to have them in order to do his
work ; but on camping every evening they had
to be handed over to the boss till morning, as a
guarantee of good faith. All this in a free coun-
try ! The long drought in most of the country
back from the Paroo and the Darling has given
the camel-owning go’s a big lift; they have been
getting the cream of the carrying biz, owing to
their comparative swiftness and the certainty
of delivery whether tanks fail suddenly along
the route or not. They don’t do much wool-
carrying, though; it rarely pays to send any
but scoured fleeces that way. And as all the
camels have to be loaded each morning and un-
loaded every night, some idea of the labor
imposed on the poor dusty devils who
tramp alongside all day (only the “ boss,” as a
rule, rides) may be formed. The “ boss ” on
the dry tracks spends a good deal of time
reconnoitring; and for “sneaking” drinks
from private tanks he often beats the craftiest
Saltbush Bill “ Banjo” ever pictured.

The Sugar Members’ Stampede.
N.S.W. sugar-members’ vote on the famous
want-of-confidence motion, when Reid dished
Barton with the help of Barton’s own men, is
still the subject of frenzied disputation. The
Bulletin has culled from the tangle of gratui-
tous information on the above subject what ap-
pear to be the facts of the case, to wit—
Under the Rf.id tariff of diminishing' duties the sugar
industry was bleeding to death. The sugar representa-
tives, seeing no probability of a change of Government
before the industry collapsed, came to the throne of
Tiberius. Reid promised to do “ something.” The
sugar members, knowing he must make some sacrifice of
principle if his “something” was to be effective, pro-
mised to see him through the difficulty. How far this
promise really went it is now impossible to say. Some of
the sugar members construed it into the support of the
budget, of which sugar was the main item. Others, that
the obligation was simply to take what their electorates
wanted and to obstruct him in the residue. Reid and
his followers made it clear that any trifling meant the
abandonment of the sugar portion of his budget. Some
of the sugar members, believing they were bound by their
promises to aid him, and that it was dangerous to help
themselves and leave him in the lurch, determined to see
the budget through, especially as it did not raise
a dispute on the fiscal policy, unless indeed the
Government followers quarrelled among themselves. The
Opposition called a meeting—business not stated. Perry
approved of a vote of censure, and pledged himself to
vote for it. M'Farlane explained that his duty to his
constituents and his pledged word must force him to vote
against it. Pyers followed in a similar strain. Meagher
always has voted for Reid. Ewing was not present, but
was known to be adverse to any motion that might en-
danger the sugar industry. It should have been apparent
that any vote of censure must fail, and fail dismally.
Barton was not well, and could not bring his usual
discernment to bear upon the question. When the vote
was taken, M’Farlane, Pyers, Ewing and Meagher stood
their ground and voted as they had stated. Perry, after
helping to lead Barton into the trap, voted against the
motion to which he had given his adherence. See washed
his hands of the whole transaction.

The Small Girl’s Romance.
It seems to be getting quite fashionable for any
lone, unprotected female, from the age of 12
away up to the seventies and eighties, who may
have strayed from her mother or her home, or
been lost, or stayed out late, or got herself
viewed with suspicion in any way, to straight-
way charge the first man convenient with hav-
ing assaulted her. The latest case occurred in
Fitzroy (Melb.). On Dec. 6 Mary Ann Ehlers,
an innocent damsel of 12, told a very interest-
ing and circumstantial story —on oath —at the
local court. She left her mother’s house at
Eltham, some dozen or so miles away, on the
previous Wednesday, and after getting a lift in
a cart, and wandering round promiscuously,
and attending an alleged circus, she found her-
self late at night in Gertrude-street. Here she
was met by a plain-clothes constable, who de-
manded the why and the wherefore of this
thusness of the little lost lamb. Her answers
not being satisfactory, and she having appar-
ently no home, the constable proceeded to
take her to the local watch-house —about
300 yards away —as a neglected child. So
far, the story was quite plausible and
probable. Then she proceeded to charge the
constable with having, during the course of
this perambulation of 300 yards of a narrow,
closely-built, thickly-populated street, crimin-
ally assaulted her. A strict cross-examination
considerably damaged this pretty tale, and
medical evidence was given showing the
absurdity of the whole story, whereupon the
constable was discharged without being asked
for any defence. Apparently it’s going to be a
very risky thing for any man to so. much as
look at the unprotected female. And, as usual,
no attempt was made to run in the small per-
jurer. The really amazing feature of Austra-
lian law is that any female can charge any man
with a most serious offence of one particular
kind, and put him to great expense in defend-
ing himself, and bring him very close indeed to
the gallows ; and then when she is proved to
be a gross perjurer can walk away without any
one thinking of detaining her. In fact, the per-
jury law is entirely abrogated for the benefit of
the dirty-minded female who wants to ruin a
man for the mere fun of the thing. And it is
by reason of this amazing impunity that the
dirty-minded female is so much in evidence in
Australia.
Prisoners’ Families.
When on the plea of the public good the State
deprives a man of a piece of land or a house,
compensation is paid him—the more liberally,
it would appear, in proportion as he has other
wealth. When, allegedly for the public good,
it deprives a wife and children of their bread-
winner, all the consideration they receive is the
cynical remark of a judge or magistrate to the
distressed husband and father, “ You should
have thought of them before committing your
offence.” Two incidents have lately happened
that should set people thinking hard on the
iniquity of this state of things. The first is
the appeal of the man convicted of something
up-country in N.S.W. a little while back, to be
punished with flogging instead of imprisonment,
in order that his wife and children might not be
left destitute —an appeal that could not be
acceded to. The public was treated to the
cruelly grotesque spectacle of a prisoner plead-
ing to be allowed literally to take his punish-
ment upon his own shoulders, and J ustice
insisting, instead, upon caring for him and sen-
tencing his innocent family to starvation. On
the heels of that case follows the tragic one of
Mrs. Winspear, who committed suicide at
Sydney, a few days ago, because her husband
had been sentenced to a term of imprisonment,
and she was left without the means of support-
ing her five children. And this woman has
been as truly the victim of judicial murder as
anyone who has been wrongfully choked to
death under the gallows; because her fatal
despair was visited upon her in penalty for
another person’s offence against the law. There
is occasion—is there not? —for us to regard the
Chinese as barbarous on account of their prac-
tice of punishing the parents of an offender ?
There is at least a chance that the parents
may be to some extent responsible for the bad
conduct of their progeny ! No doubt one
objection would be raised to any proposal to
provide for the dependents of a man whom the
State, for its own purposes, puts out of a posi-
tion to provide for them —namely, that it would
place temptation in the way of wicked wives,
and perhaps of impecunious husbands and
fathers ; but the answer is obvious. Life-
insurance has tempted unprincipled women to
procure their husband’s deaths, and despairing
men to quit the world prematurely, in the one
case for selfish gain, and in the other to bring
sustenance to those dear to them ; but nobody
proposes that life insurance should be abolished
on that account. Here the matter stands:
the public are responsible for the social system
under which a wife and children are made
dependent on the individual fortunes of one
man ; the public, also, are responsible for taking
that one man away, in what they or their
accredited representatives deem the public in-
terest ; and the public duty is clear.
The Elusive Coffin-Ship.
Premier Reid’s N.S.W. Navigation Act
Amendment Bill is a fine specimen of the lazy,
brainless legislation to which his Ministry has
habituated the public. The N.S.W. navigation
law yells for reform and consolidation ; Reid
adds a large number of ill-fitting patches which
leave the abuses almost as bad as they were.
The drafting of the monster is enough to make
Griffith shriek with agony. And, though
a number of useful regulations respecting
boats and appliances are tacked on from the
British Act, "the well-insured coffin-ship, for all
that is said to the contrary, may sail from New-
castle to Eternity as gladly as ever. If a com-
plaint is made to the newly-established Court
of Marine Enquiry—which consists of apy
D.C. Judge with the assistance of experts—an
unseaworthy vessel may be stopped ; but it is
no more any responsible official’s business to
stop rotten ships without complaint than it is
under the present Marine Board system. The
sailors, as a rule, don’t complain because they
don’t know that there is much wrong with
the ship till they get to sea and the bottom
of the vessel begins to drop out, and no one else
complains because no one else knows and it is
no one else’s business. The Marine Board in-
spectors allege that they have no power to stop
any coffin-ship, however dreadful, from going
to sea. And the new Superintendent of Navi-
gation, who is to replace the Marine Board
president, has in this particular matter no more
definite power than the old Marine Board
official. The Bulletin therefore moves this
new clause in committee :
It shall be the duty of the Superintendent of the De-
pari ment of Navigation, and of any of his delegates or
representatives, to see that no vessel, whether ship or
steamer or other, and whether carrying passengers or
not, leaves any port in N.S.W. in an unseaworthy condi-
tion ; and it shall be the duty of such Superintendent,
either personally or by his qualified representatives, to
examine every vessel leaving any port in N.S.W., and to
certify in writing that such vessel is, to the best of his
knowledge and belief, seaworthy in respect of the parti-
cular voyage then to be undertaken ; and such certificate
shall be immediately forwarded to the Colonial Treasurer,
and no vessel shall receive a clearance from any port in
N.S.W. before her captain has obtained from the Superin-
tendent of Navigation a duplicate of such certificate in
writing.
If Premier Reid doesn’t like the wording of
that amendment, he can word it as he pleases,
so long as he secures the object: the placing
upon a definite official of the definite responsi-
bility of preventing unseaworthy ships from going
to sea. That wasn’t in the old Acts ; it isn’t in
the new Bill; and it ought to be. Apart from
these little matters, Premier Reid’s uew Bill
appears to be a considerable fake. The Marine
Board is nominally abolished ; but a superin-
tendent and nine assessors pop up in its stead ;
and no doubt the old barnacles will get the new
offices and perpetuate the bad old traditions.
A promising series of complications seem to lie
in the redistribution of authority between the
Colonial Treasurer, the Court of Marine Euquiry,
and the superintendent and assessors ; but the
whole Bill is such a muddle that no one can say
exactly what will happen. The Bulletin
pleads only for certainty that one thing will
not happen—that coffin-ships won’t go to sea.

The Whitewashing of Potato Young.
Judge Owen, the gentleman appointed by the
Government of Reid and Potato Young to
decide whether Potato Young did or did not
endeavor to procure the election of Pumpkin
Smith by inferentially promising that the con-
stituency would get more public works if it
elected Pumpkin Smith than if it elected Bar-
ton, has presented his report:
I accept Mr. Young’s denial of having used the ex-
pressions attributed to him, as his denial is supported by
the evidence of all his witnesses. I also accept his dis-
claimer of any intention to induce the electors to believe
that he would favor Mr. Smith more than Mr. Barton In
dealing with the requirements of the electorate.
Nevertheless, I consider that the expressions which he
admits having used were such as might reasonably have
the effect of inducing such a belief in the minds of the
electors. Although his remarks were directed to the
position of Mr. Clarkf., yet it was in connection with the
alleged arrangement that if Mr. Barton were elected Mr.
Clarkb was to look after the local requirements of the
electorate, and, therefore, in gaying that he would eon-
sider Mr. Clarke as a mere stranger, and that it would
give him greater pleasure to work with the member of
the adjoining constituency, with whom he had been so
friendly, as he had been with Mr. Smith, and that if he
continued to be Secretary for Public Works he would be
better pleased to receive communications from the con-
stituency through Mr. Smith, it might not unreasonably
have been supposed that that referred to Mr. Barton'
himself, or, at any rate, to the position of the constitu-
ency if Mr. Barton were elected and Mr. Clarke were to
look after the local requirements.
This inference is not mere conjecture, for I am satisfied
that all the witnesses called hy me in the first instance so
understood the language used by Mr. Yodng.
I am not called on to express, nor do I venture to ex-
press, any opinion as to whether Mr. Young, while hold-
ing office as Secretary for Public Works, was within his
constitutional right in taking an active part in supporting
the candidature of Mr. Sydney Smith ; but, assuming that
he was within his right, I consider that he was guilty of a
grave indiscretion, in view of his official position, in ex-
pressing himself in the way in which, by his own admis-
sion, he did.
Mr. Young, as member for an adjoining electorate,
must have known that in such a constituency the choice
of a candidate might be determined not merely by his
views as to Federation or fiscal questions, but also by the
probability of success in the Department of Public Works
of his representations as to local wants. It was, there-
fore, the more incumbent on Mr. Young, as Secretary for
Public Works, when urging the claims of Mr. Sydney
Smith, to avoid any expressions that might lead (however
unintentionally on his part) to the inference that he would
favor the representations of Mr. Sydney Smith more than
those of Mr. Barton or of Mr. Clarke, if deputed by Mr.
Barton and the constituency to make those representa-
tions.
Upon a careful review of all the evidence, I find that
the Honorable James Henry Young, whilst in the elector-
ate of the Hastings and Macleay during the recent elec-
tion, did not abuse the powers of his office, or corruptly
endeavor to influence the electors to record their votes in
favor of Mr. Sydney Smith.
In other words, the finding of Potato Young’s
own commission of inquiry is that Potato
Young used language which he shouldn’t have
used, and that he did create the impression
that if the constituency elected Barton instead
of Pumpkin Smith it would be punished by
the loss of public works, and that the con-
stitutional aspect of his conduct is best not
mentioned. But he says he had no corrupt
motive, and it is impossible to prove what any
man’s motive is, so he gets the benefit of the
doubt and is acquitted of wilful corruption,
and discharged with a scorching reprimand. It
is claimed on behalf of Potato Young that his
political character has been wholly cleared by
the very unpleasant language which Judge
Owen used about him. And it is to be remem-
bered that the Barton party sent nobody along
to prosecute or to call witnesses against Young,
while Young had a legal representative to
conduct his defence and bring in evidence from
all parts. If Young comes off so badly as this
when the other side didn’t push its case against
him, what kind of remarks might have been
made under other circumstances.

In Melbourne, the other day, a clothing manu-
facturer was heavily fined for paying an out-
side worker 7s. per dozen for making vests, the
minimum wage fixed by law being 11s. 6£d.
According to a recent statement made in the
Chamber of Manufactures the price for making
trousers in Sydney goes as low as 4s. per
dozen pairs—and there is no minimum wage
law in N.S.W. to give redress. It is the old,
old trouble. You can’t pay a living wage of
for making an article without it
being a little dearer than if a wretched
human skeleton constructed it for 4d. And
under Freetrade the difference might re-
sult in the imported article, made by the cheap
human skeleton in some other country, cutting
out the local article. Cheapness, unless it is
caused by improved machinery or improved
methods, must be gained at the expense of
somebody. In the old days of 3d. ’bus-fares in
Sydney, the drivers worked long hours for low
wages, and two moderately-thin horses hauled a
vehicle that usually carried about 14. Now there
are 2d. fares. The drivers work longer hours
for less wages. Seats have been put on top of all
the ’buses, and two spectral horses drag about
24 passengers. These remarks now conclude
with three pheers for Freetrade, and fourpenny
pants, and skeleton horses, and 2d. ’bus-fares,
and cheapness generally.

Somebody, who affects to regard Australian
gambling as a new disease, writes to suggest
that the tote is an undeserving institution
because “ the public must lose whilst the horse-
racing game lasts, and surely that method is
best which soonest reduces it to the state when
gambling is impossible, unless in matches, thus
saving time tfiid trouble.” The serious inanity,
or ponderous satire, of the man “who never
bets” is bound to be favorable to Ikey Mo.
For the non-speculative mind there is comfort
and joy in the contemplation of a future when
everybody who has backed ’osses will be stone-
broke, and the total abstainer from gambling
will be able to gaze complacently upon the
destitution of the large majority. A nori-bettor
hates the moderate punter with the hatred that
teetotallers cherish towards moderate drinkers
who don’t point an alcoholic moral or adorn a
shocking tale. What the teetotaller specially
objects to is liquor legislation for the benefit of
moderate-drinking stomachs and pockets. He
doesn’t want his enemy to get reasonable plea-
sure at a reduced figure, hence his attitude to
the Australian wine industry. And for the
same reason the other sort of abstainer is
antagonistic to the tote.
M.L. Parliament, for one year, partially
adopted the sensible practice of allowing last
session’s unfinished Bills to be taken up where
they left off, instead of starting them again
from the beginning. It was only a small ex-
periment, applying merely to Bills which had
passed second reading, and even these had to
be revived by formal resolution. Now Premier
Seddon abandons this valuable reform on the
ground that it “ failed in its purpose of saving
time.” This is foolish retrogression. One
session isn’t a fair trial. Moreover, the scheme
didn’t get a decent show ; it ought to provide
that every Bill unfinished at the end of one
session shall revive automatically without any
formal resolution (the resolution being mere
waste of time) next session, and that unfinished
business shall only go overboard when Par-
liament dissolves. That would probably save a
vast amount of time. Anyhow, if the old
practice is as protracted as the new one,
what is the good of reverting: to it ? Seddon
is a reformer in many things, but in the matter
of Parliamentary forms and precedents he is a
bitter Conservative.
