The return of the Jubilee Singers brought
a groat collection of the unco guid to Sydney Palace
Theatre on Saturday. If first nights mean any-
thing, things look as if they are going to hum.
All the plaintive darky melodies, tinged always
with a hope of grace and a suspicion of salvation,
were trotted out again, and sung with the nod-
ding, rocking, lulling movements that characterise
this co. Mattie Allan is still the star. She looks
a very womanly little body, and makes faces
that might conquer an anchorite, but her voice
seems to come from the dark gentleman on
her left or right, and when she begins to sing,
you look at all the male Jubilee mouths to
see which is doing the deed. The voice is billed
as a female tenor, which means a contralto putting
on a man’s airs. They still sing, “ Steal away to
Jesus,” and “ Roll Jordan Roll,” but somehow the
Jubilees don’t seem to be quite as holy as they
used to be. A clever darkey juggler took the
stage on Saturday, and juggled in a way that
would have been smart at a first-class circus. Also
there was a blackie who put all the chairs on top
of each other and stood on the summit as if it had
been the ground floor. Any country cousin who
misses this cheerful show will certainly go home
the loser.
** * *
Her Majesty’s is closed this week, but
Williamson’s great Xmas spectacle, the “ Forty
Thieves,” will blaze there on Monday (20th).
And The Bulletin has good authority for report-
ing that it will be a great and memorable blaze,
and that Bagdad will be resurrected in a manner
worthy of its bygone traditions. For Bagdad is a-
city which has smelled through a vast procession
of centuries, and, in the day of its greatness, any
inquiring person could have found 40,000 thieves
there with very little difficulty—let alone 40.
Williamson has the Sydney pantomime biz all to
himself this year. The only excuse for that foolish
kind of entertainment is the glare of magnificence
that surrounds it, and painful experience has
shown that only the most bloated kind of manager
finds it worth his while to go in for it at all.
** * *
The opera season at the Lyceum starts on
Xmas Eve—everything in Sydney is starting on
Xmas Eve more or less—with “Carmen.” The
great voice of Edward Earley, which has been lost
to the stage for most of the last ten years, will be
uplifted in the baritone section of the show, and
Leumane, Miss Lilian Tree, and other singers will
fill the other parts. The company numbers 100 all
told—in the advertisement, anyhow—and the Mao-
Mahon firm intends to stage the show with great
finish and magnificence or burst—physically, not
financially—in the attempt. If it unfortunately
bursts its fragments will be published in next
issue. In the meantime “ His Natural Life ”is
filling in the last remnant of the dramatic season.
As the old Vandemonian woe has already been
running for over a week, and has only two more
nights to go, it is hereby dismissed with a caution.
It has been as good as many other versions of the
same cheerless, flogging, cursing, man-eating
tragedy, but at the best “His Natural Life ” is a
play which has always made this paper feel a
dislike to its vittles somehow.
** « *
The redoubtable Charles Holloway, brother
of Billolloway of that ilk, opens Sydney Royal on
Saturday with “When London Sleeps.”
** * *
Peggy Pryde has come back to Sydney
Tivoli; and Mr. Ajax, the man who is made of
india-rubber garden-hose boiled soft, still writhes
there on his stomach like the original serpent of
Genesis. Sometimes he swallows himself, and
then spits himself out again, and, at other times,
Ajax folds himself up and puts himself in his own
waistcoat-pocket. When he has a holiday he
spends it crawling down the stem of his pipe to
clean it. There have been times when he has
stowed himself away in the drawer of his desk and
quite forgotten where he had left himself ; he folds
and squashes and doubles up into such a small
parcel that he is as often mislaid as not. Ajax is
amazingly clever in his own line, but he is a crawl-
ing, unnatural, wrigglesome spectacle, like a
jumpy serpent with a human head apd the rest of
him man-constrictor. Sampson isn’t at the Tivoli
now. In the middle of the discussion as to whether
he was really a strong man or only a weak but
wily fragment, he quitted Sydney with his nose
bleeding badly. He had been breaking chains
with it, The Bulletin understands.
** « *
“ Robbery Under Arms,” when this paper
went to press, was doing its 28th night at the Cri-
terion, and the management’s spirits have been so
uplifted by prosperity that it has begun to publish
large slabs of poetry m its advertisement. “ His
Natural Life ” is still mentioned vaguely as the
next production, but otherwise there is no sign of
change.
“The Geisha,” produced at Melb. Prin-
cess’s last Saturday, is a more foolish narrative
than even “ Morocco Bound,” or “ The Gay Pari-
sienne,” but as a spectacle, with pleasant melodies
meandering through a meadow of slushy talk, it
amounts to something considerable. “ The
Geisha ” had to be produced here, anyhow. The
Firm io bound, by all rules of business, to import
the most popular London goods in the comic opera
line, and this trumpery vehicle for pretty music,
scenery and dresses got a two years’ run in the
Big Smoke. Granting that “The Geisha’’was
biult by an experienced stage craftsman, the
wonder is that the London manager who under-
took the show didn’t stipulate for the option of
getting the “book ” decently edited. A deplorable
book as it stands now. Yet the well-known
“ Geisha ” music (by Sydney Jones), although not
strikingly original, is always gay and festive,
despite the burden of its puerile descriptive
“lyrics.”
• » • *
The two scenes of “The Geisha” are
laid in Japan, the opera denying most of its
effects from “The Mikado,” and a good many
others from “The Gaiety Girl.” There are Jap.
firls and men in very gorgeous raiment, and
toward Vernon as a despotic form of Ko-Ko,
and Lauri as a Chinese lessee of the local tea-
shop ; also the French Maid appears again—as a
tea-shop waitress. Strangers arrive from Eng-
land ; four or five sassiety damsels who line-up
under the wing of a chaperon and some men in
uniform, just as in “The Gaiety Girl.” Only the
uniform this time is naval instead of military.
The chief Jap. girl, and naval officer William
Pauli, together with the principal English
firl and the French Maid and Chinaman
iauri, work out a lame story that falls flat and
exhausted at the finish. In point of fact, the plot
lays itself out for dead two or three minutes
before the curtain drops. And the trouble starts
dispiritingly. The first act gets left at the post,
but bustles along after a while; the second act
introduces a series of sudden exits and entrances
by the company in general, and some of the exits
are sufficiently unaccountable to be humorous, in
a sad way of speaking.
* * • •
“The Geisha,” taking the good with the
bad of it, looks likely to prove a fairly strong
attraction these times. The time when Melbourne
(or London, for that matter) would have rejected
such a scraggy yarn is not the present period. In
the light of wayback years—not so far back, either
—“The Geisha” performance doesn’t shine bril-
liantly. The new soprano, Miss Florence Perry,
is a, nice enough little warbler, with an eloquent
pair of dark eyes and an earnest expression—very
like a Miss Appleton in appearance and speech.
Her singing lacks force and roundness, and as an
actress she doesn’t fill the stage. Miss Rosa Mus-
grove is a promising young French Maid, but she
is not now appearing among amateurs, and the
crudeness of her attempts to imitate Ada Reeve’s
foreign accent and mannerisms was very obvious
on Saturday. Miss Vane, as the romping society
girl, has a showy part, and she gets vigorous ap-
plause ; all the same, little Miss Vane is not rich
in soubrette arts and graces. And her descriptive
ballad, or whatever it is!
Seems the notice sour and snarly ?
Don’t we all remember Varley,
And the graceful dancing Nellie, with her warble
high and clear,
And the golden note which Florrie
Flung to Elton and to Lauri
In the “Old Guard ” days departed—days so very,
very dear ?

In its dream of bygone voices
That the songs of newer sirens are less pleasing to

• * * *
Lauri and Howard Vernon are strong and
happy in their parts. William Pauli, making his
first opera appearance before a Melbourne crowd,
has recovered sufficient of his lost baritone to score
a vocal success, also his uniform suits him nicely.
The rest of the principals can share a big lump of
compliment with the chorus; and the scene-
painter, dress-designer, and orchestral-conductor
can consider themselves called before the curtain
to receive a blessing.
* * * *
The dead body of Brown intends to welter
in its gore at Melb. Royal until Friday night, on
which occasion Walter Baker and Albert Norman
will turn up the two greasy halves of their “Fatal
Card,” and a rather excellent melodrama will
make room for “ New Babylon.” The Bulletin
can’t remember exactly how many times Bland
Holt has erected “ New Babylon ” on the Royal
stage, but it understands that this time is to bo
the absolutely greatest up to date. Comic songs
and coster ballets are mentioned in the bill, and
many other extras of a Xmas season character are
to happen previous to the arrest of manager Holt
aforesaid, who gets overtaken by the Law in this
massive show, and is led away to be hanged with-
out the option of a fine.
* * * *
“ The Happy Life,” a conspicuously stagey
play in its sentimental places, but a fresh, young,
human comedy elsewhere, was to retire from the
Bijou last Wednesday. The comedy portions
grew breezier on acquaintance, and perhaps when
the fainted heroine is introduced to tne notice of a
Sydney audience the stage-manager will make
better arrangements for the disposal of her limp
form in the hero’s arm-chair. The mistake at the
Bijou was in letting her head hang over the back
of the chair, so that the public, naturally anxious
to note the lady’s face, could see little of it ex-
cept the chin. To-night (Thursday) and to-morrow
the Bijou closes for rehearsals of “Madame Sans-
Gene.” Mrs. Brough, in the name-part, will
make her first appearance at the gilded court of
Napoleon Brough Buonaparte, and confront the
Emperor with his overdue washing-bill on Xmas
Eve. The secenery for this expensive show was
built from Irving’s models, and the historical cos-
tumes were made in Germany ; and if Melbourne
doesn’t roll up to appreciate Brough as the Little
Corporal—well, the history of Europe will feel
grossly insulted.
*# # *
Strong-man Sampson has started to bulge
his biceps and break chains, and lift a lengthy
dumb-bell from the floor, at Melb. Opera House.
Also, he appears to bear the weight of a large
amount of wood and live girl upon his chest, but
the absolute genuineness of this feat is not made
clear to everybody. In any case, Sampson is so
strong that one would like him to do something
more useful or funny for a living. The only
amusing feature of Sampson’s engagement is the
allegation that he pulls £250 from Rickards every
treasury-day with one hand. The ventriloquist
and other people are appearing at the Opera House
as usual, also Miss Elorrie West, the “ Dazzler,”
burst forth there on Monday, unless something
happened to delay her dazzle.
** * *
Concerning Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus, at
the corner of Spring and Flinders streets, this
family journal will express some feelings next
week, and meantime it doesn’t think its views
towards the lion and the elephant, &c., have
grown less tender since the Fitz Bros, were in
Sydney. Melbourne is preparing for a rush of
Xmas productions. The Aquarium, at the Exhi-
bition Building, which was always a beautiful
shady retreat, has been made larger and more
dreamy of late. Visitors in search of a happy
afternoon are herewith advised to try the
Aquarium immediately and repeat the dose once a
quarter or so. Phil Newbury’s concert co. will
be making music for the multitude at the Exhibi-
tion Building on holiday dates.

And glad tidings comes from the vasty
Alexandra Theatre. The Haleck is to open again
on Boxing Night with a pantomime of consider-
able pretensions. The advt. talks about “ Great
Splendour ” in a reckless tone of voice, several
promising names appear in the cast, and George
Leopold, who is full of pantomime tradition, and
a past-master of red-hot poker business, will stage-
manage the sudden venture. The Alexandra
panto, has no direct opposition to its claims upon
the infant understanding, so the Beanstalk which
Jack built may take root and flourish.
*- * *
Melb. Argus seized the opportunity of
Henry Irving’s illness to make some solemn mis-
statements concerning the actor’s career. —“Old
London playgoers remember about the time of the
Franco-German war a certain comedian named
Irving, who amused in the part of Digby Grand.”
. . . . “ Possibly Irving would be comedian still
had it not occurred to him—always ambitious —to
beguile an interval by reciting ‘ Eugene Aram ’
before the curtain. . . . He fascinated his hearers.
It was a case of grasping the skirts of a happy
chance. But for ‘Eugene Aram,’ Irving might
not have troubled to buy a manuscript play which
contains the most gruesome of all his characters.
Then playgoers would not have witnessed the spec-
tacle of the comedian suddenly metamorphosed
into the tragedian, and making them shudder with
his portrait of the haunted burgomaster in ‘The
Bells.’” One of the most pretentious absurdities
in daily journalism is the “historical ” leader by a
man who is talking from foolish hearsay, or from
his own bad memory. The character which Irving
Elayed about the Franco-German war time was
>igby Grant (not Grand) in “ The Two Roses.”
Digby Grand was the name of a novel, also of a
horse that won the City and Sub. Handicap about
that time. Long before 1870 the picturesque in-
tensity of Irving’s acting had been noted by
Charles Dickens. It is on record that Dickens,
witnessing Irving’s performance in “Dearer than
Life,” said to his son who was with him : “Mark
me, that young man will make a great name.”
Remembering which prophecy, the Dickens
family presented Irving in after years with
a souvenir of the dead author. Again there
is the fact that Irving had scored a big
success at the Gaiety Theatre, as the gloomy
Mr. Chevenix in “Uncle Dick’s Darling,” pre-
vious to appearing as Digby Grant. As for the
“ Eugene Aram ” recitation aforesaid it was first
fiven at a Dramatic Fund benefit mating, at
)rury Lane, after Irving had become famous.
He was then acting in either “The Bells” or
“Charles the First.” “The Bells” manuscript
was sold to Bateman, who, against the advice of
his friends, decided to give the play and Irving a
chance to draw all London to the Lyceum, a
notoriously unlucky theatre up to then. All
London came, and Bateman soon found himself
rich for the first time in his life. He raised
Irving’s salary by leaps and bounds, and secured
him on a long lease, so that when he died, in the
middle of the first “ Hamlet ” boom, Irving went
with the theatre under the management of widow
Bateman. When her lease of the theatre expired
Irving took possession.
At Poverty Point.
Widdy O’Brien Sheridan and the awful Teddy Lonnen
are running a Recital Company around the small towns
of Hingland. Business is said to be gloomy. Alice
Lethbridge and Gracie Whiteford figure in the co., and
they all talk about dear old Australia, and the days
when they achieved great receptions and treble encores.
This country gets a splendid character from departed
“stars” who are playing to an audience of about 40
souls in an ill-lighted hall at the other end of the world.
Charles Cartwright was wanted for the part of
Richelieu in one of the London productions of “ Three
Musketeers,” but was said to be too ill to accept the en-
gagement.
“ F.” : In defence of the recent “ Mikado ” perform-
ance, more “comic” and less liked than any previous
treatment of the same opera in Melbourne, manager Wil-
liamson revived an old Yimkee-Australian story to the
effect that “ Pinafore ” didn’t begin to catch-on in London
until it had been played with enormous success in New
York, by a manager who enlarged the effects and made
“a number of important innovations.” The writer of
this par. remembers “ Pinafore” as a London boom from
the very first. “ The Sorcerer,” which preceded it, had
been reckoned a revelation in operatic humor, and “ Pina-
fore ” was everywhere said to be much better than even
that. “ Pinafore ” jokes and “ Pinafore ” music travelled
around the town like wildfire. The story about American
excitement “finding its echo in England” must have
been founded on the mistaken notion, that “ Pinafore ”
was the first of the Gilbert and Sullivan successes.
George Rignold assures a Sydney paper that his future
arrangements are “in the hands of the Almighty.” Was
George dissatisfied with his former business manager?
“ N.” : Battle of Omdurman at Melt). Exhibition. Bing,
bang, boom. Supposed killed, seven —at the Sick Chil-
dren’s Hospital opposite.
“ S.” : Ugo Biondi gave rather startling proof of his
limitations before leaving Melbourne, when he added a
new feature to his entertainment. This was an attempt
to represent about a dozen of the world’s celebrities, and
there is only one word to describe the show —bad. The
work was bad in detail and in the lump, and Ugo’s cele-
brities looked like a procession of debilitated nonde-
scripts escaped from a cheap waxworks. The Prince of
Wales, as represented by Biondi, was a shocking carica-
ture. This impersonation was sedition and treason of
the most reckless description.
Dear Bulletin.— The man who assures you that he saw
James Milburn do the “ Scarecrow Dance ” 25 years ago
can’t be proved to be a romancer. I can only tell you that
Milburn never danced the “Scarecrow ” to the knowledge
of men who knew him well and performed at the same
halls with him.— Unbeliever.

“S.S.” offers his opinion: “Most satisfactory of the
Brough importations, judged by their work in ‘The Liars’
and ‘ Lord and Lady Algy,’ is Dartrey, whose two old
men have been the neatest, cleanest kind of comedy.
His Duke of Droneborough is intelligent and decisive
caricature, in sharp lines. By the way, * Lord and Lady
Algy ’ is a most callous comedy, and stubbornly disregard-
ful of histrionic conventions in leaving everybody morally
exactly where it found them. One feels that the reunion
of Lord and Lady Algy is merely pro. tem. Carton con-
verts nobody and improves none, and does not even take
the trouble to clear up many of the misunderstandings
between his stage relations. Reconciliations on the
boards are always rash and palpably unsubstantial.
Carton’s trick gives the idea that he has caught a bit of
1 sassiety ’ on the move, and has left it moving in active
pursuit of all its favorite sins.”
When lately heard from, Alfred Rolfe and Mrs. Rolfe
(Miss Lily Dampier) were in England, playing in a huge
sensation called “ Honor Bright.” The two authors of
“Honor Bright” have struck a rather novel horror.
Their heroine is tied at night to the great bell of St.
Paul’s, so that the big iron clapper will strike the hour
on her head and drop her brains into the sleeping city
below. So she hangs there, taking a deeper interest in
the time than she ever took before.
A wizard’s reputation takes long to grow, seemingly.
Dante, at 27, is only beginning to make a name for him-
self. Fifteen years hence he will be living on his name
(if he hasn’t died meanwhile), and younger rivals will have
to beat him hollow as an illusionist before they can hope
to prosper.
“ Blister”: Rebottle-and-rag collectors’entertainments.
There’s a 10-year-old youngster in Carlton (Melb.) who
has been giving Saturday afternoon performances in a
shed for over two years. Admission, front row (no chairs)
a bottle ; back rows, batch of rags or bones ; very back
row, o bone. The best house he has had was 2s. 3d. A
Is. 3d. house he calls fair, and a 9d. poor. Performers,
himself (song and dance artist) and two younger aspir-
ants ; sometimes three when a “ grand double bill ” is on.

Trottaire ? He say he is a moozishun, bot who effer hear
him plai ? At some consair you vill see de naime on de
pro-grom. He vill plai de Liszt Rhapsodie nommer fife.
On de night of de consair comes a man on de steeje out
an zay he sorry dat Meestair Trottaire have influenza in
hees leetal fingair and can not possible plai. Ze rapporta
of ze pepper he not at de consair, bot he write good
nottice an’ say, ‘Mr. Trottaire plai Liszt Rhapsodie
“ byootafull." ’ Desem ting happen to me one time. De
rapporta say I plai ‘ MotoPerpetua,’ splandeed, an’ l am
not at de consair at all. lam blin' dronk chez moi. Some
people who have hear it say dat Mr. Trottair can imitate
lovely de dicky herd ‘ peeweet’ on de fifteent position oop
behint de britch of de feeddle—bot he starfe all de sera.
Vot do you tink he ees by birth ? He is a tailor! An’l
zay it is bettair to mek monai as a goot tailor dan to
starfe as a bad moozishun. Hein ?
An Ophelia at the Johannesburg theatre insisted on
being carried on instead of the dummy for the burial
scene. She died two days after.
Apropos of his success with the farcical comedy, “ What
Happened to Jones,” our old friend Charles Arnold has
been interviewed in London Echo. Arnold, in the course
of a ver.y readable yarn, said that his most useful experi-
ences were gained during a two years’season in Montreal,
with a stock co., when a new show—comic opera, comedy
or drama—was produced every Monday, to run for one
week. He played all sorts of dialect parts, in addition to
acting as manager for the front of the house, looking
after the ads. and newspaper paragraphs. In England,
and nowhere else, he has played the character of de-
fendant in a police-court case, brought against him by
the Society for the Protection of Children. The society
objected to his little baby-girl romping with him in
“ Hans the Boatman,” but the Bench dismissed the sum-
mons, with costs.

It would seem that Henry Irving has a kind of back-of-
the-head idea of visiting Australia some day. Robert
Brough, when in London, wanted to buy the rights of
•‘ Olivia ” (the beautiful play which Wills wrote out of
Goldsmith’s “ Vicar of Wakefield ”) and “ Nance Oldfield.”
Irving owns these two pieces, and one would have thought
that he could have no objection to selling the Australian
rights of them. But he wouldn’t. “ Olivia ” would fit
Mrs. Brough like a glove.
A female opinion picked up in a Melbourne theatre:
“Do you know, I think he is an atheist. He never rises
when the orchestra plays 'God Save the Queen.’ ”

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Even among the many muddled Bills which the
present N.S.W. Government has thrown together with a
hay-fork and then shied at the head of Parliament the
new Land and Income Tax (Declaratory) Bill is notable.
Its first clause reads: “1. Any person, wheresoever
residing or domiciled, who, either before or after the
commencement of this Act, extracts ft'oin the soil, wins,
produces, or manufactures in New South Wales any pro-
duct, commodity, or substance, and exports the same,
shall be deemed to have carried on and to carry on in New
South Wales the trade of extracting from the soil, win-
ning, producing, or manufacturing such product, com-
modity, or substance ; and the value of such product,
commodity, or substance when exported shall be deemed
to have been and to be the ‘ taxable amount ’ within the
meaning of the Land and Income Tax Assessment Act of
1895.”
The most glaringly foolish Government should
be able to see where this clause leads to. A man
may export £IO,OOO worth of wool, and, after paying
wages, interest on mortgage or bank overdraft, freight,
and all other expenses, he may have only £3OO clear in-
come. Yet, because he exports his wool, the taxable
value is to he taken at the whole £IO,OOO, conse-
quently his income-tax will be £250. Last balance-
sheet of the British Broken Hill Cos. showed that
it had exported minerals to an immense value, and yet
it had no income—instead, there was a heavy loss. Yet,
under this amazing clause, it would have to pay Cd. in
the £on the whole value of the ore exported. And, sup-
posing a bank exports £2,000,000 of gold in a year—gold
produced in N.S.W.—would there be £50,000 income-tax
to pay on it? According to the wording of the clause, it
looks as if there would be.
» * * *
Premier G. Reid, of N.S.W., spends much of
the country’s good ink and stationery in publishing,
through the Government Printing Office, more or less
unreliable “ fakes ” about the imaginary misdeeds of his
predecessors in office. He lately issued, at the country’s
expense, a statement which purported to show that the
cost of running N.S.W. under his own beautiful adminis-
tration, and under the awful administration of the other
fellows, was:—
1886—Jennings.. .. £9 7 4 per inhabitant
1890 —Parkes .. ... 813 5 „ „
1894—Dibbs .. .. 714 10 „ „
IS9B—Reid . ..7 32,, ~
A notable circumstance is that every one of these figures,
except his own, is a great deal higher than the amount
stated by Coghlan in his State Statistics. That official
charges Jennings, in 1886, with £9 2s. lOd. ; Parkes, in
1890, with £8 7s. 3d.; and Dibbs with £7 Bs. 4d. He also
specifies that a great part of Parkes’s extra expenditure
arose through the fact that he paid much more of the
public works expenditure out of revenue than the present
man does. Same Old George’s figures always have an
ancient and fish-like suspicion about them somehow.
* • * *
The Australasian Temperance and General Life
Assurance Society concluded a moderate year on 30th
September, 1898, so far as its Ordinary Department is con-
cerned. Some of the figures for the last few years are as
follows:
Income. Working Expenses.
1892 ... £37,399 £BB9B
1893 ... 33,367 8240
1894 38,304 7907
1895 .. 38,120 8134
1896 ... 39,707 10,885
1897 42,787 11,888
1893 .. 46,647 13,816
These figures show, among other things, that the working
expenses, instead of diminishing as the society grows
larger, have increased till they now reach the tremendous
figure of over 29J per cent, on the total income, or
over 38 per cent, on premiums—about the worst record
in Australasia. Since 1892 the income has increased
by £9248 and the expenses by £4918, and an increase
that is bought so dearly is hardly worth having. The
receipts from interest and rents (equal to 5 per cent,
per annum on the average funds) look very satisfactory.
Also, the balance-sheet is a very clear and definite docu-
ment which supplies full information about the society’s
situation. In fact, the only thing that looks very much
wrong, so far as the ordinary department is concerned, is
the dreadful expense account. There are too many life-
assurance societies in these provinces, and one that is
small and little known and not too prosperous has to
spend so much money to get business that the business
is hardly worth getting. The 38 per cent, of premiums
that goes in expenses makes a fearful hole in the bonuses.
For the five years ended 30th September, 1895, the society
had only £4365 to divide among its policy-holders, and the
bonus prospects at the next investigation in 1900 are an
interesting problem.
The Industrial Department of the Temperance
and General is also, so far as expenses are concerned, one
of the most remarkable things of its kind in the Seven
Provinces. Its record of late years is as follows :
Income. Ex P ense3 ’ Claims ’ Outgoings.
1892 11,790 £4,297 £1,349 £5,646
1893 10,792 3,485 2,078 5,563
1894 10,224 3,342 2,494 5,836
1895 9,592 3,301 6,057 8,358
1896 9,501 4,490 7,192 11,682
1897 . 13,067 14,741 6,892 20,633
1898 17,879 16,744 6,680 22,424
This shows some curious results. Up to 1895 the Temper-
ance and General’s industrial business was steadily
diminishing, though every year it added from £IOOO to
£6OOO to its funds. Then in 1897 and 1898, the society
started to hustle furiously for new business, which is a
woefully expensive affair in the industrial line, and it got
it with such success that the expenses alone more
than swallowed up the total income. At the end of
1895 the industrial department of the T. and G. had
an accumulated fund of £40,012. Now, with larger
liabilities, the fund is only £20,756, a sum of £4061
having been written off for depreciation. If the expan-
sion of business continues on the same glorious principle
for a few years more the liabilities will be a good deal
larger still, and there will be no fund at all. The T. and
G. is becoming an interesting study in both departments.
• * * *
That small concern, the People’s Prudential
Benefit Society (Limited), of Sydney (which is really a
proprietary company doing a mixed industrial life, acci-
dent and sick benefit business, so that the term “ society ”
is a trifle misleading), sends along its report for the
second year of its existence, to 31st August, 1898. The
two years compare as follows
Total
Income. Expenses. Claims. Outgoings.
1897 .. £8,611 £7,228 £534 £7,762
1893 .. 11,670 4,646 6,173 10,819
£20,281 £18,581
The difference (£1700) represents the accumulated assur-
ance fund. The institution has also a paid-up capital of
£4043, but there is nothing to show for £3500 of it except
the goodwill of the business—that amount having been
paid, The Bulletin believes, to one Mulligan, who for-
merly owned the concern all by himself; therefore the
cash security w'hich the shareholders offer for the due
fulfilment of the society’s obligations is really only £542.
Anyhow, the institution doesn’t look quite so bad as it
did last year in one respect, expenses being only 40 per
cent, of receipts, whereas last year they were over 80 per
cent. Forty per cent, is reckoned very cheap in the
industrial business.
* * * •
The bulk of the claims (£5600) are compensation
for sickness or accident, and as these are matters which
go by no definite rule, it is difficult to say whether the
society, with £2200 of visible assets, is sound or not.
Presumably it will some day have a valuation of its life
and endowment liabilities, and thereby throw some light
on its position. In view of the multitude of small in-
dustrial institutions doing business in Australasia, the
present one doesn’t appear to be needed, but presumably
it affords a decent maintenance for the people who
live on it, and it certainly appears to spend less of
the premiums on expenses than do most institutions
of its kind. Still if it can’t save more than £BSO out of an
income of £11,670, the prospects of the shareholders
getting an honestly-earned dividend don’t look up to
much, and it certainly seems wildly ridiculous to value
the goodwill at £3500. Still, that is the shareholders’
private funeral. At all events, the People’s Prudential
has abstained from declaring dividends so far, which con-
trasts favorably with the record of another new office—
the Australian Metropolitan of Brisbane—where the pro-
prietary helps itself to 10 per cent, annually without wait-
ing to discover, by a formal actuarial valuation, whether
there is any surplus to pay it out of. Barring the good-
will item, there is nothing particular to complain about
in the P.P. this year save its superfluousness.
» * * •
Announced that the Cornwall Fire and Marine
Insurance Cos. (Launceston) has sold its fire business to
the Sun Cos. (of England). Presumably the marine busi-
ness goes somewhere else, as it would hardly pay to keep
that precarious line of industry going by itself, and the
Cornwall is making a general retirement. This makes the
sixth Australian insurance co. which has sold out to Bull
this year, the other five being the City Mutual (Sydney),
Southern and Indemnity (Melbourne), Queensland
Mutual (Brisbane), and S. A. Insurance Cos. (Adelaide). The
Cornwall was a reasonably-prosperous concern fors7 years,
but one bad year incidental upon the great Melbourne
fire made it lose heart. Both it and the S.A. Cos. got
into trouble through not sticking to one province, as a
State insurance department would do—in other words,
through adhesion to that maxim of private fire-insurance
management which advocates spreading the risk over a
wide area. It was that same spreading of the risk which
brought them into the Melbourne conflagration.

* * * *
The Langridge Mutual Permanent Building
Society (Melbourne) reports a profit for the year ended
20th September, 1898, of £2472. The last seven years’
profits of this institution have been :
1892 .. £8265 1896 .. £1917
1893 .. 5148 1897 .. 2254
1894 .. 3378 1898 .. 2472
1895 .. 3046
The Langridge has been a remarkable survivor of the
general boom-wreck, and, after touching bottom in 1896,
it is apparently well on the up-grade again. In the seven
years its capital has diminished from £123,038 to £86,025,
mainly through repayments to investing shareholders, so
the fall in earnings is partly owing to the reduced size of
thebusiness. In the same period deposits have been reduced
from £57,874 to £15,370, and reserves have increased
from £16,120 to over £30,000. The only dubious point is
that £129,836 of assets are put in at book-value, the
Langridge following the foolish practice of keeping the
old inflated values standing among the assets with a big
paper-reserve on the other side. Still, as the reserves
are large enough to cover a drop of nearly 24 per cent, in
the book-value of the assets, the capital is probably intact,
and possibly there is a margin to the good.
* * * *
The little Tasmanian Permanent Executors and
Trustees Association reports a profit, for the half-year
ended 30th September, 1898, of £405, and pays a 5 per
cent, dividend (£250 and £l2 10s. dividend-tax). This is
(with the exception of the first half of 1896) the best
record the institution has put up since September, 1593.
In March, 1893, it paid 6 per cent. ; in September, ’93, 7
per cent. ; then it paid five dividends of 6 per cent, up to
March, ’96, since when the div. has been 5 per cent.
Between March, ’94, and March, ’97, it five times paid
away more than its entire earnings in dividends, and the
net result of six years’ work is an addition of only £4OB
to the funds. There is no reserve-fund, unless the £673
accumulated profit can he called one, and this isn’t much
to show after an existence of 11 years.
» * • •
The man who is dead is a great institution in the
land of the Q.N. Bank. There was quite a corpsey flavor
about Somerset’s defalcations and sentence. Prisoner
pleaded that in the boom-days he went ’Ome rich, leav-
ing his affairs in the hands of a Q.N.B. official ; on return
found himself a ruined man ; and, later on, the aforesaid
official died. The present Auditor-General excused the
non-audit of the Pensions Account (where Somerset’s de-
falcations came in) because his predecessor (dead) had
not had them audited. The Under-Secretary of the
Treasury says his predecessor (dead) didn’t trouble much
about them, so it was mainly an accident that brought
the game to light.

* • * *
The N.S.W. Govt, purchase of Tarrawingee
line, out from Broken. Hill, has stirred again the Far
West agitation for a line between the Barrier and the
River Darling, tapping the latter at Menindie. The dis-
tance would be about 65 miles. Three main arguments
are used in favor of the undertaking, which has already
been favored by the Public Works Committee : It would
open up an alternative route from the silver-fields to the
ocean, via Darling and Murray ; it would give irrigation
and agriculture somewhere near the Menindie lakes a
lift, for Broken Hill cannot grow as much as a cabbage for
its own consumption ; and it would put Broken Hill, town
and mines, which now pay 255. a ton for firewood, within
handy distance of the almost inexhaustible supplies of
box on the river flats.
The route suggested for the proposed Govt, lino
to connect Broken Hill with the South Australian railway
svstem at Oockburn is by way of Pinnacles and Thacka-
ringa. At both of these places a few small mines still
struggle on, and, as they are essentially lead mines, they
would certainly do better if a through line were built.
The route was proposed almost as early as that now roi-
lowed by the Silverton Tramway—the fattest monopoly
in all Australia—and was engineered m the Barriers
early days by G. M‘L. Matheson, one-time M.P. for Glen
Innes, who lately died in Westralia. South Australia, of
course, approves cordially of the idea that N.S.W. should
either build a line to compete with the Silverton Tramway
or take over the latter ; for if it does either of those two
things, it is all the less likely that N.S.W. will afterwards
build a competing line to connect Broken Hill with the
Sydney system.
• * * *
The British B.H. Co.’s cabled loss of .£3552 on
the half-year’s operations covers everything, including
depreciation. Apart from outlay on rearranging the
machinery, the accounts about squared. The co. has a
zinc process—the Cowper-Ooles—in hand ; zinc oxide,
suitable for paint manufacture, has been produced direct
from the ore ; hut other things have yet to be reported
on. This, of course, isn’t necessarily much of an advance.
The Carmichael and other Australian processes can get
out the zinc all right in the laboratory, hut unfortunately,
in practice, the appliances so far devised will not stand
the necessary acids and the intense heat sufficiently to
allow of economic success. That, too, was what was the
matter with the Ashcroft electrolytic system.

Booth’s United, a mine in the Gundagai dis-
trict, N.S.W., on which big money from Vic. and London
has been spent. “ Passed through horizontal seam Bin.
thick, carrying gold. Started to clean up Mt. Morgan
shaft; lode at bottom split into several stringers, which
show nice gold and give excellent prospects by dish.
Assays taken from same average 4oz. per ton.” The
writer has heard a horrible story of this show, and, from
his youth up, he has been suspicious of all mines having
“seams and stringers,” where any reliance is placed on
“assays,” and which have anything about them called
“ Mt. Morgan.” Therefore, he is sorry for Booth’s United.

• * • •
Dear Bulletin. —Your correspondent “Dun-
can ” touches a Vic. Govt, scandal when he asks : “ What
has become of the promised report on the Mount Ded-
dick alleged mines?” A year ago Melbourne Argus and
Age were publishing Deddickskite pars, at the rate of
about three a week. When Minister Foster and the Govt,
geologists were inspecting the God-forsaken Deddick dis-
trict, last May, they were accompanied by reporters who
wrote yards of vague description, treating the visit as a
matter of vast public interest. Also, from that time
until now there has been a sustained effort to boom
Mount Deddick as a mining “ field ” for Govt, railway
enterprise, and it was even said, by the Argus and Age,
that the Cabinet would hold a special meeting to consider
the great Deddick question. All this time the truth about
the alleged mines was a matter of common knowledge on
Melb. Stock Exchange. If the Govt, geologists’ reports
had been published long ago according to promise, the
mere suggestion of a Deddick railway at the expense of
the State would have looked ridiculous. But the reports
didn’t appear, and the Deddickskiting went on unchecked
until last week, when “ a deputation of gentlemen in-
terested in the silver field ” waited on Premier Turner
and was definitely told that the precious “field” had
done nothing whatever to deserve a railway.— Conrad.
* • * •
Miles of Campbell’s and other creeks in the
Castlemaine (Vic.) district have been taken up for dredg-
ing, and the results will be of great interest, particularly
to N.S.W. The bulk of the dirt to be worked is not only
tailings, but tailings that have been turned over several
times. If it still remains good enough to pay by dredg-
ing, then there are millions of tons of old wash as well as
new on the old alluvial camps of N.S. W. that should pay
much better. A little dredging is about to be started in
N.S.W., and a great deal is talked about. Talk is even
cheaper than dredging.

still if the mine changed its name.

Announced in London that Staples has arrived
there with an option on the New Moon mine (paid-up capi-
tal, £3600; dividends to date, about £57,000), Bendigo.
It has had a longer div.-paying run than most Bendigo
mines, though the average is under 7dwts. per ton. The
value represented, by the option in cash and shares is
about double that of the market, and it will be interesting
to know how much above the option the public will be
asked to pay in London.

round Chillagoe and Hodgkinson fields paints a deplorable
picture of the state of affairs from a working miner’s point
of view. Country mostly locked up and under exemp-
tion waiting for boom to come for floating purposes. At
the Hodgkinson there is not room left by the syndicators
and shepherds for a working miner to crawl in sideways.

Sinking recommenced at Ophir Block, Croydon
(Q.), 1st Dec., to further test the “slide” theory. At
250ft. the co. is sinking on the face and grade of the
slide which is carrying a vein of quartz. In the No. 7
North Golden Gate the reef was picked up in increased
richness below the slide. At the Ophir it is fondly hoped
that a similar result will follow.

A well-known mining man, who was enthusias-
tic over the richness of Hannan’s long before other mining
men saw it, is now just as bad concerning what he de-
clares to be a similar formation found by him in Northern
Queensland. He has secured a large area, had several
bulk crushings for upwards of an ounce to the ton, cya-
nided the tailings for over half an oz., has taken the ore
for a great width, and is otherwise assuring himself of
its value. It is in the vicinity of a long-neglected gold-
field, and would probably have continued to be passed by
unnoticed but for his experience at Kalgoorlie.
• * * •
Gilfillan and McCreery, mining engineers,
metallurgists and assayers, 434 Collins-st., Melbourne.
Reports on mines, prepares plans and specifications for all
kinds of mining plant. Complete plant for bulk tests.*
• * * •
Dear Bulletin. —N.Q. papers are printing an
attractive yarn about a big gold discovery (unproved) on
the Hodgkinson by R. B. Pettigrew, “ the first man to
discover payable gold at Kalgoorlie.” 1 always thought
it was Dave O’Neill, of the Lake View, who found payable
gold at Kal.; but let that pass. The point is the effort to
resuscitate a dead boom on the Hodgkinson by saying
the formations there appealed to the trained eye of R. B.
Pettigrew as similar to those at Kalgoorlie. Q. Govt,
geologist, in his report on the Hodgkinson in ’83, says : —
“ I found that the gold dies out in depth. Miners sink
block shafts in front so as to pick up the gold chute in
greater depth, and in this way they are disappointed, and
I would advise them to drive on the reef horizontally both
sides of the shaft as there is more chance to pick up a
new chute.” Doesn’t this go to show that the gold runs
are not permanent ? The field has had a fair trial. For
instance, the Tyrconnel Company spent £12,000, paid one
dividend of £750 and abandoned the show ; the Home-
ward Bound spent £5000 and failed ; the Home Rule
spent £2000 and failed; the Caledonia spent £1600 and
failed ; the Homeward Bound taken up again by a new
company spent £1400 without success ; ditto the Union,
which erected a mill and winding plant and sold the lot
for £75. The Reconstruction mine crushed from 1893
to Aug., ’97 548 tons of stone, and when the yield was less
than 2oz. to the ton it would not pay working expenses.
The Sunday claim crushed 6£ tons for 66oz., and it took
two men seven months to raise the 6) tons. They worked
for two months after that crushing, but never got a color,
and the mine was abandoned. The gold chutes are too
small; that is the secret of the failures. So as not to do
too much work in the blank ground, shafts have been
sunk on the same mine almost within a stone’s throw of
one another. On the Caledonia, five shafts have been
sunk in this fashion. On the Vulcan, six shafts are down
on a strip of ground 560ft. long. The blocks of reef left
between the shafts will not yield 4dwt. A trial crushing
from the General Grant—the stone being taken between
the shafts—yielded 5dwt. If you read the tables of yields
from the old field you will see that the last crushing of a
number of mines was 2oz. and over. If there had been
any more of that tack, would those mines be idle now f—
Hodgkinson.
• • * . *
The Ballarat Woollen Mills lately called for
competitive designs and tenders from builders of steam
engines, that of the Austral Otis Engineering Company,
South Melbourne, being accepted. These engines are of
the compound surface condensing type, with cylinder*
17" and 38" by 60" stroke, and will indicate 700 h.p. on a
consumption of 151b. steam per hour. The design is a
handsome and massive one, and they are probably th®
finest example of engine-building yet produced in the
colony.*

Now that the Mount Lyell Co. has had its
“ burst ” on the rich ore and has to settle down in earnest
to make a living out of plain average rock, it should be
time to cut down the staff and make the balance do a
day’s graft like any other workmen. There is no cash
now to waste on finding fancy billets for anybody* uncle
or other relative.

Mephan Ferguson, engineer, &c., of Melbourne,
Adelaide, and Perth, the sole agent in Australia for J. and
E. Hall’s patent carbonic anhydride refrigerating and
ice-making machinery, is at present showing a large stock
of these machines, which have been got ready for the
summer season. The cost of refrigerators to produce
6cwt. of ice per day has been reduced to £100 each.
Special prices are quoted for machines to make up to 50
tons of ice. The patent carbonic anhydride system ha8
been adopted by the Victorian and other Australian
Governments; also by all the leading steamship com-
panies, meat companies, dairies, ana butchers of the
world.*

Cabled that the Privy Council dismissed the
appeal Croudace v. Zobel, referring to the old Cadia
copper-mine, in the Orange district. Zobel seeks to get a
lease to work for gold, and it looks as if he would now get
it. The Mining on Private Property Act specially ex-
cludes copper, and how Zobel is going to work gold with-
out copper will be the next trouble. The Cadia was a
good mine 30 years ago.

The latest yarn going the Melb. rounds is
that Gov. Brassey is in the habit of meeting
Premier Turner down at St. Hilda baths every
morning, and competing with him in diving for
threepenny-bits. As Turner always provides the
coins (which looks eminently improbable), and
invariably lets Brassey win (which looks still
more improbable, for it is Turner’s capacity for
pursuing the threepenny to the bitter end which
is the secret of his success as Treasurer), his Ex.
revels in the sport. Fancy Brassey revelling !
He also swims Turner to the fence and back “fpr
fun” because Turner always licks him at that.
.The Bulletin has doubts about this yarn all
round.
“Does a man abandon his hair in the
barber’s shop ? ” was the question put to Judge
Real by a leading solicitor in Brisbane, the other
day. Judge Real thought he did—of necessity.
“But,” continued the legal querist, “if a girl
has a nice head of hair cut off in a barber’s shop,
she still owns it when it is removed, and she takes
it away with her. ” The Judge looked puzzled.
“ Further,” continued learned counsel, “ a man
had his leg cut off in hospital. On the leg vvas
an interesting tumor coveted by the operating
Sawbones. But the man claimed his leg, collared
his limb and his tumor and departed.” And the
court groaned, held its head in its hand, and
thought hard.
* * • *
A spectacle of great tonnage is that of an
upright magistrate'wrestling with a desire to be
lenient to a first offender providing he refunds.
Some little time ago a Sydney youth passed him-
self off in Melbourne as Toby Barton’s son, and
obtained goods by false representations from sun-
dry warehousemen. At the City Court his
brother stated that most of the ill-gotten property
had been returned, and that he (the brother) was
struggling hard to make refunds for the remain-
der. On the understanding that he would make
all the losses good, the case was adjourned, and
the brother kept on struggling with an idea that
his relative would get off. At next appearance,
P.M. Pan ton was informed that he had faithfully
fulfilled his promise, and that it was hoped the
week’s imprisonment and the fright would be a
warning to the offender. Then Panton passed
sentence of six months’ hard—which was probably
about as much as the prisoner would have got in
any case. If the brother who nearly busted his
immortal soul raising the means to refund the
stolen goods had known what was going to happen
he would probably have saved his money. Only
then the importing firms wouldn’t have got their
property back.
«. • * •
Over in Maoriland a young wife wants to
be divorced from her artist husband because he
paints from models in the altogether. He was an
artist before she married him—quite naturally—
and he then painted the nude hired female in his
studio. Presumably his best girl saw his pictures
or heard of them, but either she didn’t quite
realise the situation, or she assumed, in her bliss-
ful ignorance, that nude pictures were painted
wholly from imagination. After they were
married he pursued his art at home, and, naturally,
the nude subjects called there in their business
capacity. (This isn’t meant to imply that they
were nude when they called.) The bride solemnly
avers that she took them for wealthy ladies who
called to get their portraits painted, till the inter-
fering woman in an adjacent house told her they
were models. She asked what models were, and
learned that they were shocking people in various
stages of undress. Then she bored a hole through
the studio partition, saw her shattered idol pursu-
ing his profession, and fled home to her ma, and
now the “ nood ” in art is at a discount in the
Scotchbyterian land of the Moa. Yet even Moa-
land will probably admit that an artist can’t live
solely by painting bony kirk elders in black coats

Dear Bulletin. —Recently tracked the
city boarding-house fowl to its lair—in a wood-
and-coal yard, and have since noted that every
other coal-yard notifies young fat poultry for sale.
Fancy the succulent Xmas duck dieted on coal-

• * * *
“ S.S.” : Fly-paper is an excellent medium
for a gamble in the fly season, provided you are
located Out Back, and beyond the reach of the
usual allurements. I have been told of a club
that has Sunday afternoon sittings in a dull
stretch in an auriferous area in Croagingolong
(Vic.), where flies are the most active emissaries
of the enemy of man. Each member of the school
arrives with a virgin sheet; these are spread
fairly, and for every fly he entangles the owner of
the sheet draws a copper from the other com-
petitors. Another plan is to mark a sheet of fly-
paper into numbered squares, players to back the
square that pleases them to catch the largest
number of flies in a given time. The winner
scoop 3 the pool, less a percentage for expenses and
refreshments and fly-paper.
* * * *
N.S.W. Public Service Commissioners,
after shying off Broken Hill for a couple of years,
got that far the other day. The local oflicers had
torn a leaf out of the book of the God-fearing
Ministry, and had made prayers—not for rain,
though, but for scorching sun, and blistering
wind, and blinding dust. And once more things
went wrong ; the bad days came along before the
board did, though a bit of dust was left over. The
disgusted servants had prepared eloquent pleas for
special allowances, by reason of the awful climate,
and the climate wouldn’t turn up when it was
most required.
• • • •
John Blyth, a hairy Scotch person who
was a prominent figure in the late demented
land-boom, stated the other day, at a Harbor
Trust meeting, that 225. a week was a good wage
for a working-man. In fact, the hairy one thought
it was more than good. “Ah believe,” he said,
“ that 22 shullings is a vera excellent wage fur a
worrking-mon—a vera excellent wage.” And the
useless old boomster charges about twice that sum
for talking a few minutes’ Scotch at Harbor
Board meetings once a fortnight.
• • • *
Some time last year a crowd of influential
Melbourne citizens resolved to present a gold
watch to that weary humorist Zox, M.L.A., his
previous watch having been snatched from his
copious white weskit by parties who are still at
large. They had a great festivity over the pre-
sentation ; 24 bottles of champagne were drunk,
and 50 cigars were smoked, and vittles were
assimilated, and Zox’s happy red countenance
could hardly be seen for smoke when he returned
thanks in a deep, fruity voice. For nearly 12
months since then it appears that the miserable
caterer has been pursuing the committee with the
bill for refreshments provided the night Zox got
his watch, and, by way of a squalid end to the
proceedings, Judge Gaunt lately ordered the com-
mittee’s secretary to pay up the balance or go to
gaol for seven days. Apparently, the hat has
been passed round in vain among the influential
citizens and the miserable secretary is left.

“ Onlooker ” : Be our cable-
service. What in Hades does it
matter to the average Australian
sojourning in sin, sand, sorrow, or
solitariness whether Gwendoline
May or Augustus John “ pass a
fourth-class exam, in music, or
muffin - making, or anything else ?
The Australian cable- 11 service ” is
the most ridiculously lop-sided, jerky,
and unreliable arrangement that ever
disgraced the press. One day we get
the first instalment of a really inter-
esting bit of news : next day it has
been discontinued, or strays off into a
tangle that takes the month-old files
of the TiMiiS to put straight—when
they arrive. Or some absolutely in-
significant occurrence or person receives
ten words (extended, of course, by the local
cable-liar as desired), and he, she, or it is
then mixed up with the “latest develop-
ment ” or “scare ”of the European situa-
tion, or gets sandwiched in between a big
battle and the Chinese question. The sooner
the improvement you speak of comes along,
the better everyone—bar, perhaps, Gwen-
doline May’s and Augustus John’s “people”
—will be pleased, and the Recording Angel
will get a spell.
About the meanest thing recorded this
year is the agitation of S.A. ultraqjietists to
deprive the poor wretches in gaol—some for life—
of their supply of Xmas beer. The meanness of
the proceeding is manifest when it is considered
that a large number of S.A. gaol-birds consist of
people who were local-preachers and piety-mon-
gers before they were found out, and who enjoyed
their little brown drink on the quiet just as much
as their more fortunate brethren who haven’t been
found out yet.
At Wellington (M.L.), Mrs. M‘William
has just been gaoled 'for seven years for shooting
promiscuously at Mrs. Dick, an in a
fashionable drapery shop. The cause of the
trouble isn’t very clear, but the manner of it was
full of miscalculation, and shows how lovely
woman rushes at a conclusion without due evi-
dence. When Mrs. M‘William fired at Mrs.
Dick the latter bolted through a door, and sat
down on the floor behind it to keep it shut. Mrs.
M‘William reckoned that her antagonist was
standing up on the other side of the door, and
emptied her revolver through the upper part of it
with a view of plugging her in the head; so,
naturally, she missed. A more logical person
would have aimed low, with a view to blowing
Mrs Dick’s legs from under her if she was stand-
ing up, or hitting her promiscuously if she was
crouching down. As the enemy couldn’t be sitting
on the ceiling, there were two chances in firing
low and only one in firing high. But the unreason-
ing shootist fired high, and the result is that Mrs.
Dick is uninjured, while Mrs. M'Williain is doing
seven years.
* * * *
People in Victoria still remember a
J.P. who, not so very long ago, issued the cruel,
creepy order that the body of a suicide should be
taken out and buried with obloquy at dead of
night after the manner of the olden time. His
own wife committed suicide a few days ago. Thus
does hardshell piety come home to roost.

One recent hot day, the other week, there
were awful explosions in a well-known Melbourne
pub. Four bottles of alleged whisky burst on one
of the shelves behind the bar. Now, what the
blazes could have been in these bottles ! '
* * * *
A person of the hair-dressing persuasion,
at present under a cloud down South, is the sub-
ject of a quaint story. At the time of the present
trouble he was leading a comparatively blameless
life in a boarding-house. The worst things alleged
against him there were that -he was in arrear with
his board, which may happen to anybody ; and
that he borrowed £3 from another boarder, a sort
of thing that the other boarder is always liable to.
He seems also to have alleged that he was coming
into a fortune left him by an uncle in America,
but there was nothing criminal in that. In almost
every boarding-house there is a harmlessly-inflated
individual who is coming into a fortune shortly,
and generally he gets twice as much gravy and a
much less skinny chop than any other boarder.
Anyhow, accused became engaged to a nice girl
who also boarded at the emporium, and 250 people
were invited to a dance by way of celebration be-
fore she heard of certain alleged past doings of
his and threw him over. He had to seek a new
hash-turret, but a night or two later a message
came to the old establishment that Mr. Blank
MASHED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We meet and we pass—not a word—
At the turn of the street;
The beat of my heart she has heard,
’Mid the patter of feet,
And I’ll swear that her own heart has stirred ;
But the red lips are firm—not a word
As we pass in the street.
Nine o’clock every morn, she is there
At the turn of the street,
A halo of bonny brown hair,
Such dainty wee feet,
With a figure of shapeliness rare ;
And I smile and I nod—or I stare
As we pass in the street.
Some morn—should I speak to her there
At the turn of the street ?
(There are days when all things I would dare
For a treasure so sweet:
To fondle that bonny bright hair,
To kiss those two rich lips and rare,
Far away from the street.)
She would pass as before—that I know—
At the turn of the street,
And her eyes with contempt for me glow,
Who would mash—in the street.
And I think if she didn’t mean “ No,”
I might not be mashed on her —so
Let us pass in the street.
’io.
L. M. D.

DREAMS AND SHADOWS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
N.S.W.
In the clear air the blue-plains raise
Their branches meeting never.
In the dim path below them dark
Dream-shadows clasp for ever.
Our waking lives stand far apart,
Ne’er touching, scarcely greeting—
In shadowy dreams ’tis heart to heart,
One long, sweet, endless meeting.

“Vinegar” writes:—
One mm and a-half attended the last woman’s-franchise
meeting, Brisbane. The half was a fine specimen of the
inconstant sex, but tucked his feet under his chair when
cockroaches were about and wore a large buttonhole of
bleeding heart. The attendance of cockroaches was
encouraging. “The brute!” members whispered when
the man planked his foot on an insect. “Silly ass!”
they murmured when the half gathered his feet up on
the chair to dodge a beetle of unusual dimensions. There
were delightful possibilities in the scramble down the
dark Trades Hall stairs, but the nasty one and a-half fled
like frightened deer, and none of the suffragists overtook
them in time to get hugged on the stairway and then
scream for help.
* * « *
“ Malle ” : Walking through Hyde Park,
Sydney, this morning I took stock of Reid’s
Christmas-dinner hustlers. At one tree-enclosure
there were two 20-year-olds, one squatting down
with paint-brush poised against the railings ready
to paint in frantic haste if an inspector happened
along; the other lying on his side admiring the
painting he hadn’t done—both yarning in a dull
kind of way about a dog or a bloke. Further on,
a grey-haired, palsied ancient was cutting grass
with an adze, dead earnestness and frenzied
endeavor to faithfully earn his ss. in every line of
his wrinkled face and every motion of his worn-
out limbs. At a seat near; a strong-built, middle-
aged man was smoking a briar and working
stolidly; he’d calculated how much work ss. is
worth, and intended to do it.

A firm at Toronto (Canada) sends out a
circular announcing that the gospel of St. Matthew
in broad Scotch is now ready and that orders
are being received for the broad Scotch New
Testament. A few verses from the gospel of St.
Matthew in broad Scotch :
“ Whan ye wad do a gude wark, lat yere left haun no
jalouse what yere richt haun is thrang wi’.”
“ Rhyme-na things ower and ower, incontinent, like the
heathen folk : for they trow that gin they speak eneuch,
they sal be heard.”
“ But ye, whan ye fast, snod yere heid, and freshen
yere face, that ye be-na seen by men to be fastin.”
“ And anent cleedin; why soud ye hae sic cark and
care ? Look weel at the lilies o' the lea, how they growe.;
they toil-na nor spin ; yet say I, that Solomon, in a’ his
glorie, wasna buskit braw like ane o’ thae.”
“ Melos ” : The Pres-
byterian Mission people are
talking of yet another
“ Dayspring ’’ for the Gaws-
pil-trade; coin of course
to be dragged out of unwil-
ling Sunday-school kids at
“ ’Ome. Previous “ Day-
springs ” have fared ill. No.
1 was lost; No. 2 was sold,
probably because she cost too
much to keep going ; and
No. 3 piled herself up on a
New Guinea reef, second voy-
age. At present the mission
stations are attended to by an
ordinary mercantile hooker,
but the missionaries complain
that this involves insufficient
accommodation for female
native passengers. True it is
that all native passengers, male
and female, are berthed toge-
ther, but no instance of resuit-
ant impropriety has ever come
within the writer’s ken, which
is more than could be said
were so-called Christians in
same circumstances. In fact,
the average South Sea Islander
is honest arid straight until he
gets Christianity and civilisation
pumped into him, though then,
if he doesn’t beat the white
man on his own ground
\at thieving and lying,
it’s not for want of
effort.

Some members of a
travelling show com-
pany were staying at
a M.L. hotel where
the comely Scotch
waitress was of a
morose disposition and
wholly devoid of mirth.
Many unavailing attempts
were made to bring a smile
to her face. But one day,
when at table, one of the
company made a slip with
the carving knife while saw-
ing the big loaf, and the hitherto
smileless one exploded into fits of laughter.
When sufficiently recovered, she explained that
she “ thocht the mon had cut his thoomb aff ! ”
Scawtch humor with a vengeance 1
* * * *
The Adelaide Chows came out in philan-
thropic guise recently, but they didn’t fill the
character any too well. To help along a charity
carnival prominent Celestials undertook to pro-
vide a Chinese procession in costume, the charity
committee paying all expenses. The “procesh”
was .a most painful and humorless affair, but
John’s little bill was a distinct success. The great
aggregate John demanded =6150 for the costs and
expenses of his procession. Nearly every heathen
connected with the affair was down for a rotund
salary, and there were also items like: “Cabs,
<£l3 155.,” and “dinners and refreshments,
.£33 10s.” A huge dragon was imported from
Ballarat, and the committee nearly burst with
indignation upon discovering that each of the
numerous legs of that monster was drawing a
bloated salary of <£2 a week. But John said “no
savee ” to all protests, and after much wrangling
he got <£loo for proceshing with his dragon in the
alleged interests of charity.
* * * *
“S.”: The grand jury that sat to consider
the case against the Melb. detectives charged
with conspiring to commit a crime looked like a
selection of choice specimens from a Benevolent
Asylum. The grand jurymen were nearly all old
and mostly deaf and lame and otherwise afflicted,
and one or two, when asked their names and dates,
replied that it was, indeed, and one said “Yes,
so long as the rain keeps off. ” This is the third
grand jury that has sat in Melbourne, and there is
a local impression that a grand juryman must be a
grandfather.
“ School-ma’am ” writes :
Re “Dominie’s” careful avoidance of being alone with
a female pupil. What sort of an influence would “ Domi-
nie ” exercise over the girls of his school, he regarding
each of them as a modern Messalina, Delilah, or Potiphar’s
wife ? The usual bush-girl of a school-age has such an
abundance of reverence for “the schoolmaster” as to
make her painfully constrained in his presence. I think
that “ Dominie ” would be quite safe alone with at least
999 out of 1000 bush-schoolgirls. Besides, the “big”
girls often leave school when a man-teacher arrives, and
it is not out of any over-strained solicitude for the young
man’s moral health either ! But, perhaps, “ Dominie
has a wholesome doubt of his own manners, when with a
pretty girl in her teens in a lonely school-house. If so,
he’s a wise man. Moreover, the Public Service Board
would do well to appoint only girl-teachers to mixed
schools where only one teacher is required. I never heard
of a female teacher taking any precautions for her own
moral safety (whatever she might do to avoid scandal)
against the big boys of the school, and yet they are
brought up under the same home-conditions as the girls.

“ W.1.A.” : Was once in a Melb. arith-
metic competition for boys under 14; prize £5;
two trials, oral and written. A lad of a well-
known turfy Hebrew family (now a layer of the
odds himself), dead-heated with me in the oral
exam, for first place; and, fancying my chance
for the written exam, (to take place some days
later), approached me to “ cut up the fiver ” with
him. Now, what show has a Gentile of 13 with a
simple Yid of like age ?
The rumor of a city railway extension
through old Redfern cemetery has induced many
people to unearth and transplant the bones of
their friends. At one of these cheerful ceremonies
a little while ago, where a large family, long-
buried, was being shovelled forth for removal to
Rookwood, there came to the surface a skull to
which was attached a phenomenally long thick
plait 'of golden hair. Strange to say, it shone
almost as brightly as if it hadn’t been among the
worms for a generation. Some lugubrious bard
should write poetry about that hair.
* * * *
The Sydney clergy, as seen by “Jack
Shay ”: —
I have been making a study lately of the religious pro-
fession in Sydney. The R.C. clergy are mostly brawny
and well-fed-looking, but they are nearly all quite “ out
of condition,” and very few have the kind of face which
painters usually give to absolute saints. The Anglican
churchmen are mixed in size and condition, but the
majority have more of the look that the vigorous fasting
and penances of the R.C. Church would theoretically
induce among the priesthood—the sort of look that Card.
Moran himself has, for instance. The Dissenting clergy, as
a rule, are a narrow, weedy lot, lack of stamina their
predominant characteristic. They mostly look like men
who eat a lot of food that does them no good. The best
crowd are the leading lights of the Salvation Army. The
majority are not only big in bulk, but they possess in
general that sense of bigness which shows itself in
feature, voice, and gesture. I have heard snufflers and
whiners, droners and lispers, in all the other clerical
classes, but never yet one among the Army captains.
They are blatant, raucous bellowers or loud, sonorous
preachers, but never a taint of weakness or effeminacy in
a man of them. I can understand the hold the Army
has got on the large semi-illiterate mass, which is the
biggest factor in averaging our Democracy. The Army
preachers look well-fed, well-clothed, well-trained soldiers,
veritable workers in the vineyard, and their selection tells
of more than blind religious fervor on the part of the
Booth hierarchy.

That rather bumptious person, Salmon,
M.P. (Vic.), made an attack on the girls of the
telephone exchange in Parliament the other night.
He complained that they did not say sir and
“ please ” when he rang-up, and accused them of
want of civility. “ What some peopiewant
interjected Trenwith, “is not civility but ser-
vility.” Salmon says the Sydney phone girls
always said “ sir ”to him. Salmon is the sort of
man who is always liable to have sir said to
him in a city where he isn’t known.
In a recent Sydney divorce-case petitioner
deposed that for 12 years he had lived happily
with his spouse in the bush, before the spouse
grew tired and thirsted after civilisation. lo
gratify her, he then took a cottage nigh unto the
haunts of men, but when he returned suddenly
from a journey he found a man haunting it, so he
promptly stoushed the intruder, sat down on him,
and calmly waited till Providence should send a
policeman along. Would have sitting there
still had another man not come that way and
given him the captives name. Husband asked
for decree nisi and got it.
This was pretty well as it should be, but in a
remote M.L. township writer once got mixed up
in a shocking example of how things should not
be On that occasion hubby came home, found
a stranger hugging his wife, and waded in for
slaughter. But half a minute later the mangled
remains of hubby shot through the door and made
a bee line for the railway-station with the intruder
in chase. Hubby rushed into the office and writer
locked the door and met pursuer at the parcel-
counter with an enquiry if he wan ted any thing.
“Yes I want the cove inside. Gant nave
him”’ “I will have him.” “Better come and
take him then.” “ Right! ’’-and he came flying
over the counter and got his nose broken by a
blow from a blackwood ruler. As he landed,
writer caught him round the knees and threw him
on top of a bull-pup which bit him in three
places; but, seizing the b.p. by the hind legs
assailant knocked writer down with it, then got
hold of fugitive and bull-pupped him till pup was
dead, and hubby all but. Then started on writer,
poured all the ink in the station over him, painted
the walls with him, and was trying to push him
into the stove when the porter rushed to the
rescue. Then he threw writer at porter, knocked
latter down, and was dancing on him when the
woman appeared and took him away. They the
fugitive, the porter, and writer—cautiously crawled
over to the pub. and were put to bed.

From the Mooki :
The country out here is as bad as I’ve ever seen country
look anywhere ; burnt-up, brown, bare plains with a few
dead thistles scattered about; and the surviving stock
look as thirsty as the first inmate of Sheol ought to be by
now A lot of stock travelling—by rail, at “starvation
rates,” though. No “ Olanc.y ” business hereabouts. If
the drought lasts anv longer there won’t be any grass to
truck cattle to. The “ bullockies ” suffer more perhaps,
than the big men or the cockies. Driving famishing
bullocks about the country, fossicking for “feed where
none exists, cursed at and hunted by every squatter and
cockie—it’s time to give up punching oxen.
Bishop Thornton, of
Ballarat, has let go in his
old age at a portion of
our reptile contemporary,
the Australian
country press.
“The country
press in some
places,” he said,
“ was contempt-
ible, if no more. It
was little else than black-
/ 4 mail levied on residents jby
incompetent advertisers.” Which
bitter speech is a reminder that some
years ago a sensitive resident in N.S.W.
back country requested the local newspaper
man to refrain from throwing his painful
journal through the window, and promised to pay
the subscription regularly, provided the paper was
kept off the premises. The newspaper man has
since sent his quarterly account and been duly
paid, and the arrangement is likely to last till one
or other -dies.

“Face”: Was passing a big Sydney
school, recently, when about 80 boys sallied out
and opened fire on au aged vegetable-chow with
hunks of bread, mud, &c. Was surprised to notice
that the leader of the attacking party was a young
chow of about 16, who bombarded his countryman
with great precision and yelled with joy when he
was hit.
WHEN LILAC BLOOMS.
When Lilac blooms what whir of wing 1
Through hawthorn boughs what gambolling 1
And in sweet peas and mignonette
On window-ledges quaintly set,
What hum of bees ! what murmuring 1
And as the lindens sway and swing,
This slow blood moves with everything ;
The pulses throb with fume and fret
When Lilac blooms !
And old Love stirs, and fain would sing
And revel with the lusty Spring-
Old Love who lingers with me yet
In this strange garden of regret—
Alas ! too late his wakening
When Lilac blooms!
Sydney.
DORA WILCOX.
