A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ _ ~ Sydney, December 20, IS9B.
My Deab Myee—
Sydney Philharmonic gave us something new
last week m “Psyche,” a musical presentment of
the naughty girl who wanted to know too much,
ihe opportunities of making a great splash with
this fine old argument will be patent to anyone who
has never tried, but Niels W. Gade, the composer,
has not yet put much inspiration into his work.
Parts 1 and 2 were conspicuously uninteresting
and monotonous. Also, there was a general air
of not being entirely familiar with the work, that
probably came as much from the unacquainted
listener as from the Philharmonic. Things im-
proved when the Society got into the lower world
and Part 3, where the composer worked in some
realistic hints about the noises of Hades. Part 4
was graced with a tenderfully tuneful song for
Eros, but opportunities for yawns were sprinkled
plentifully over the rest of the programme.
Psyche fell to the lot of Miss Hettie Holroyd,
who dressed the damsel in white and interpreted
her songs in a somewhat similar color. In spite
u cr y stal voice there was not much meaning
to be gleaned from her interpretation. The music
seemed a size too large for her. Every one agreed
that Eros was rightly given to Mr. Burns Walker,
who is still the idol of the aesthetic fair. Nor does
rnu throatiness appear to be easing.
That striking-looking songstress, Eva Mylott,
represented Proserpine, but had so little to do that
one can scarcely comment on her. By the way
Melbourne wai highly delighted with Miss
Mylott s singing, lately, and several critics compare
her favorably with Ada Crossley, but she hasn’t
the .velvety richness of Large Ada, who always
reminded some people of a big, soft, purring pussy -
Our other promising- songstress, Florence
Schmidt, hasn’t settled down to anything yet. At
present she is in Milan hearing things. After
.Xmas she goes to Paris, which will probably
mean Marchesi, though nobody says so. Alva
turned up at Milan lately, and the two sopranos
had tea and talk together, although their opinions
°f Australian musical taste and its treatment of
artists must have been widely different.
J had » look at Mr. Longstaff’s latest work the
other day. It is a portrait of Ed ward Knox, executed
to the commission of the Union Club. If anyone
wants to see a remarkable delineation of a bald
forehead with a glisten of flesh so lifelike that you
can t believe you’re not looking at the gentleman
himself -but I don’t know whether anyone can
see the picture, as the Union Club hangs it up in
the privacy of its rooms next week. A weakness
for this same artist’s “Lady in Black ” impels me
to express my pleasure that this sweet, adorable
vision has returned from the Eadith Walker Ex-
hibition, and will hang again in our Gallery soon.
® on t you think that those thin, beautifully ner-
vous hands, and all the suggestions of a fascinat-
lnf?> kindly, witty woman of over thirty, are worth
1111^ 101 ! cow - e y ed and almond-eyed beauties
after Dash and Dash. And yet, because the cow-
eyes don’t yearn at you from this canvas, and
because the mouth goes over the beauty limit, and
th© figure is indubitably thin, you will hoar any
day at the Art Gallery, “ Fancy anyone painting
a woman like that 1 ”
A packed house welcomed back the dark, de-
lightful Jubilee singers at the Palace Theatre on
baturday. Mattie Allan, now Madam Mattie
Allan Orpheus McAdoo, is evidently going to be
as well-beloved by the populace as ever. She was
warmly received, and all the flowers went her way.
One couldn’t but admire the artlessness with which
the little family history of Mr. and Mrs. Orpheus
was depicted on the back of the playbill. A por-
trait of Mrs. Orpheus appeared on one side of the
programme, one of Mr. Orpheus on the other, and
U P 111 the corner of his proud father’s portrait was
a dear little darkie in a sailor hat, aged about two.
It reminded one of the little girls who rush about
excitedly with the great news, “ We’ve got a new
baby.” Unfortunately Orpheus was ill in Mel-
bourne and unable to appear on Saturday.
You wouldn’t expect much frocking at a show
so much patronised by the religious bodies, but
Mattie Allan sported two Frenchy frocks with a
piquant swagger that didn’t seem in the least
a / “? ,ld °f what the “Sunday schools would say.”
(Of course you know that’s a quote from a certain
Australian publishing firm when dubious about
accepting a book decorated with asterisks and
little straight lines.) Her first frock was a high-
necked mauve silk with broad white stripes,
draped with an apron of thick white silk lace.
All the co. changed frocks for the second part of
the programme, and Mrs. McAdoo came out in a
festive black net sprinkled with big white burrs.
This was made over a green silk slip, and the
bodice. was turned down with jewelled revers of
dull pink. The lady described as black Melba
rather accentuated herself here with black-and-
white. stripes, and sleeves that looked as if they
were intended to wear with boots. The beaming
faces of the co. and their mincing, mouthy, im
genuous little attempts at putting on frill, give
you the idea that they are all aged about seven,
and have dressed up for the night in their mothers’
frocks.
A gratifyingly noiseless performance is the Cine-
matograph display, which shows how Fregoli,
Biondi, and others manage their quick-change
business. The pictures—which, by the way, dance
just as hard and aggravatingly as ever, and can
only be kept steady whenyou peer at them through
half-closed eyes—let you into the great Fregoli’s
dressing-room, and you see the four valets waiting
at different doors to pluck off and cast on the
actor’s garb. Everything is done like lightning,
but without flurry. One man’s business is just to
pick up the clothes Fregoli flings on the floor.
Also, you see that Biondi has slavishly copied his
master. From what I saw of the elder man’s busi-
ness, there seemed to be nothing left for the
younger one to originate. In fact, I shouldn’t
think Biondi would be particularly pleased with
this give-away of his biz.
I* l. o°ks 0 °ks as if Xmas weather was going to favor
Pudding and beef-eaters this year. The cruelty of
the average Xmas stuffing doesn’t need dilating
on, and it isn’t strange that the first movement
agamst Xmas comes from the sex that swelters
over colonial ovens for half the chief day of the
Christian year. The Woman’s College leads off
by keeping up Xmas in the middle of June, with
roaring logs and brandified puddings and a Xmas
tree and a dance. People sneer at this Institution
and say it’s useless and unpractical, but there you
are!
Ihe shops have all their best bravery out this
week, and the limited influx of country cousins is
the only deterrent to a bright shopping Xmas. I
looked round at various shops, and was struck
with the constancy with which different houses
keep their reputation for one particular line of
goods. For instance, David Jones for lingerie,
fichus, scarves, Webb’s for veils, Waters for gloves,
and so on. “Davy” Jones is showing some
dressy skirts that must commend themselves to
anyone who wants something nice in a hurry.
One in particular was soft white silk, billowy with
myriads of val.-edged flounces. These ran up to
the waist at the back, leaving a brief apron striped
with val. lace in front. The bodice accom-
panies the skirt, but isn’t made-up. Other smart
white skirts are of simple-looking silks, trimmed
with chantilly lace until they become worthy of
race-meetings and garden-parties. The same firm
is showing some delightful things in linen and
lace and embroidery, such as tea cloths, pillow
shams, table-cloths. And I must not forget that
alluring case oi baby clothes, which would turn
any toddler into a fairy queen at a moment’s
notice. Ihe latest baby fashion does away with
capes and pelisses, and originates a sort of silk
redingote with loose little sleeves that slip on over
long or short clothes.
To describe Anthony Hordern’s is a large order,
rather like describing all the other shops in one
breath. Country and suburban people arrive at
eight m the morning, bring their lunch along, and
spend the day inside the emporium walls. On the
whole, I think the toys are the special feature
here. A huge underground hall is crammed from
floor to ceiling with herds of fiery little rocking-
horses, nations of male and female dolls, cities of
dolls houses, butcher’s shops, baker’s shops,
the commonplace passing right out of them in
these miniattire replicas. The book counters are
also marvellous, and when you see the works of
good writers, modern as well as classical, well
bound and printed, selling at a shilling a vol., you
wonder where the publishers come in, to say
nothing of the authors. Although they are not
expected to come in at all.
For every sort of dainty frippery, scarves, veils,
belts and smart millinery, Webb’s is well worth
visiting. You get the prettiest muslins in the city
there. Waters’ is also gaily stocked with feminine
fancies, silk blouses, ties, a line of Irish goods that
are not to be found everywhere, and all the
smartest things in ribbons. Pitt-street Hordern’s
Xmas speciality appears to extend to all depart-
ments, a line of popular horizontally-striped silks
being specially commendable.
Jewellers are also putting a Xmas shine on their
plate-glass windows. Stewart Dawson’s establish-
ment always sets forth its good things attractively,
the windows at present revealing one temptation
after another—to say nothing of long-felt wants in
the shape of household-plate and personal jewel-
lery. And here, to baffled men who want to make
a seasonable present to their women-kind, let me
whisper muff-chains and charms—many charms.
1 heGoldsmiths’andSilversmiths’Alliance abounds
in thase fancies, from the tiny gold doll that came in
with La Poupee to microscopic elephants. Also, you
will find here sparkling Parisian-diamonds, and
dainty be-diamonded aigrettes within the reach of
moderate purses, as well as all the up-to-date
jewellery stock. Saunders and Co., at the Hay-
market, captivate country cousins with huge
assortments of faithful Waterburys. Their win-
dows glitter with all the table equipments that
make glad the heart of the housewife, and they
are equal to any run on chains, rings, brooches,
and so on. Fairfax and Roberts put forward all
manner of high-class jewellery, some special lines
of purses, and beautiful table silver, until fanciful
people, who can t find Xmas presents anywhere
else, must certainly be satisfied here.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Janet Lady Clarke wore black and dia.
monds, while daughter Mary aired her crim.
son pelisse, at the farewell function tendered
them by Austral Salonites on Dec. 15th.
Mrs. Evelyn Gough, spokeslady for the club,
voiced members’ esteem for their departing vice-
president and their unalloyed regret, &c. The
guest of the evening replied in pleasant, unaffected
strain. Unlike Blunderbuss Brassey at Cliveden,
t’other night, she refrained from alluding to
Salonites as dear good creatures, earning tea and
toast by their own struggles and perspiration, or
anything to that effect. Said gubernatorial utter-
ances, by the way, provoked to indignant bridling
certain libelled ladies of leisure among his hearers.
Hard luck, after joining an association with a
view to being considered ‘in society,’ to find
you’ve only made yourself liable to be patted on
the head as a worthy wage-winner and a promising
inky toiler, deserving of encouragement from your
Betters.
“Re dainty do-nothings : Alfred Moule, in his
Melbourne days, was prouder of his white hands
than of his music. Often when a singing pupil
blundered in for her lesson without knocking she
surprised her teacher m the act of gloating over
his outspread lily-white paws. Then he promptly
accused the intruder of chilblains or hang-nails,
and wound up by saying, ‘ One can’t have decent
hands without care. I, for instance, have never
cut a slice of bread-and-butter for myself in my
life ! ’ J
“Toilers or loafers, Salonites, as a class, look
physically fitted to fight the battle of life. Such
massive arms as dot the landscape on full-dress
evenings. Sarah Bernhardt, in her Melb. appear-
ances as Cleopatra, habitually strolled into view,
arms supported by slaves on either side. Not that
hers appeared over-weighty. But at Salon func-
tions most of the short-sleeved dowagers on show
could do comfortably with a little side-wing por-
terage.
. 11 At that farewell reception the Salon programme
included some rollicking recitations by Sophie
Osmond, an elocutionist bound to please so long as
she refrains from the weird.
‘ ‘ Miss Florence Stanbridge, Daylesford’s orphan
heiress, is revisiting her native Australia after
three years European travel, winding up with pre-
sentation at Court. This'lucky possessor of £6OOO
per annum is a tall, graceful 20-year-old, with dark
hair and eyes, clear - cut features, creamy com-
plexion, and sullen scarlet mouth, in fascinating
contrast to the rest of her personality. ’Tis said
that a blue-blooded English lad, a year older than
the handsome heiress, has let no grass grow under
his feet, and is shortly to follow the young lady
and her guardians to Australia, with the sanction
of all concerned.
“ The other night, when the Brasseys were
being driven along Swanston-street to a function
or a fete, a rigid, angular female with wild hair,
driving a perky but emaciated pony in a battered
part, chipped in between the jangling aide and the
vice-regal caravan, and maintained the position to
the delirious joy of street-arabs and disloyal pedes-
trians. The beautiful military person on the gor-
geous horse piloted the way, blissfully unconscious
of the indignity that was being put upon the vice-
regal institution, and the rigid female, and the
wild-haired pony, and the cart, jogged along at his
heels, whilst Governor Brassey and party hung fire
at the tail of the procession.
“On the ‘Geisha’s ’ first night (Saturday, Dec.
17th), Janet Lady Clarke’s big farewell theatre-
party filled the centre of the Princess d.c., except
the small frontage block booked by ‘Govment
’Ouse.’ Lady Brassey, in unobtrusive black,
brought with her Mrs. Vallange, who wore a white
frock under her lilac-brocade pelisse, while her
Jap. top-knot roosted as high as ever. In fact,
for evening wear, the more-or-less-elevated coiffure
quite holds its own. Aides Nevill and Cecil sat
together, bored of aspect, and only very occasion-
ally smiled with difficulty at the fooling of Chin-
Chin Chinaman Lauri.
“Mr. Cecil, a scion of the Salisbury clan, still
goes on crutches in consequence of his ’board-ship
accident. Throughout the Cup gaieties he was
bed-ridden at Surgeon Fitzgerald’s hospital, where
Lady Brassey visited him daily before starting for
Flemington. His sunburnt, boyish, and, at pre-
sent, anxious face, seen en profile, is not unlike
Mr. Vallange’s, though less irregularly featured.
“The hostess of the Cliveden party, in black
decolletage, wore a slightly-worried air re a dia-
mond trinket supposed to have been lost en route.
All round sat kith and kin—Snodgrasses, Hughes,
Ryans, Dr. Rowans, Cruickshanks, Falconer-
Macdonalds, etc. Also, Frank-Stephens and
ramifications of the Madden clan, for the Chief-
Justice’s clique and the Cliveden-Clarkes are
sworn allies.
“ The departing aide, Captain Frewen, the
freshest and most cheery-faced of the Brassey staff,
sat with the Cliveden contingent. Somehow he
often arrives ‘on his own’at functions, when all
the vice-regal chairs are appropriated, and either
stands apologetically on one foot while ‘Lord
Richard ’ re-hashes the party, or else perches afar
off with outside acquaintances. Now he goes to
rejoin his regiment.
“Stage Geisha-land is a paradise of beauty.
Hard to decide which of its scenes one would
rather wake up in after death—that tea-house
mantled with mauve wistaria and approached by
bamboo bridge spanning a lake spangled with
water-lilies, or the palace gardens gorgeous with
chrysanthemums. And there’s something ecstatic
about the clothes, too. They’re too radiant for
this work-a-day world. Such warm, appealing
pinks, soothing greens, and blues both bright and
delicate.
“Florence Perry, the imported Geisha, is not
‘beautiful as a butterfly,’ though an attractive
warbler. The satin groundwork of her first gold-
embroidered robe is delicious pinky-heliotrope. In
her second costume, pale blue predominates,
heightened by brilliant pink blossoms tucked into
the wearer’s raven locks. Rose Musgrove, as a
French interpretress in Jap dress, wears soft pink
Canton crepe to begin with, strewn with scattered
bamboo sprays in faint brown. Tall, fair, pretty,
vivacious and tuneful—the gay Juliette is suf-
ficiently cocksure and nipperish to excuse Marquis
Vernon’s obstinacy in not taking any till he can’t
help himself.
“Dolly Vane, as a touring English Miss, affects
white serge Russian jacket and skirt, peppered
with small buttons by way of trimming ; even the
placket-hole buttons outside. First-nights seem
flurrying. Miss Vane, re-appearing in the frock
just described, had evidently whisked it off in the
meantime, popped on as many Jappy things as
possible, in readiness for future scenes, and hustled
the serge on top hardly in time for her cue. She
caine on struggling with jacket buttons. Mrs.
Bracy sneaked one fastening for her, but there
were still gaps. One can’t blame either lady for
finding no plausible explanation of ma’mzelle’s
dishabille to offer. If the place had been a public
bath, or a dressmaker’s ! But it was ‘ the house of
ten thousand joys,’ given over to geishas, British
naval flirts, and bad Jap. ’ristoerats, and the young
female foreign devil had been warned off the grass.
So what reason could one give for undress?
“ Gov. Brassey allowed the unsatisfactory Capt.
Frewen to stay long enough to join in the annual
romps of the State-school children at Govt. House
before packing him back to England. It’s about
the only fun he has seen. The other aides have
been carried around the stations and allowed to
get glimpses of real Australian life and see kanga-
roos and things ; but this one, perhaps on account
of his youth and want of push, has been shut up
within the gates. Frewen is sent back under the
wing of Janet Lady Clarke, so it is to be hoped
she will find a means to soothe his wounded feelings
and make the way pleasant for him as far as they
go together. Brassey’s desire in aides seems to be
something of an unknown quantity. He got rid
of Capt. Wallington because he took too much
upon himself, and it is understood that Frewen
goes because he took too little.
“ Baronet Rupert Clarke is back in Melbourne
after his trip to India. He has left his moustache
behind him. He now looks like an actor, of the
usual clean-shaven variety—a resemblance which
will probably tend to greatly please Mrs. Baronet.
Also baldness is creeping stealthily over Srupert’s

“H. H. Champion’s marriage with Miss Elsie
Goldstein—gone through in the bride’s family’s
parlor, with only the bride’s nearest friends for
witnesses, with condensed nuptial-service, per-
formed by Dr. Chas. Strong in a few minutes—
must, in its simplicity at least, have presented one
feature in common wi h that first marriage of his.
Long ago, in his ardent youth, Mr. Champion, as
the exponent of Socialism, sought to bridge over
the gap between ‘ the classes ’ and ‘ the masses ’ by
stepping straight from Mayfair to find a bride in
the East End. Rumor has been pretty silent as to
the result, probably because there was nothing ex-
citing or unusual to say. It is possibly this atmos-
phere of romance clinging to his past that has
made H. H. Champion, Socialist, with his some-
what cold exterior, the idol of so many different
specimens of the New Woman.

“The heroine of above midnight adventure is a
large, commonsensical dowager, conspicuous afc
evening entertainments by her sartorial souvenirs
of a recent tour —lace scarves worn cap-wise, Orien-

Melb. first of all for Mrs. B.’s health, now com-

was designed for her convenience.
“Lieutenant Lilley, who has been so curiously
jobbed into an artillery commission by the
Imperial swashbuckling crowd in Melb., is a tall,
dark youth with London Journal legs and a
penchant for wearing a straw hat several sizes too
small for him—though, to do him justice, any hat
would be too small for him. He originally in-
tended going for a commission in the British army,
but the examinations disagreed with him and ho
was left to get into a common kawlonial appoint-
ment by a side door. Lilley is a grandson of old
Ben Williams, at one time member for Beech-
worth in the Vic. Assembly. Williams was one
of the most popular men in the district in the
old days, and there was never a drover or a digger
within 50 miles but swore by his whisky.

There still lives in Melbourne a man who,
when he married, advertised his fair Nora Kath-
leen as “ the last surviving descendant of Brian
Boru.”
A Northern medico the other day at-
tempted suicide. Took prussic acid, injected
morphia, hacked himself with a knife, and—
recovered. Yet a common Chow can unerringly
do the job with his pig-tail.
“Sammy” Grimes has just completed his
20th year as representative of Oxley, in the
Queensland Parliament. Foxton has represented
Carnarvon for 15 years, and Groom has done 31
years as member for Toowoomba.
Kindly Judge Noel is leaving the Queens-
land bench to plunge into the arena of politics.
Will stand as a Liberal Democrat, and intends
Sit is understood) to have a try for Cairns. The
fudge has many well-wishers all over Bananaland.

A well-known Sydney stock and station expert a

Wait till they come to realise.”
Australasian Premiers who went to England
as Agent-Generals and returned to local politics
to cut more or less insignificant figures for the
rest of their careers: Cowper, Forster, Berry, Vogel
and Gillies. John Douglas, of Queensland, became
Premier after having filled the A.G. office, but it
was only a flash in the pan. His best Parlia-
mentary work was done before, in the Macalister
and Lilley Ministries.
Was Thurston, Lora High Commissioner
of the South Pacific, a Machiavellian trickster, or
a man among men ? Anyhow, even his enemie
admit the tremendousness of his personality.
At a public meeting at Levuka, in the early
days of expediency and experi-
ment, when the hall was
thronged with the men who
sought vainly to “ down ”
him, Thurston unctuously
thanked his foes for his
advancement from the
boatswain’s berth to a
vice - regal commission.
Then, turning to his little
knot of supporters, he
assured them, in curt,
Caesarean terms, that he
ewed them no thanks, and
would be double d d
before he would give them
any. Nevertheless, a chief
of high native rank ha
been known to remark, after
a severe wigging from the autocrat of the
South Pacific, and the scant entertain-
ment of a cup of tea and a bad cigar,
that the shell-back emperor of the Fijis
hinted to him that he would not disdain a
few turtle. And they were promptly sent!
A pathetic Melb. sight is the poor old
Scotchman who perambulates the streets in a cos-
tume crossed between a Highlander’s and a barn-
storming theatrical super’s. The old man was a
soldier in a Highland regiment, and as he served
with gallantry through Crimean campaign and
Indian Mutiny he is fired out by his grateful
country to hustle for a charitable crust.
When witty old Vincent Pyke was elected
by a narrow majority to Maoriland House of Re-
presentatives, he was twitted by his defeated oppo-
nent with having polled “ votes from the ceme-
tery.” “ This assertion of my naturally-aggrieved
friend may or may not be true,” said Pyke in
reply ; “ but if it is true, it only goes to show that
even the dead rose in judgment against my oppo-
nent. ”
The vitriolic j. H. Finlayson, editor and
part proprietor of S.A. Register, and Premier
Kingston’s oldest journalistic enemy, returned to
Adelaide the other day. “Finny” has aged
much since he departed two years ago, but there
is a kick in the old horse yet. Starting as office-
boy, Finlayson, who is much liked by young jour-
nalists, has been editor of the Register for over
20 years.
Mrs. Ellen M‘Nabb, the young Footscray
(Vic.) mother who poisoned her baby after being
jointly and severally bashed by sundry male
friends, was found guilty of manslaughter with a
strong recommendation to mercy, and C. -J.
Madden let her off with 12 months hard. The
sympathy of all was with the unfortunate girl,
but, all the same, had she killed the youngster
before it was born she would probably have got
seven years.
Recent Bulletin reference to the late
George McCullagh Reid’s connection
with the founding of Auckland Stab
turns on the reminiscence-tap of a cor-
respondent. Thomson \V. Leys suc-
ceeded Reid in the editorial chair, and
has sat there, working with tireless
energy, for a quarter of a cen-
tury. And if he has worked
hard himself he hasn’t spared /
his staff—always competent
and well - paid. Among
those who have been on
Auckland Star at differ-
ent times are Alfred
Stewart Rathbone, who
founded Auckland Ob-
server, and is now pro-
minent in journalistic
circles in London, and is
believed to write, among
many other things, Syd-
ney D. T’s interesting
“ Personal Notes from
London”; John Black-
man, author of “A Life of
Thomas Day” and much
melodious verse, and who was
a complete guide to Dickens,
whose friendship he enjoyed ;
the versatile C. O. Montrose,
soldier, war correspondent,
and raconteur; J. A. Philp,
well-known on Sydney side ; J. F. Edgar, sub-
editor of Melb. Argus ; J. Liddell Kelly, well-
known to Bulletin writers, and now editor of Wel-
lington Times (M.L.), and others who have made
some sort of mark on their generation. Brett has
kept his paper ahead of all competitors. He was
the first to print with web machinery in Maoriland,
the first to introduce linotypes, and he has the
biggest general printing and bill-posting business
in the province.
George R. Johnson, the Melb. architect
who died on his way back from the west, out-
stripped by far all other Australian designers in
the building of theatres—no less than 15 standing
to his credit. Johnson was a red-headed, reckless
sort of man, with a vein of humor seldom to be
found in his profession. He it was who sent in a
design for the Melb. Commercial Bank competi-
tion with the motto, “ Refer to Drawer.”

Litigants before a certain Australian Dis-
trict Court Judge have no reason to complain of
the law’s delays. His Honor invariably knows all
about the dispute on reading through the plaint—
sometimes even sooner. He can try a case while
a party is getting from the back of the court to
the witness-box. Evidence is a detail which only
helps to delay the trial of the cause ; if the case
should jiroceed after a few questions have been
asked and answered his Honor becomes restive,
and in rapidly succeeding stages irritable and
aggressive, and the refractory litigant is informed
that of course his Honor will hear all the evidence
if he insists on protracting the case. If he is wise,
he doesn’t insist on protracting the case, because
should he do so his Honor gets so very mad that
he has no judicial attitude left.

“ Pick-me-up.”
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa, by taking the place of
stimulants of an ardent nature, has made happy

freshing ; it leads the way among all beverages,
and nothing can equal it

Recently inquiries came from Cape Town
to Westralia for camels and information there-
anent. Geo. Throssell, Minister for Lands, re-
plied as follows : “ I was the first to introduce
camels for carrying purposes on to the goldfields.
Cost me about .£65 per head landed in W.A. ;
about 20 shuffling off this mortal coil immediately
after landing. Ultimately, out of 46, seven sur-
vived to tell the tale. I have never liked the
animal since.”
George Wells—who put down one of the
first eight holes on the famous Black Hill rush at
Ballarat, was the holder of the 20th licence issued
at Mt. Alexander (Castlemaine), and was earlier
still on the White Hills (Bendigo) rush—pegged
out his last claim the other day at Bendigo. Mr.
Wells, who was later in life a director of many
Bendigo cos., was regarded as almost a model
man in his relations with the woiking miners and
other employes.
“ Sheoak ” : Does the Stephen Judgeship,
like Tennyson’s Brook, go on for ever ? Note the
following from a Hobart Town monthly of 1828:
“ There are three persons of one family belonging
to the Sydney Bench, viz., Mr. Judge Stephen,
the father; Mr. Sydney Stephen, son, acting as
Solicitor-General; and Mr. John Stephen, son,
acting as Registrar. The Solicitor-General (Mr.
Alfred Stephen), of Van Diemen’s Land, is also a
son of Mr. Judge Stephen.”

loytrin’in the strate,” he said, sternly. “Sure,
yez never offered to shift when Sir Philip kem by ! ”

During the last half-century Adelaide
Anglicans have had three bishops—Short, 1847;
Kennion, 1882 ; and Harmer, 1895. Three deans
—Farrell, 1849; Russell, 1869; and Marryat,
1887. And four archdeacons —Hale, 1848 ; Wood-
cock, 1858 ; Marryat, 1868 ; and Morse, 1887.
Archdeacon Hale was afterwards respectively first
Bishop of Perth and second Bishop of Brisbane.
In 1853 Dean Marryat, who is a nephew of the
naval novelist, was Chaplain of Darlinghurst and
Cockatoo Island, N.S.W.
“ Dabton ” : The story of Vic. bushranger
Power’s apprehension by Supt. Nicholson, as told
to the warder to whom he was allotted. When
Power was dodging the police in the vicinage of
Euroa he was very ill from an old surgical trouble
and was suffering intense pain ; so to avoid the
ingloriousness of giving himself up he wrote to
Nicholson indicating his hiding-place, and asking
the super, to come and take him. Nicholson’s
version was that he surprised Power asleep. He
got promotion and a reward.
Related by musician Kowalski that during
his Parisian student days he went with a confrere
to look for lodgings. Saw a house they thought
would suit, knocked at the door, and “ze portair
he come and I say, ve vant rooms. ‘Two room,’
I say, * for my friend hez a dogue.’ ‘ And vot it
eez your beezness, zhentlemen ? ’ ‘Ve are musi-
cian,’ I say. So he say, ‘my mastair eez out, but
I tell heem. Vill you call to-morrow?’ Ve go
next day, and ze portair he tell us, ‘ My mastair
say he vill accomodate ze dogue; but ze musician,
—no, nevair ! ’ ”
An appeal is being made to Vic. Parlia-
ment for a pension or annuity for the surviving
pioneer of Melbourne, Mr. Robert Russell, now in
his 91st year and living at Richmond. He arrived
at the future site of Melbourne in 1836 before
Governor Bourke named the place, and before
Surveyor Hod die, who amassed money out of the
surveys, ever saw the spot. Mr. Russell was the
first Commissioner of Crown Lands and principal
surveyor of Port Philip. His original plan of
Melbourne is in the survey office in that city. It
is proposed that Govt, grant a small annuity for
the joint lives of Mr. Russell and his wife.

tralia—dating from time of admission. If Tas-
mania counts, I think this is a mistake. Both
Adye Douglas, of Launceston, and John Roberts,
of Hobart, date from the thirties.
The late “Jimmy” Tyson lies buried in
the Anglican section of the Drayton and Too-
woomba (Q.) cemetery, close to four other old
Darling Downs pioneers—James Taylor, W. B.
Tooth (of Clifton), W. H. Kent (Jondaryan), and
Tyson’s neighbor for many years, William
Hogarth, of Balgownie.
A strange sight was seen in Burketown (Q.)
the other day when a dying Chow, on his last legs
and almost speechless, was carried by a country-
man to the Savings Bank to draw his little all
and thus save probate-duty. He died the same
night. Verily the Chinese do not pay much
towards the Government of the leper-stricken pro-
vince.
“Jimmy” Allison, of sainted memory,
was once running a show, in which the relations
of the sexes didn’t meet with his approval.
“ Never had such a company as this,” said Jimmy.
“There’s one man paired-off with one woman, and
another man paired-off with another woman. In
fact, the whole company is living in a perfect state
of idolatry.”
Journalists are born, not made. A live
freelance pressman, of Sydney, spotted a newly-
deceased horse lying on a suburban road. He
made a paragraph of it with indignant remarks on
the supineness of the local nuisance boss, and sold
the paragraph to a morning paper. Then he went
back and hewed off about 501 b. of horseflesh to
feed his dog with.
S.A. Sheriff Booth by has held office tor 46
years, but he nearly had a sudden stop 20 years
ago when the Gothenburg went down en route
from Port Darwin to Sydney with Judge Wearing
and party, returning from a N. Territory circuit
court. Boothby was to have made the trip, but a
Leg. Council election (for which he was returning-
officer) made it necessary to detain him in
Adelaide. A special Act was passed to appoint a
Deputy Sheriff in Boothby’s place, and the substi-
tute went down with the rest.
The noseless “Nosey Bob” is not the
only disfigured executioner N.S.W. has had. In
the bad old days, when the job meant constant
work, one “Split-Mouth” filled the billet for
nearly ten years for N.S.W. and Norfolk Island.
He had, before his Govt, service began, captured
a pair of bushrangers at Port Macquarie, and in
the struggle had received a cut from a hanger
across the mouth, opening it to the ear. Under
excitement the scar had a peculiar habit of turn-
ing black and blue. Private employers declined
to give him work, so Govt, engaged him as man-
butcher. Many of his victims cried out in sheer
horror at the approach of “ Split-Mouth.”

“ Yooey ” Taylor, once M.P. for Parra-
matta, bore an intolerant hatred to his successful
rival, Dowell O'Reilly ; and his supporters, who
fully shared it, have ever prayed for the downfall
of the usurper of “ Yooey’s rights.” After the
recent Ferris victory, the Tayloriles enthusias-
tically celebrated the occasion. At midnight, a
prominent townsman was asked to go home.
“ Nom-ine,” he said. “ Nom-me must tell
Yooey (hie) ’bout Reilly’s lickin’—Yooey will be
sho pleased ” —and off he went up Church-street.
He was found next morning asleep in the ceme-
tery, on Mr. Taylor’s grave. His fuddled brain
just remembered where he had last seen his old
friend going, and off he had staggered to con-
gratulate him.
Nowhere do managers of big mines get
more miserable pay than in Bendigo. One
of the biggest mines paid £4 a week to the
manager, who in years handled OOOOoz. of
gold from its workings. Eighteen months ago
he put a mythical person on the pay-sheet, and
kept him there until somebody stumbled on a
discovery which has landed the manager in gaol
under an 18 months’ sentence. The poor wretch,
whom the co’s representative said had been
otherwise an excellent officer, mortgaged
his belongings and scraped together £100; and
the co., of course, accepted it, and doesn’t
want him punished very severely. By this time
he and his “large family ” are probably sorry that
he “ worked his way up,” as it- was urged in
his favor he had done. To work up to the manage-
ment of a Bendigo mine seems very like rising to
perdition.

Aboriginalities.
A Big Scrub police ex sergeant, when look-
ing for a prisoner escaped from Twowharves lock-
up, got hopelessly bushed in a lawyer-vine and
lantana patch. At last he was tired of hunting
for the lost track and tried a loud “ coo-ee.” The
call was answered. “Who's there?” “ Sanior-
sarjant Dooley from Twowharves, lukin’ forra
niscaped prisoner. ” There was a long laugh, and
“All right, old man, stay where you are; I’m
tli’ bloke ya lookin’ for. ’ And then there was a
great quantity of silence.

“ Surveyor ” (with 15 years’ experience in
Tasmanian survey-camps)
The man is not yet born, and probably never will be
born, competent to right the wrongs done to Tasmania
by her old survey - system. That system scrambled
over all obstacles for gain, unmindful of the past,
callous as to the future, and left no. trace save chaos
behind. It appointed men who went flying across
country armed with compasses stuck on sticks, link-
chains as heavy as bullock-drags and hardly so accurate,
and meat-choppers in lieu of axes. These warriors of -the
past walked all day without rest on a mouthful of oat-
meal and water, and coiled up in a hollow log with as
much content as other men would on a feather bed. They
took their sights at a venture and dragged the chain at a
hand-gallop. WHat they couldn't measure they guessed,
and their guesses were more wonderful than the stars.
Their most intricate problem was how to get through
the scrub in the swiftest way possible, and the pride
of t-heir profession to rush recklessly down a boundary
creek, noticing the curves that weren’t there in their
field-book. An old sojer, who has been as well cursed as
any man in Australia, rode round his lines with a pocket-
compass in one hand and a meat-chopper in the other,
blazing the trees as he went. Any tree 20 yards on either
side was good enough with him. When he reckoned he
was far enough—by his watch —he would dismount, hew
the top off a sapling and hammer it into the ground with
his meat-axe. This was his corner peg. When he had
Sroceeded thus to the third angle (having previously
ghted a fire at the starting point) he merely made
straight for the smoke, and the survey was complete.
“ There, tna mon,” he would remark to the perspiring
selector who had followed him round, “all the ground
eenside tha fouah cor-runers belangs to yoursel.” One
selector, of writer’s acquaintance, refused to ac-
company the old man round the lines. He did
better than that. He hanged out the signal-fire
and lighted another about ten chains to his own good.
The warrior on horseback arrived at the fire all
right, and observed that it was “ a copital close ! ” This
is not romancing. Scores of similar farces were enacted
in those days, and their results are multiplied all over
the island. The whole country is a net-work of erroneous
lines, and the old charts are pictures of inaccuracy ; good
as the work of the present system may be, it is only in-
creasing the same old errors of the meat-chopper brigade,
and it will go on increasing them until there is no room
to err in. Then the boss surveyor and his assistants will
Stand up on top of the Cradle Mount and inspect poor,
much-cut-up little Tas. —and weep.

“ Werriraee ” : Most bushmen aver that
snakes, no matter at what time killed, will squirm
till sunset. Have never watched for proof, bub
have noticed that the head can be “killed” out-
right, whereas the body, however battered, will
wriggle out the average slayer’s patience. The
tail part wriggles the most. If cut off, it wriggles
more, but will not last as long as the body.
Apropos. Snakes are fond of milk, and a saucer-
full, set adjacent, will bring any snake out from
under a house. Also, the only Australian snake
I have never seen up a tree is the black-backed,
red-bellied variety. All others, so far as my ex-
perience goes, are excellent climbers.

Be Fred. Bradshaw, on pigs (Bulletin,
19/11/’9B). I once knew a cove who, when visiting
his best girl, always used to take an old boar with
him for protection against the dogs. Plan of
campaign was as follows : The cove would make a
feint to the rear of the girl’s premises, when the
dogs would go for him. He would settle No. 1.,
while “ Billy,” the boar, would do likewise with
Nos. 11. and 111. Then an entrance to the front
would be made, the he-pig staying outside to tackle
dog IV., who generally came home late, being a
dissipated brute. After a time they got to know
that boar, and my friend could visit his girl with
impunity.—6Y.H.

Harry Stockdaie tackles De Rougemont
again re aboriginal feminine duels :
De Rougemont’s description of a “ lubra combat” is
obviously not from personal observation. He says,
“ They would then stand face to face, and one would bend
whilst the other dealt her a terrific blow between the
shoulders or on the head with a formidable club. The
blow, which would be enough to kill an ordinary white
woman, would be borne with wonderful fortitude, and the
aggressor would hand the club to the woman she had
just struck. The latter would then take a turn,” and so
on—blow for blow about until one gave in or was unable
to rise. Now, though much of this is perfectly accurate,
three important points are very wide of the mark. First,
the lubras do not tight with clubs, but with a “ fighting
pole,” about 4ft. long, thicker than an ordinary broom-
handle, and of heavy wood, such as red gum or
ironbark. Secondly, the women do not strike
between the shoulders; they strike only on the
head, for which reason a thickly-matted head of hair
(Parramooroo) is of great service to these combatants.
There is no choice of position or any kind of manoeuvring.
The wronged one (generally the challenger) usually gives
first whack. The one to receive the blow
bends the head, as in illustration, usually
putting the hands either on the knees (if a
man), or between the thighs (if a woman),
and the head is so posed as to offer to the
blow the strongest part of the skull. No
dodging is permitted ; friends usually being
picked to see fair play, as in a European
fist-fight. In the rare case of any unfair-
ness, the offender is similarly treated or in-
jured. The third mistake is that women
always use both hands on the fighting pole
—not one, as depicted under de R.’s de-
scription. I have seen many of these ladies’
duels in different parts, of Australia, but
never saw anyone killed, though most of
the blows are very vicious. Occasionally
only one or two blows are struck, one or
the other having had enough ; but gene-
rally it goes an until one combatant is to-
tally disabled. It should be borne in mind
that the natives’ huge head of hair, as
has been said, matted and mixed with
grease and ochre, renders comparatively
innocuous a blow that would naturally
smash the undefended skull. The Cooper's
Creek (Central Australia) Dieyeri tribe,
when fighting a duel accompany each blow
with a savage shout, “ Diemarro! ’’ (I
strike). When a blow fairly fells the re
ceiver, the striker eagerly calls “ Duumkami ” (get*
up, get up); very rarely is there any interference
with a woman’s quarrel, no matter how freely
flows the koomaru (blood). Neither is there any
parrying or feinting (koorathurroona) as when the men
fight. Anything more terrible and brutal in the
way of combat it would be hard to conceive ; but the
women of the aboriginals are inured to blows and bear
them with a fortitude incredible to those who have never
witnessed it.

“J.E.N.”: For years he had been kan-
garooing, dogging, and rabbit-trapping, but,
though I had beard of bis queer ways, I never
dropped across him till one hot day when I was
making tracks back to camp. Reaching the sum-
mit of a sand-hill I caught sight of him gesticulat-
ing, talking at the top of Ins voice as if in violent
controversy, and occasionally clinching a point
with a stick which he had in bis hand. After
watching him for a bit, I walked down towards
him, but he didn’t seem to notice me, and when I
bade him “Good-day,” he wasn’t the least put
out. “Hot,” I said. “’Tis that.” “Do you
know, mate,” I ventured, “that you have been
talking to yourself for the last half-hour?”
“Yes.” “Did you enjoy it?” “Likely I did,
if I kept at it half-an-hour.” “What was the
subject in dispute ? Give me a bold of it and the
pair of us can thrash it out.” “ I can’t give you a
hold of it,” he said. “ The fellow I was arguing
with has got it. He always clears when strangers
Miss ADA CROSSLEY,
Photo., E. Walter Barnett, London, S.W.
come along, and never comes back till they’re gone.
He’s a bloke, too, as mostly minds his own busi-
ness—ain’t cheeky enough to interfere with other
people. Good-day.”

“M‘G.” : Talking about the superstitions
of the Australian bush, who knows anything
about the “ Hairy Men ” of the Murrumbidgee
ranges ? Two men I have met from that locality
swear by the existence of a race of Hairy Men—
diminutive in stature and supernatural in state—
who kill sheep, throw stones at camps after night-
fall, and make themselves generally useless and
objectionable. My informants even hinted that
one’s life was not safe in a neighborhood infested
by Hairy Men, and ascribed many mysterious
disappearances to their malignance, though ad-
mitting that no case of murder was absolutely
traced to them. Nevertheless, nobody living
about the Murrumbidgee ranges would think of
sleeping in a camp without another keeping
watch. My acquaintances both added that they
had themselves seen Hairy Men, though they had
not been injured by them.

Dear Bulletin.— lf the jackass doesn’t
kill snakes, and good big ones, too, how do you
explain this? In the Perth Zoo, not long ago, a
snake about 20in. long led a happy life in a box
with a wire front. Tiring of the monotony he
made his escape by means unknown. That even-
ing a High-school boy, aged 14, was wandering
round when he spotted the escaped serpent, and,
with the enthusiasm of youth, “ bottled ” him in a
jam-tin, took him along to the kookaburra’s cage,
and introduced him to the three or four inmates.
Talk about a circus ! In one act the snake was
done for—hammered to death by one bird against
the log on which the jackies roosted. I was there
at the time, and I’m not more than an average
liar.— Onlooker.
* * * *
Hoolan had a paddock of oats which,
every night for a week, had gradually been getting
less. There was no police protection at the place,
as a shearers’ strike was on at the time, and th?
trooper had been called away. However, O’Toole
came to the rescue. He told Hoolan to bring
along a bottle of whisky, and they would catch
the scoundrel if they had to watch for a week.
Next night Hoolan (who kept the pub.) turned up
at the paddock with two bottles of whisky, and
they laid themselves out to watch. After a little
time a fellow with a bag and a hook entered the
field from the opposite side and started cutting.
Then Hoolan got excited and shouted, and he and
O’Toole charged down upon the reaper, when the
latter raised a weapon and fired. O’Toole fell,
and Hoolan made tracks for home with his hair
trying hard to get through his hat. Then O’Toole
rose again and he and the reaper drank the grog
between them. Next morning he showed Hoolan
his riddled . hat, and Hoolan paid for a new one,
and stood sundry drinks, and tendered a sovereign
as some compensation for the shot which was
alleged to have entered O’Toole in a large number
of places, and caused him great suffering.

Dear Bulletin. — Be “D.L’s” remarks
about Busy and her pups. You couldn’t beat that
strain : so intelligent and so conscientious, too
Next trip we were running the Warrego down
with a flock of 2000 sheep, and found we had
dropped 17 somehow. Sent the pups back to
hustle them along and kept on travelling our-
selves. Forty miles lower down the pups caught
us up. They had 15 of the stragglers, and had
brought along two skins of those they had to use
for rations on the way. Later on, had one of the
pups mustering a paddock at the head station—
we counted out one short, and sent the dog back.
He didn’t bring the stray —but rounded up and
fetched along a sundowner whom he had caught
red-handed killing the sheep.— Kosciusko.
“ Onlooker” : An old drunk at Nan-
nine, Murchison, after “a big night,’ wan-
dered out of the local pub., and fell asleep a
few yards off, in the bush. In the morning i
found an iguana, about 3ft. long, lying along
his chest, and snapping up the flies which
clustered about the drunk s nose, ears and
eyes. In fact, you could scarcely see an
inch of his face for the insects, while, at
first glance, it looked as if he had gut a snake
down his throat. Couldn’t wake him, so,
when iguana bolted, covered his face with
sacking.

“ Jeh ” : I herewith give “ Ben-Sun
(B. 19/11/'9B) the result of study of ants and
their antics conducted at expense of much
“ slapping ” for lateness at school. The colo-
nies of ants have a simple system of freetrade.
Between each mound is a regular track.
Knew one colony situate near a wheat pad-
dock and another near a corn field, and
there was a regular exchange of wheat and
corn between the two. Ants’ senses of touch
and smell are enormously developed at tin-
expense of voice and hearing. Have hid
piece of bread behind grass a few inches off
track and observed ant staggering along with
piece-of wheat, when abreast of bread, drop
wheat and scramble over grass to bread ;
smelt it, and, finding it too heavy, rushed
back and met three mates to whom he ap-
peared to communicate the news. Anyhow
the three quickly had bread over grass on
to track. Very interesting also are their
defensive arrangements. When the enemy
(a stick in writer’s hand) scraped top of
mound, a band of scouts rushed out in great
excitement and scampered off in all direc-
tions to locate the foe. Then the mound be-
came black with the main body, who mus-
tered and marched off north, south, east and
west, leaving behind a reserve who quickly
gathered up pieces of stone and mud and
fortified each hole. Presently the scouts,
finding no enemy, returned and held a head-
to-head confab with rest, and all retired
except a small band of observation that re-
mained on guard for hours.

Dear Bulletin. — Be your footnote to my

19/11/ J 9B). Now that you have reminded me of

used to make a nice little bit out of their skins,
which are well furred and make excellent rugs.
The eggs of the truffle were considered a great
delicacy in those days, but, being extremely rare,
were very costly. Indeed, only one man in Bun-
garee—a retired police-sergeant—could afford to
eat them. The Government, as usual, “ pro-
tected ” these valuable and beautiful animab

“ S.S.” : The bush-barber is often a brutal
miscreant, with the soul of Torquemada. Tin-
last one 1 unguardedly visited shaved me with a
hoop-iron razor that operated on the principle of
the giant stump-extractor. The barber's shingle
was hung out on the chimney of a slab-hut, and
hisshaving-saloon was furnished with a stool, a
six-inch pier-glass, a bar of yellow soap, and a
young mpnkey-bear. The shaving-brush was
made from the tail feathers of a dead horse, and
the artist operated with cold water. When he
had shaved me I was as raw as a skinned rabbit
and fifty times as unhappy. Asked him if lie had
been long at' 1 the trade, and he said nearly a week,
DEAD WOOL.-A BUSH PICTURE.
Oh, there’s cursing on the station when the order comes
to straddle
The worn and weary horses whose death would be their
gain ;
With a curse on all around us and a woolpack on each
saddle
The station-hands go picking up the dead wool from the
plain.
Oh, the weariness of riding when the whole world seems
a-frying
And misery’s cup beside you is ever, ever full;
When you curse yourself for living and you curse the
sheep for dying,
Leaving you to gather up the stinking rotten wool.
When we come unto a carcase we shake it for a minute
To free it from the maggots and sundry swarms of ants,
Then we cram it in the wool-bag leaving many thousands
in it
Who shortly get to business on the inside of our pants ;
Oh, it’s weary, weary riding with the hot sun on our faces
To feel yourself a crawling mass from stirrup-bars to hat;
For the black ants get to business in most aggravating
places
And make you feel as active as a circus acrobat.
Oh, the muddy-rotten water where the bogged sheep lie
a quiver
When the crows have plucked their eyes out and drilled
them through the sides,
We draw them out in hundreds from the filthy, fetid river
And cut their throats for mercy—for mercy and their
hides ;
Is there any wonder in it when we join the shanty’s revel
That we try to drown all misery by keeping ever full;
Is there any wonder in it that we plump it for the devil
When vye ride in from the paddocks after picking up dead
wool ?
So, in lieu of something better, fill your quart-pots at the
river,
Where the carcases are boiling in a perfect devil’s stew,
’Tis an excellent restorer for hydatids in your liver,
So drink the health I now propose, and get hydatids, too.
To the sheep that die in drought-time ! may we find them
knee-deep feeding,
Though the parson says the dumb-beasts from the earth
can mount no higher;
But we who saw their misery and eyeless sockets bleeding
Just hope and pray that we may find the parson is a liar.
C. P. Q. C.

Tt is seriously alleged that, at the battle of
Santiago, the Americans fired off 0000 shell and
only hit with 123. Apparently the difference was
that the Americans were pretty bad marksmen
while the Spaniards were a great deal worse.
When two big ironclad fleets come together, both
in splendid fighting trim, manned by really good
marksmen, as well as by men of grit, and well-
officered to boot, what kind of holocaust will it be ?
During the month of last Sept, the New-
South Wales Lager Bier Brewing Co. Ltd.
supplied the German warship Falke with 0000
quart bottles of Sydney Lager Bier for use on the
voyage to Samoa and Apia. During the vessel’s
stay in our harbor GOO gallons of bulk Sydney
Lager were supplied her. Another tribute to the
excellence of the local lager comes from the Ger-
man warship "Buzzard, which ordered 5500 quart
bottles of Sydney Lager for the return voyage to
Kiel. During this vessel’s stay with us she
received 50 gallons of Sydney bulk Lager daily.
Both ships’ crews cannot speak too highly of the
excellence of Sydney Lager. As the firemen,
officers and crews come from the country that has
always been famous for lager production, it is
clear that the Sydney Lager Bier Brewing Co.
deserves all that has been said by every con-
noisseur in favor of its lager.*

“Tom H.” : “ Nack’s ” legged-snake was
probably a “ shingleback.” Oily-looking reptile,
either jet-black or yellow-and-brown, and never
much more than 18in long ; body thick, compara-
tively. Several that I have killed were found
under heaps of split fencing-stuff stacked in sand-
stone ridges. Can’t say if venomous.

“ A. A. A.” : One of the camels of the ill-
fated Burke-Wills expedition got astray on the
Thompson, and for many months was a lone and
humpy wanderer across country' until it reached
civilisation at Dunglebar Station, on the Barwon.
An old aboriginal who was shepherding about
seven miles from the homestead first discovered it,
and, thinking it was an apparition, deserted
his sheep, and, mounting his horse, made a bee-
line for home at break-neck speed. Arriving
there, almost paralysed with fright, the only ex-
planation that could be dragged from him, after a
liberal dose of brandy, was : “ Holey gose ! Seen
a blully sumthen, blue blully sumthen ! ” The
“ blully sumthen ” turned out to be the above-
mentioned camel, which was eventually handed
over to Melb. Zoo.
“A.,” on the evanishment of the Tas-
manian aborigines :
Perhaps the most singular circumstances connected
with the history of the Tasmanian blacks are their total
disappearance, after the last camp was captured, and the
few traces which remain of their existence. Solitary
stone implements have been unearthed in some locaiilies,
and stone ovens, doubtfully credited to the blacks, are
found on the S. and VV. coasts, notably at Remine, Trial
Bay. Only at Green Point, on the northern portion of
the West; Coast, are there extensive evidences of the
old inhabitants. About a quarter-of-a-inile inland, and
some 50ft. above high water, are huge mounds of burnt
sea-shells, now sheltered by guintrees, which have grown
through their centres. I have sat upon one of
these monuments of the past, and reckoned that,
within a radius of 50 yards, 2000 tons of
shells were deposited and burnt. How many teams
of long-suffering gins must have toiled up those
shifting sand-dunes to supply their families with roast-
mussels? But, apart from these solitary witnesses, there
is now absolutely' nothing on the island clearly connected
with its original possessors. Like the V.D.L. emus, they
walk the plains no more, and What remains is but a
memory, and perhaps a vacant and doubtful skull in a
museum-case. The last blacks were brought in from the
Arthur River by Robinson about ’4B. This extraordinary
man, formerly a Hobart brickinaker, and merely a
handful, tramped from the capital via Port Davy, Mac-
quarie Harbor, and the Pieman to the Arthur River ; and
thence to Circular Head. Here he heard of a notoiiouslv
blood-thirsty tribe (they thirsted for the blood of their
murderers), and, getting on to their tracks, overtook
them at the Black Bluff, about twenty miles south of
Ulveston. Fenton describes how, when the warriors
were drawn up in battle form, Robinson, unarmed
and alone, walked on to the little plain and shook
hands with the boss nigger. The whole tribe then fol-
lowed the plucky little man to Hobart. This incident
is unique in the history of Australia. However, when we
take into consideration the shooting proclivities of the
old Hinglish sports, the depredations and gallantries of
sealers and the military, and the odd hundreds that were
shot, drowned and pole-axed in the interests of civilisa-
tion and agriculture, t he disappearance of the blacks does
not seem quite so curious after all. There is a cliff at the
N.VV. point of the Island down which, old hands have
affirmed, more than 209 blacks were driven into the sea,
chased violently thither by mounted emigrants, who
afterwards pelted the drowning niggers with rocks.
There were slaughtered in the short space of 43 years
the whole of the owners of Van Diemen’s Land, with
the exception of a few miserable consumptives who were
marooned on the Flinders group as punishment for daring
to be still alive.
• * * *
Some years ago a surveyor and his assistant
were doing some fishing on the Herbert River,
N.Q., by lamplight, and from a boat moored, as
they hoped, beyond spear-range from the shore,
the blacks having of late been plentiful and un-
unfriendly. Suddenly, with a crash, over went
the lamp, and the surveyor and his assistant took
to the water, yelling in panic, and struck out for
camp all they knew, leaving boat and fish to take
care of themselves. They dodged the allodiles
and crocogators safely, and, negotiating the mud
and mangroves, they reached camp, scattered the
fire, and lay armed and watching, eaten alive by
sandflies, waiting for the attack. When daylight
came and their courage returned, someone had to
swim to the boat. After much hesitation, the
boss ran the gauntlet of the ’gators a second time,
and discovered lying in the bottom of the boat a
big mullet that had jumped at the light and ex-
tinguished it.

“E.S.S.” writes :
There are many noted camps in N.S.W. and Q., said to
be haunted, on which no mob of cattle will stay the
night. Conmion-sense can hardly accept the ghost
theory ; but there is something about these camps that
terrifies cattle, and which could be explained, perhaps, if
looked into. It might be merely'the peculiar shape of
a bush ; a very limber tree bending low in the wind ; a
hawk or other bird nesting in a tree ; or some singularity
in the surroundings. The scent of a strange animal, too,
will frequently cause a rush. Note how excited a horse
will become on crossing a camel-track. Again, the
“ghost” on many camps may turn out to be a'carpet-
snake, which wanders at night. One beast has only to
jump from it to start the whole mob, and, once they'
nave rushed, the least thing in life will startle them again.
It’s the crushing the majority get that makes them so
terribly anxious to be up and away'. We had one bullock
in a mob from Bulgroo we called “Rushing Rocket.”
He was a very quiet bullock, and didn’t bother getting
up in the first rush ; but, when the hoofs began to pound
him, his bellowing was something to remember. After
that, he always camped 20 or 30yds. out from the mob.
If a beast got up to stretch himself, or one coughed or
switched its tail, “Rushing Rocket” would be on his
pins and going for the Melbourne Cup in one second.
“ Tear-an’-scratch-it ” was another, only- more so. He
tore and scratched so much one night that we lost sight
of him. Was seen next day tearing across a sandhill 20
miles distant. Think he’s tearing yet—if he hasn’t
bu’sted.
* * * *
“Tassy”: When writer was prospecting
on Mt. Dundas (West Coast, Tas.), self and mates
used to hang our meat (nearly always salt-junk)
by a cord to the limb of a tree against which our
humpy was built. Native-cats used to climb
down string and gnaw our food. At last one of
us got a kerosene-tin, flattened it out, and, pierc-
ing it, passed the string through the hole, and
hung the meat beneath. Itcompletely “euchred”
those cats. They would climb down the cord until
they reached the tin—where, of course, there was
no foothold —slide down it and land on the
ground with a thump. Then they would climb
the tree and repeat the operation again and again.
They must have been very puzzled animals, and
considerably sore about the feet, by the time they
left off.
THE WESTBALIAN DIGGER’S PRAYER.
God ! why do You send those flaming flies?
We do not mind the snakes or centipedes,
Hot, stifling winds, Barcoo, or blighted eyes,
Unholy blacks, tin dog, or duffer leads.
All these are diggers’ luck. We patch our pants,
And hail the washing-day with deep disgust;
Whack tucker with the socialistic ants,
And swamp it through the spinifex and dust.
But, Lord ! why do You send those cursfed flies?
They foul the air and fetch the fever on;
In raging masses creep into our eyes,
And make our lives a hell. Not we alone,
But our poor horses—they must suffer, too;
So we, in desperation, come to You.
Unto the silent stars our plaints arise,
Hark to our prayer ; “ Lord ! call off Your flies.”
D. B. G.

MIDSUMMER DAY’S DREAM.


Not generally known that the Emperor of
Austria is “King of Jerusalem.” Which is
probably vvny German William went there. He
wanted to see if the kingdom was worth snatching
Re Albert Edward's late lamented birth-
day. Sanders (“Life of Richard Brinsley Sheri-
dan ) records that Sheridan’s grandfather' a grace-
less Irish parson, lost a comfortable Govt chap-
laincy by preaching on the birthday of the then
Heir-Apparent from the text “ Sufficient for the
day is the evil thereof ” !
“ APENTA.”
The best natural aperient water. Relieves the
kidneys, unloads the liver and opens the bowels.
Sole exporters : The Appolinaris Company,
Limited, London, W.
The irony of it. The German Anthropo-
logical Society has just established that Bismarck’s
ancestry was not Teutonic, hut Celtic ; of the
same race as the Celts of Central France. What
a shock for the old lion to have discovered that
his beloved Prussians were really aliens, and that
it was his own kith and kin he was tramnlinsr
down in 1870.
Related of a certain old-time Tas. peda-
gogue, lately deceased, that, his wife’s pet poodle
haying annoyed him by its yapping, he caught the
animal by the head, placed his foot on its hind-
quarters, tore the shrieking animal in halves, and
tossed the bleeding remnants into the river. Also,
when on one occasion he was thrown from his
horse, he tied its head tightly to a tree in a bush
paddock, placed a bundle or two of green fodder
and a bucket of water a few yards out of its reach,
and left it there for some days. To make the
animal feel its position more acutely, he daily rode
another horse to the spot and allowed it to eat of
the greenstuff and drink of the water, and if the
starving creature looked too pleadingly on, or,
goaded by exposure and hunger, attempted to
break away, he lashed it unmercifully. This
noble gentleman is now in Heaven, no doubt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
S.A. Government proposes lowering the
income-tax exemption from ,£2OO to £l5O to meet
the slump in revenue arising from a fourth con-
secutive failure of the harvest.
Alleged that Gov. Buxton will not return
to S.A. at the .end of his leave, next March, and
will be succeeded by the Dook of Leeds, who was
an aspirant for Curzon’s billet in India, and is
now waiting for something to be thrown to him.
John M‘Kenzie’s retirement from the
Maoriland Ministry is announced to take place
definitely next March. Old John’s mighty con-
stitution is breaking up, and he wants to get out
of the collar and the furrow in his declining years.
“Actinomycosis,” according to Solomon,
M.P. (S.A.), is a sort of Parliamentary disease
resulting from enlargement of the jaw. Under
the new Health Bill cattle suffering from it are
supposed to be killed, but politicians will escape.
Dear B.—Warwick is the R.C. constitu-
ency of Leperland. There is no fiction in this, as
Mr. Morgan says. The writer of the par. in B. of
26/ LO/’9B is a native of Warwick, and knows nearly
every elector personally.— Pompous Peter’s
Watchdog.
Premier Reid consented to abide by the
feeling in the Assembly that salaries of public
servants receiving over £3OO should not be in-
creased. His own little increment of £5OO, equal-
ling about 20 of the proposed increases, was, how-
ever, cheerfully assented to.
Gormly, M.L.A., fell off his horse lately
at Wagga, one foot remaining in the stirrup. The
Albury'BoßDEß Post says: “ Unfortunately the
horse remained at a standstill and the M.L.A.
escaped uninjured.” Type-setting is a risky
occupation about Christmas time.
N.S.W. Govt, contractors are bound, on
paper, to pay laborers not less than 6s. per day,
under a penalty, also on paper, of £5O for each
breach. It is time a few fines were raked in on
this account —if only to convince certain contrac-
tors that the Govt, really means business.
Some S.A. Ministerial measures passed by
the Assembly and killed by the Council this
session : Law Reform, Early Closing, Household
Suffrage,, Workmen’s Compensation and Land
Values Assessment. No Bill which is not hope-
lessly Conservative can now get through the S.A.
Upper House.
After years of agitation a partial and tem-
porary Wages Attachment Abolition Act (holding
good till 1902 only) has passed the Vic. House of
48 Rich Landlords. It provides that there shall
be no attachment of wages up to £2 a week, and
no costs except stamps and court fees (no shark’s
costs, in fact) for attachment of wages up to £4 a
week. The British Parliament has wholly abolished
wage-attachment, even the House of Lords making
no demur. Victoria’s House of Landlords, less
humane and more Tory and more yearnful after
its lib. of flesh, wouldn’t hear of the idea until it
dawned on it that the attachment of wages often
prevented the landlord getting his rent. Then it
sat up suddenly with its hair on end.

their Xmas dinner.
Notwithstanding the glo-
rification of municipal work by
Sen Tillett and the Webbs, the
S.A. Labor-party let the recent
local election go by without wak-
mg ; and yet the S.A. party has more
practical horse-sense than most. Sydney Webb
touched this spot as well as a good many
ethers. You can’t arouse interest (which is to say,
you can’t get the working-man voters to the polls)
unless you send the whole crowd of councillors for
election at once.
Brisbane Chamber of Commerce is peti-
tioning Govt, to prohibit Japs, from owning
pearling boats. No objection, of course, to Japs,
cutting out white labor. “ White men cannot do
the work,” say Northern capitalists. No—neither
could they trash cane or cut it, or do any form of
plantation work until five years ago—and now
whites in hundreds do every form of plantation
labor conceivable.
Roberts, M.P., told the S.A. Assembly
the other day that most of the accidents happen-
ing at sea occur because vessels are manned
with cheap amateurs, tramps and loafers.
Archibald, who represents Port Adelaide, as-
serted that he knew a vessel to be filled up with
a crew consisting of an undertaker, a tinker, some
drapers’ assistants, an ex-policeman, and other
similarly qualified hands.
Rutledge, ex-Wesleyan Minister and Q.
Crown-Prosecutor (he worked-up the grotesque
Q.N. Bank cases), has his eye on the Q. Attorney-
Generalship ; and is going in for politics again
will probably try a squatting electorate. His
oratory is of the table-thumping style. He tried
it on the Towers miners some years ago ; but,
although the Wesleyan element is very strong up
there, he was left to nurse the baby.
Though the N.S. W. Supreme Court has
ruled that the State is not responsible for the lives
and limbs of prisoners, Victoria long ago acknow-
ledged its responsibility in this direction. It is
now about four years since an explosion occurred
in the Pentridge quarries, badly mangling a burg-
lar named Church and rendering him a hopeless
cripple. Church was nursed back to health in the
gaol hospital, and was then granted a pardon
together with an annuity of .£75. The Vic. Govt,
did not wait to be sued for damages, and did not
try to wriggle out of its moral obligation on legal
technicalities.
It really looks as if next year’s general
election for S.A. Assembly will be fought out on
the question of the totalisator. At recent bye-
election for L. Council, the anti-gambling crowd
made the abolition of the machine the test ques-
tion by nominating Haslam, who was also the
Govt, candidate, and, despite their defeat, the
Ikey Mo supporters are already actively organis-
ing for next April. The three organisations now
canvassing against the machine are the united
council of churches, the W.C.T.U., and the Chris-
tian Endeavorers—and it is doubtful which crowd
makes most incoherent row.
Now that the Legislative Council has
scornfully kicked his financial proposals down-
stairs on their head, Premier Reid says, for the
third time, that the time has really come when
that aggressive gang of nominees will have to be
dealt with. He said the same thing when it threw
out his previous Probate Duties Bill, and again
when it threw out his Land Tax ; but though the
country has twice given Reid a majority on the
assurance that he would immediately reform the
Council on a large scale, it remains just where it
was all along. And, so far as N.S. W.’s fat poli-
tical welsher is concerned, it seems likely to stay
there.
The sparkling J. H. Gordon's retorts are
nowadays almost the only things that differentiate
the once-Liberal S.A. Legislative Council from
other drowsy Upper Houses. And most of Gor-
don’s shafts are now sent towards the Labor
benches. Gordon was pooh-poohing Kingston’s
law-reform proposals. He knew, he said, four
gentlemen who were awaiting the opportunity to
practise law. Then Robinson, inanely: “Can
they chop wood?’’ Gordon: “Yes; and they
will be glad to do so if the hon. gentleman will
bring his head into the back-yard,” Robinson
might have shot back something about axes and
the little one that lawyer Gordon was grinding
but he didn’t.

An amazing person
named “M, J. Foyer ”
who recently globe-trot-
ted through Australia, pub-
lishes his wild discoveries in
the Natal (South Africa) Ad-
vertiser. Foyer describes him-
self as a “ single-tax enthusiast; ”
■J and his best discovery is that
Queensland keeps its only tweed
factory going by a 20 per cent, protec-
tive tax on imported wool—apparently
the single-tax enthusiast actually im-
agines that Australia is a wool-importing
country. Also, he is greatly pleased to find that
the <ias services in N.S.W. are owned by the State,
and sets it down to the blessed influences of Free-
trade.
The old relief-work system has started
again undisguisedly in N.S.W. It has really
raged all the time in the shape of sand-shifting,
forest-thinning, scrub-cutting, railway deviation
works, reclamation jobs at Shea’s Creek, blanket-
distributions and the like, but the public took little
notice. But the putting on of 1730 unemployed
at one fell swoop to paint the Sydney park railings
at ss. per day (this just after Reid's blatant asser-
tion that the unemployed trouble was almost
extinct) is a sudden shock to the believers in Jolly
and Fat.

NEW-CHUM : “ Great Scott I And they call this a
DRY season! ”
When the Yic. political crisis was at its
height, the other week, the possibility of Tren-
with’s Premiership was seriously discussed in the
lobbies. One of the Ministries formed on paper
was: 'Tren with, Premier and Chief Secretary;
Styles, Treasurer and Railways ; Higgins, Attor-
ney-General; M‘Lean, Lands; Hancock, Public
Works and Customs; M‘Cay, Solicitor-General
and Education ; Burton, Mines ; Donald Melville,
Defence; Godfrey, Postmaster-General.
Ono of the Turner Govt.’s proposed
amendments of the Factories Act provides that
all regulations and by-laws may be disputed for a
year after their promulgation, but after that
period they shall not be challengeable on any tech-
nical grounds. This is a most important pro-
vision, and, whether passed now or not, will
ultimately be found essential to the success of the
Wages Board principle. Without such a provision
the law will be in a continual state of flux on
account of the extreme improbability of a board
of laymen framing the wording of a regulation on
strictly accurate legal lines. Even a gathering of
lawyers seldom frames a law which doesn’t break
down somewhere on an utterly useless technical
point. The proviso that one year, and no more,
shall be allowed for hunting up technical flaws
would come in handy if applied to the Statute
Book all round.
S.M. Herald came out on 12th December
with a leading article of great whoop about how,
under the blessings of Freetrade, a foreign soap
firm (Lever Bros., of England) had been induced
to start business in Sydney. Under the Freetrade
system the number of N.S.W. soap-works has
dropped from 50 in 1895 to 40 at the beginning of
1898 ; employes from 476 to 310 ; soap made from
299,356 cwt. to 187,142 cwt.; candles made from
4,632,7301 b. to 2,638,1751 b. And this despite the
fact that the business has been greatly improved
on paper by the inclusion, since 1896, of small
factories employing less than five hands, not
previously reckoned. If Freetrade destroys 11
Australian-owned soap factories and puts one
foreign-owned one in their place the blessing
might be a little more obvious without much in
jury to its constitution.
Both N.S.W. Houses are now finished
with the proposed Federal amendments, and each
one demands that the financial arrangements con-
tained in the old Bill shall be completely reor-
ganised. Yet neither House gives the slightest
hint as to the kind of reorganisation it wants ; or
explains what is wrong with the present scheme.
The N.S.W. Premier is simply to meet the other
provinces and say: “Our Legislature wants the
financial arrangement altered.” Then will come
the natural question : “What kind of alteration
does it want?” “ Dunno !” “What does your
Parliament think is the matter with the present
arrangement?” “Dunno! Never asked it. We
want the Braddon clause left out, anyhow.” “Do
you want anything put in instead of it?”
“Dunno! Our Parliament didn’t say.” “Have
you any new financial scheme about you ? ” “No,
not ex-actly.” “What kind of financial scheme
will suit the N.S.W. Parliament?” “Dunno.
Never thought of asking.” “Is there any part of
the present scheme you object to more than the
test?-” “Dunno.” “ Well, what do you want us
t<* do about it? ” “ Dunno.” No wonder Federa-
tion is getting along rapidly.
The commendable Early Closing Bill, in-
troduced in N.S.W. Assembly by the Reid
Government, is not really an Early Closing Bill at
all. It provides that no person, except the boss or
a member of his family, shall be compelled to
work more than a certain number of hours per
week; but if the man who owns the emporium
likes to hire relays of employes he can keep open
24 hours in the day and do no harm to anybody.
Indirectly this gives a lift to the small man who
runs his establishment all by himself as against
the big one who-works with hired labor, which is
a decidedly good feature. But there are far too
many exemptions—chemists, coffee houses, res-
taurants, confectioners, fish, oyster, fruit and
vegetable shops, booksellers, news-agents, tobac-
conists, and hotels can still work their employes
to skin and bone for an indefinite number of hours
per day. There would be good sense in exempting
these in an Early Closing Bill, but in a Bill which
merely restricts the hours of labor and lets the
shop keep open as long as the owner pleases, no
exemption—except, perhaps, in the case of the
chemist—is needed. Still, the move is in the right
direction, though when a democratic measure like
this comes on at the last days of a hurried, jaded,
overworked session, it is apt to be only a good
intention.
In Maoriland, under the State resumption
of lands for closer settle-
ment policy, one little g r P
of landowners demanded
.£120,000 from the Treasury
for 43,000 odd acres, and
swore to the valuation as
honest and even moderate.
The same crowd, when id
sent in its land-tax valua-
tions, had just previously
sworn that the same property
was worth less than <£47,000.
And nobody seemed much
surprised. Little things like
that are always liable to hap-
pen.
The police-system,
when a charge of hav-
ing no sufficient law
ful means of support
arises, of ringing
in all sorts of evi-
dence embracing
every subject
under the Z sun,
receives a rebuff
from the Vic.
Chief Justice.
The case was
one of a man
convicted of
vagrancy,
partly on the
evidence of a
witness who
alleged he had
seen him pick-
in g pockets.
The C.J.
ruled that it was
the first princi-
pie of law that a man was to answer only the
offence on which he was charged, and if evi-
dence was brought relating to other charges
the conviction was bad. The average amateur
J.P. should cut this out and paste it on the
whisky-bottle, so that he will see it frequently.
The most amazing circumstance of the year
in S.A. politics was the rejection, by 27 votes to
23, of the Aliens Restriction Bill. The British
Government flatly refuses to assent to a measure
which shuts out the British nigger and the British
Chinaman, as such, from any part of the empire.
But it professes its willingness to sanction a Bill
providing an educational test for all immigrants,
white and colored ; and, as very few of the yellows
and the browns are likely to crawl through the
needle’s eye of this restriction, the idea certainly
seems good. It was rejected by a temporary com-
bination of the Labor-party and the Tories —which
was the maddest thing the S.A. Labor-party has
done up to date.
Now that the Legislative Council ot N.S.W.
has thrown out Premier Reid’s probate and suc-
cession duties, he has only £70,000 left out of the
.£279,000 of new taxation which he demanded.
When he asked for the original £279,000 he told
the Assembly it was all required to make ends
meet. The Assembly professed to want to make
ends meet, yet it knocked off £129,000 of the
amount—nearly one-half—and offered nothing in
exchange ; so it tacitly recorded its opinion that
Reid, when he said the whole amount was essen-
tial, was either a fool ora liar. The bill came
before the Council as a demand for £150,000 which
was all alleged to be absolutely essential, and the
Council, by knocking off another £BO,OOO, re-
corded its tacit opinion tiiat Reid was either a
fool or a liar. Both were merely expressions of
opinion ; still, they were very unpleasant opinions.

Queensland Assembly by the wildly cultivated
Matthew Battersby, M.L.A. —the gentleman who
objects to “ book-larnin’,” and opposes the estab-
lishment of a Queensland University :
Mr. Battersby : Mr. Annear, Mr. Annear, I am sorry
