Colonial Artist’s Success.
Among our colonial artists whose names have
come prominently before the public is that of Mr.
Ulic Nichols, of Sydney, and his recent success
with Warner’s Safe Cure is but another proof of
the merits of this great remedy that others imitate
but none equal.
The Studio, 337 Pitt-street, Sydney,
25/8/98.
“ I had suffered for some years from liver and
kidney complaints, as well as those of the bladder,
which Warner’s Safe Cure has effectually cured.
I was unable to stand long at my easel, which was
a great trouble until I took this medicine.”
The experience of this well-known artist is
not unique. It is simply the repetition of that of
others who persevered as he did.

W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir. For years past (about ten), I have been
trouble with “ Acne” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene ”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “Cam Grano Palis" and was in-
clined not to use it; eventually I iried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy'. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligmolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
Prevention of Seasickness. —Extraordi-
nary interest has been aroused by a large number
of letters appearing in the Evening News and
Star testifying to the efficacy of the new remedy,
“Seajoi.” The Mayor of Annandale, Mr. Allen
Taylor, writes that hitherto he was “always sea-
sick,” but took “Seajoi” on a recent journey to
Port Macquarie, and “ was enabled to onjoy the
trip without suffering any of the previous discom-
fort.” Another interesting letter was from Mr.
Alex. M‘lntosh, of the North Coast S. Ship Co.,
Grafton. He wrote : “ I and Mr. Me each
took your preparation, and I have now the pleasure
of informing you that neither of us had the
slightest sympton of sickness on the voyage to the
Clarence.” “ Seajoi ” is one of several valuable
remedies prepared and sold by Mr. Albert Sharp,
of 257 George-st., Sydney. (In bottles, 2s. 6d.
and 4s. 6d.; posted to any colony, 4d. and Bd.)*
Dear Bulletin. —A young Melb. matron
was, last week, sentenced to 12 months’ imprison-
ment for the unusual crime of poisoning her baby
with a dose of spirits of salt. As a poisoning case
pure and simple it was rough on the deceased, for
the spirits of salt burned the victim’s throat and
stomach horribly. Nevertheless, a jury sympa-
thised with the mother “ on account of the provo-
cation she had received,” and Chief Justice
Madden went one better by letting her off—poor
soul ! she appeared in deep mourning for the baby
—with al2 months’ holiday from work. A ridi-
culous sentence on the face of it, for a mother
who doesn’t deserve more than that for a fiendish
destruction of her offspring can’t possibly deserve
any punishment at all. But the excuse in Ellen
M’Nabb’s case was the excuse that always moves
Judge Madden to make a sentimental object of
himself. “She had been deserted,” said his rampa-
gious Honor, “by a villainous husband who cared
nought what became of her or their child. She
had been battered, ill-treated and almost starved
in a villainous fashion and then taken back by her
parents.” Also her father had slightly harrowed
her feelings by suggesting that she might have
managed her husband better perhaps. Anyway,
she was in great trouble, therefore she became
irresponsible for her actions and killed the baby
in a burst of frenzy. Justice Madden took the
evidence of the prisoner and her friends for
granted. The dead baby said nothing to the con-
trary, and the husband is away in Westralia or
somewhere, so he couldn’t throw light on his
alleged villainy. The husband was found guilty
behind his back and condemned to infamy (by
Madden) for evermore. Nobody.had the cruelty
to remark that Ellen M‘Nabb, the excitable baby-
slayer, was possibly the sort of wife that creates
iil-conduct in a husband. The- fact that she has
proved capable of burning her child’s internals to
death looks like it. Madden’s faith in the
grievances of the opposite sex needs a wholesome
corrective. If a poor, forsaken, ungovernable-
tern pered- Melb. wife were to relieve her feelings
by pouring half-a-pint of acid on Madden’s bald
head she would thereby explain a whole lot of
things to him. — Conrad.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
.To adopt the language of solemn journalism—
“ it is within the knowledge ” of this flippant red-covered
paper that Mr. W. It. Wilson desires to be a self-respecting
horse-owner. Seeing there was no malice in some Bul-
letin references to the recent deeds of Symmetry, Auri-
ferous and Scorn, owner W. R. Wilson has furnished full
explanations, verbal and documentary, and this paper
feels more than ever smileful at the blind ignorance
of the average punter. Take the case of Scorn.
The owner backed the colt heavily at its first dis-
appointing attempt, and again for £lOO when it
lost the Debutant Stakes (Caulfield). When it won
the Maribyrnong Plate the owner, his faith being
considerably shaken, put only £5O on his chance.
Yet “clever” folk suggest that Scorn was “stiffened”
for the aforesaid Debutant Stakes (worth £1000) with a
view to lengthening its price for the Maribyrong (worth
£1070) so that the stable might land a great coup.
Revenue, another two-year-old object of suspicion, carried
the same amount of Wilson capita]—a big amount—each
time it ran, losing one race at Caulfield previous to win-
ning twice at Flemington. And in these cases, as in all
the cases under notice, W. R. Wilson’s stable manager,
Leslie Macdonald, speculated on the same lines as the
“ boss.” What the one knows about the 'osses, the other
knows. Sometimes they both know nothing. It is only
the ’oss that knows.
Touching the performances of Symmetry and
Auriferous. The mare, long time invalided, had not
been out in public for a year when she and Auriferous
went for the Toorak Handicap (one mile) at Caulfield.
Mr. Wilson put £5O on each of them, but didn’t fancy
them greatly. He planted £25 on Devoted likewise.
Symmetry, in the stable’s opinion, was not likely to prove
herself a stout mare. She pulls hard, and given a very
fast race, she tires in the seventh furlong. Auriferous is
notoriously a colt that needs plenty' of whip persuasion.
Neither he nor the mare went near to winning the Toorak
Handicap. And W. R. Wilson, being then almost con-
vinced that Symmetry couldn’t stay, ran her in the
Coburg Stakes (one mile) on the V.R.C. Derby day',
and did not hack her, or advise anyone to back
her. She was unbacked, except, perhaps, by a few dozen
very small punters who ought to have known better,
owner Wilson would say, than to throw their shillings
away upon the representative of a popular stable when it
was clearly not a popular “tip.” The Coburg Stakes
failure, coming after the Toorak Handicap failure,
naturally tended to put the public off Symmetry. They
were not to know that the stable reckoned her fairly
good goods for a sprint and a sprint only'. On Cup-day
the twice-beaten Symmetry came out for a six-furlongs
race, and owner Wilson went for a recovery' —not
a “plunge” by any means, but just a little dip. The
stable didn’t regard Symmetry as a “dead-bird,” y'et she
got there all the same. Owing to her brilliant win in the
Railway Handicap, Symmetry was made a hot first favor-
ite for the Oaks. Sopiebpdy must have “plunged ” over
Symmetry. Not W. R. Wilson, or anyone connected
with him. The stable didn’t have a solitary shilling on,
The Bulletin is assured. It was not reckoned a reason-
able investment at the price. W.R.YV. told his friends
that Symmetry couldn’t win the Oaks(l£ mile) if the pace
were fast throughout; his brother wired him to put £5
on, but he did not invest his brother’s fiver as requested.
The Oaks was a “muddling” race, and the fleet Sym-
metry managed to win it by a few inches with a final
sprint. The public thought that the stable “ brought off
a coup,” whereas the stable let its first favorite run loose.
So much for popular turf delusions.
On the clay when Symmetry won the Oaks,
little Auriferous showed smart form in running second to
Catspaw for a 1J race ; therefore, when the stable com-
panions, Auriferous and Elusive, came out for the Final
Handicap (on the Saturday), many people preferred the
little ’un. As a matter of fact, W. R. Wilson backed both
of them, preferring Elusive, who had also run well earlier
in the week. He put £lOO on Elusive, £5O on Auriferous,
and “ savers” on three other horses. Elusive won, whilst
Auriferous ran nowhere. Mr. Wilson explains that the
boy on Auriferous had his whip knocked out of his
hand at an early stage of the race, and has never
seen it more. The whip was lost for ever, and
the chance of the sluggish Auriferous was lost with
it. To cut a long story shorter, The Bulletin rises to
express a full belief- in every plain statement made by
owner W. It. Wilson concerning his horses, and his own
bets, and the bets of Leslie Macdonald, who has ever been
regarded by outsiders as ttie stable wire-puller and
“ tricky ” man. Macdonald dresses himself up to look
like the shiny villain in a Bland Holt melodrama, but he
doesn’t actually play the character in the smallest degree.
Were he really such a. crafty dandy as he looks—well, he
would dissemble.
But to believe that W. R. Wilson is a very white
man, as horse-owners go, is one thing. To admire the
Australian racing system of which Mr. Wilson is a respect-
able exponent is quite another thing. The Bulletin has
always maintained an attitude of snort towards a horse-
racing community whose most honest members see
nothing unsportsmanlike in running a supposed sprinter
over a mile course, un backed, as owner Wilson admits to
having run Symmetry in the Coburg Stakes. That sort
of thing is not done by the trusted owners—the Roths-
childs and Roseberys—in England, the home of horse-
racing. Moreover, Mr. Wilson argues that it is fair sport
to run an unfit two-year-old together with a fit stable
companion which admittedly carries the owner’s coin and
confidence. He talks, as ail Australian owners talk, of
the “ necessity ” for making a youngster acquainted
with a crowd before it is seriously started in busi-
ness. Rats! The risk of a two-year-old “run-
ning green ” at a first attempt is not so great
that it ought to be run at the public expense. Every horse
that represents an important stable carries some public
money, and is supposed to represent some stable hope of
success. It has no business to be “ out for an airing,”
though, the “ airing ”be known to almost anybody. The
“out-for-an-airing” policy gives unenlightened punters
a chance to drop their dollars and half-sovereigns down
the sink, and any owner who follows this common Aus-
tralian practice is not a real At 18-carat sportsman. To Tub
Bulletin’s way of thinking, every turfman in this country
invites suspicion, and ought not to feel surprised when
he gets it.
« * • •
Victorian turf authorities are wrestling hard
with this problem : A Vic. bookmaker was instructed by
a client to put £lO on Scorn for the Maribyrnong Plate,
and, in case of a win, to put the winnings on Bobadil for
the Derby. He agreed, and Scorn did win the Maribyr-
nong Plate. But ttie Derby was run before the Maribyr-
nong Plate, so he decided that he couldn’t put the
winnings on Bobadil—both parties appear to have for-
gotten the respective dates when the wager was made.
Bobadil didn’t win, by the way. Now the question is : (1)
Does the backer get the £6O he won on Scorn for the
Plate, and does the matter end there ? Or (2) does he get
nothing on the ground that he lost on Bobadil the £6O he
won on Scorn though he hadn’t then won it—in other
words, was Bobadil supposed to carry all the money that
the backer was going to win on Scorn, whatever it might
be, and supposing he was going to win anything
at all? Or (3) does the backer get back his £lO on
the ground that there was no bet at all; in other
words, the money having been staked on the allegedly
impossible condition that the winnings on the second
race, if any, were to be staked on the first race,
which is the same as going to do last week what
should be done to-morrow, is the whole transaction off ?
In The Bulletin’s opinion No. 2 is the logical answer,
and the gentleman with the bag is, for once, a legitimate
winner. The backer ordered that his winnings on Scorn
(next week), if any, were to be staked on Bobadil (to-day).
He didn’t say they were to be staked on Bobadil to-day
after they had been won on Scorn next week ; he simply
said they were to be staked. Therefore, a logical bookie
would have carried out his instructions literally. The
case as regards Bobadil was : “ I lay Mr. , £
(amount ta be filled in in the event of Scorn winning)
against Bobadil at (say) 2to 1.” If Bobadil won, and the
odds were 2 to 1, then the bookie had simply to pay
double the winnings on Scorn, if that animal won. When
Bobadil lost, then the bookie had merely to make a note
in his own capacious mind : “ Whatever Mr. wins
from me on Scorn, if he wins anything, he has already
lost to me on Bobadil. If he doesn’t win anything
on Scorn, then he hasn’t lost anything to me on
Bobadil. Either way, the transaction is closed.” As to
the contention that it was impossible to back Bobadil
with the money won on Scorn because Bobadil’s race came
first, that is a logical absurdity. If anyone will send the
writer £lOO with instructions to back a horse in the Mel-
bourne Cup of 1900 at starting-price, and, if the animal
wins, to put the winnings at starting-price on a horse in
the Melbourne Cup of 1899, he will do it, and thereby
demonstrate the possibility of the matter. Also, he will
do it with the utmost ease—on his head, so to speak.
* • • *
The Totalisator Bill has been “ postponed ” by
N.S.W. Council, and, as the session will probably end
before the postponement is over, that simply means re-
jection. G. C. Heydon, Webb, and Renwick all im-
plored the Fat Men not to legalise the curse of gambling,
and the Fat Men decided that they wouldn’t. Now if
these three pious shriekists will introduce a Bill under
which the papers will be fined (say) £lOO each every time
they foster gambling by publishing the odds, and every
time they publish “Tattersall’s ” sweep advt. ; and
which will also order the arrest and imprisonment of
every bookmaker laying the odds on any racecourse or
anywhere else in N.S.W., and the fining, to the extent of
(say) £lOOO, of every racing club which allows book-
makers on its grounds, and the fining or jugging of the
man found backing a horse, and a few other things—then
Tiib Bulletin will begin to believe in the sincerity of the
three gentlemen aboveinentioned. As they never do
anything, however, to suppress the gambling evil they de-
plore so much, they look like three very unconvincing
whited sepulchres who are helping the bookmaker to
carry on the worst gamble going, for reasons which this
paper doesn't want to inquire into.

“ Bill ” Long’s Grand Flaneur colt Beaulieu,
looks the deadest of birds for the rich December Stakes at
Randwiek on Saturday. Judging by the way in which he
humped 9st. 31b. and ran away with the 2-year-old race at
Warwick Farm, the other youngsters will have to improve
wonderfully to get within “ cooee ” of him at weight for
age.
* * * *
The divs. paid by one of Sydney’s illegal ss.
totes (pay first three) on the Warwick Farm Handicap,
make interesting comparison with the “legal” odds laid
by Ikey Mo on that race. The placed horses were Eastern
King, Fitzroy, and Standard ; and Ikey, who pays first
horse only (not first three), was laying 7’s, 10’s and 6’s
respectively. The tote returned £3 17s. Bd. for every
“ crown ” invested on Eastern King—close on double the
odds laid by the books. Fitzroy paid the remarkable div.
of £4l 19s. 6d. for second place, while the 133 who had
their modest dollar on Standard were each returned
4s. 2d. The books, of course, paid nothing on Standard
or Fitzroy. Had Fitzroy won, the machine would have
paid at the rate of about 600 to 1. Imagine Cohen laying
tote odds, and bawling “ Six hundred to von Fitzroy ! ”
A ss. tote, run by same man, on last Kensington Handi-
cap, returned £5 3s. 6d. for winner, against whom the
books were laying the munificent odds of 6 to 1.
• * • •
No better gauge of the possibilities of well-
conducted pony-meetings can be taken than the fact that
Mr. P. O’Mara has decided to throw in his lot with the
ponies, by continuing the management of Kensington
and giving Rosehill the go-bye. Mr. O’Mara has held the
dual positions for many years, and is assured of the pony
future by his past lengthy experience, for it is scarcely
likely he would throw up the substance for the shadow.
The A.J.C. may yet regret its decision compelling
racing officials to choose between the “horses” and
“ little-’uns,” instead of meeting the inevitable and taking
the pony management in hand.
• • • •
The added money of the next Newmarket Han-
dicap has been raised from 750 to 1000 sovs., whilst the
sweepstakes remains 25 sovs., so this race is well worth
waiting for and winning. The Australian Cup is kept
at its old “figger”—looo sovs. with sweepstakes of 25
sovs.

The team of racers and chasers going ’Ome
under management of Mr. J. E. Brewer is a purely
Australian conbination. Now an opportunity will be
afforded of seeing our horses, trained and ridden by Aus-
tralians, competing with the English cracks.
• • » *
When this issue went to press the N.S.W. and
S.A. teams were still battering the sphere with the piece
of wood in an unsettled kind of way. N.S.W. made 374,
and S.A. 334 first innings; Donnan’s 160 and Trumper’s
68 for N.S.W., and the 82 of Jones and 73 of Hill for S.A
being top scores. The fallen idol, Giffen, was out for 15.
And when last heard of S.A. was belting the sphere, in
its second innings, with great vigor and only moderate
prospects.
• • • •
Noble, who threw up his bank billet foi the
interprovincial cricket tour in esse and the English
one in posse, should have paused to consider the example
of Geo. Giffen. He will have to do wonders before he
does for cricket what Giffen has done, and then, should
he fail once or twice, the cricket-loving public will turn
dog on him as they have done on the South Australian.
AFreneh Premier’s portfolio isn’t so slippery as the billet
of a cricketing idol.
* * * •
Cricketers who have scored over 200 in inter-
provincial matches : Murdoch, 321 v. Victoria (still the
record); Moses, 297 not out v. Victoria ; Trumper, 295
not out v. Tasmania ; P C. Macdonnell, 239 v. Victoria ;
Clem Hill, 206 not out v. N.S. Wales, and Geo. Giffen, 271
and 239 v. Victoria, and 203 v. N.S. Wales. Strange that
Joe Darling, probably the best batsman in Australia, has
never made over 200 in one innings against any province.
* • * *
Ned Gregory is lying dangerously ill at his
home in the Cricket Association Ground.
* * * *
The Melb. country cricket week has just ended.
The idea was to unearth young and rising talent, particu-
larly in the bowling line, but all the old fossils that have
been playing cricket for the past 20 years or more—
Pleasance, Jack Harry, Bruce, the Mackays, and others —
came down to play. The province was divided into four
districts, a team came from each. They were pitted
against each other, and, at the end of the week, a picked
team did battle against a very moderate Victorian eleven,
captained hy Laver. Naturally enough, the four country
captains chose the team, and, naturally enough, the four
country captains were amongst the elect. Laver, who
apparently glories in bowling, bowled all the bumpkins
out, although there were some comparatively untried
trundlers on his side whom he did not make use of. So
the object of the country week was in some measure
defeated.
• • • *
The mammoth scoring still goes on in Yic. pen-
nant matches. Carlton, captained by Worrall —the only
unbeaten team, so far—made 881 in three Saturdays
against Richmond in the last round, and the Richinond-
ites thereupon abandoned the match. T. Warne scored
402 by a magnificent display of cricket, marred by only
one sharp chance at 41. He batted for nearly three
Saturdays, and his false strokes could be counted on one
hand. He seemed to lose his head when approaching
Worrall’s record score of 417 (not out), and hit wildly at
a shortish one and was caught at cover. His doings for
this season are : 141 (not out), 56 (given out wrongly), 77
(run out), and 402.
Dear Bulletin. —He paragraph in Bulletin
Oct. 15., I wish to make a correction. It was for Hamp-
stead v. Stoics that Stoddart made 485, not Hampshire
C.C., as the “Q.C.P.” states. Stoddart never played for
Hampshire, only for Middlesex. Hampstead is a suburb
of London (where the said Stoddart lived), and Hampshire
is a county. So, I fancy, the “ Parson” must be dream-
ing, to put it mildly.— Kensinqtonian.
* * » •
During the running of the Austral final, a new
feature was introduced in the shape of the trainer's
whistle. As the race assumed a different aspect, as men
came along from the rear or dropped back, the whistle
would ring out in notes of different length and number
telegraphing the position of the field to the man plugging
onwards with his head down.
* * * *
Billy Middleton, who finished second in the
Austral, is the third cycling member of the plucky
Dunnolly mining family, and seems to have better judg-
ment than either of his brothers.
The failure of Walne in early part of Austral
meeting was atoned for last Saturday by his brilliant
finish of the 10-mile scratch, which he won by lengths,
•weeping round the field in the final run in as good style
as ever. In his heat of one of the minor events Walne
also made a dash, that was one of the features of the
afternoon, unsuccessful though it was.
M‘Donald, another Dunollyite, who made a
name by defeating Walne in the mile-scratch on the pre-
vious Saturday, was the most erratic performer the last
day. Riding splendidly in the first half of one race, he
faded away at the end and finished nowhere, only to
come out and win the preliminary of another race in the
style of a real champion. But the final heat of the second
race saw M’Donald collapse once more, after riding half
the race in splendid style.
A stout figure in a white helmet pervaded the
arena for several minutes during the afternoon, and the
crowd started asking what Mossy Isaacs was doing there,
and several yelled from the fence fora price about Finne-
gan. Then Minister Best came along, and crawled at the
feet of the man in the helmet so squirmingly that he was
identified at once as Baron Brassey himself.
Porta, fat as a Legislative Councillor, passed
the ladies’ reserve without an eye being raised to greet
him, and not once did he pedal round waving his hand as
of yore. Maximilian rode nowhere in everything, and
retired to brood over past greatness, when bevies of St.
Kilda beauties brought him flowers to solace him for a
broken leg, and when he was carefully shaved in bed
every morning by a barber who guaranteed not to cut
him and “ mek him hoogly."
Cyclists seem to be afflicted with names of a far
more extraordinary nature than other athletes. The
Austral programme contains Busst, Crisp, Diseaciati,
Hefter, Tame, Mutton, Hickox, Howqua, Jochimsen,
Body and Neunhoffer ; while Gargurevich and Jamoneau
finished first and second in one Austral heat.
* • » *
During the interval in the performance of “ My
Sweetheart,” in Hobart, the other night, the one-armed
round-the-world pedestrian, Schilling, appeared and made
a speech. Like most big athletes, Schilling is somewhat
slope-headed, and, when introduced to the audience,
looked half-inclined to run away and hide somewhere.
However, with a mighty yawn, intended to hide his con-
fusion, he began a stammering bit of battered-speak to
the “ladies and gents.” present. The start was painful
in the extreme. The big fellow floundered over the first
few mixed-up sentences somehow'; made an attempt to
catalogue his route in guide-book fashion ; and got mixed-
up worse than ever. Then he mentioned something about
how much he missed his “ doag,” that faithful animal
having been run into quarantine in Sydney forsix months.
Mention of the “ doag” brought a mighty cheer instead
of the laugh which seemed inevitable. The gods were
with the speaker at once. The athlete was cute enough
to notice his advantage, and w'orked that “dong” for all
it was worth. The rest of the speech was mostly “doag,”
punctuated by cheers. If the Teutonic-Yank'had only
dragged a horse into the trouble, lie would have made
never-dying fame as an orator. One touch of dog or
horse makes all Australia kin.
• * • *
For years Sydney has not had such an exciting
swimming competition as that at Coogee last Saturday
afternoon for the Flying Squadron team championship, or
club supremacy, of the province. Deafening yells and
screeches of encouragement and applause marked the
progress of the contest from beginning to end. Thanks
to the brilliant efforts of its two sehool-boy champions—
Byers and Grant—Balmain Club won by six yards from
East Sydney, which team beat Newcastle by a yard. But
for the absence of one of their best men, Kemp, through
the death, by drowning, of his father on Friday, the
Novacastrians might have proved successful. As it was,
they won their heat in 2 l-ssec. faster than the time of
the final, but some of them, apparently, couldn’t saddle
up again.
• * • *
There’s no mistake about the manner In which
swimming has taken on in Sydney. Over 90 swdmmers
competed in one event last Saturday, and the bringing of
it off necessitated 14 preliminary' heats, three semi-finals
and, of course, a final.
* * * *
Strong-man Sampson, when he left Sydney last
week for Melbourne, was a very weak man, through having
burst, a blood-vessel on the previous Friday. Medical
advice was opposed,to a continuance of his business for
at. least a month, but Sampson has made engagements
ahead, and must start breaking things again. His advent
in Sydney gave a great impetus to the chain-making
industry. Almost every second man you meet has his
theory and is experimenting. Two well-known sporting
medicoes spent all one Sunday, aided by half-a-dozen
friends, in testing one of Sampson’s chains which they
had secured. Tugs-of-war were arranged, and herculean
attempts made to pull the house down. A vice was
pressed into service, and, with the chain-bracelet en-
circled thereon, expanded, but all to no purpose—the
links would stretch but not snap. Shillings also
resisted the power of a pair of pincers in each hand.
How’s the thing done, anyhow ?
* * • *
At a recent Sydney “ glove ” contest there was,
for personal reasons, very bad blood between two of the
“ pugs,” and they fought like tiger-cats. After one of
the rounds, one of them suddenly jumped up, rushed
across, and, brandishing his fist in his antagonist’s face,
yelled, “You , you ’it me foul!” The other res-
ponded with equal eloquence, and the choicest amenities
filled the air until the referee stepped between, with
“ Come, come, gentlemen 1 No unpleasantness, please I’’
• * * *
The design for this year’s ladies’ tickets for the
Sydney Cricket Ground (curiously resembling a teetotum,
and executed, regardless of expense, in common card-
board) is another attempt to grapple with the problem of
twice as many ladies being present in the stands as there
are tickets issued. A minion at the gate is to stamp out
one of the figures on the dial, which prevents the ticket
being used again that day. Also, the ugliness of the
document is probably intended as a discouragement.
Comparatively few display these cheeseplate-looking
affairs compared with those who wore the pretty heart-
shaped tokens of previous years. ,

They’ve got things down fine in Melb. just now.
One Bourke-street tobacconist sells quarter-plugs of weed
at a penny; and a publican on Poverty-corner for a
“ tray” has been giving a long-sleever, a free lunch and
also laying the customers £5 he didn’t name the first
three in the Cup, or £3 he didn’t strike the winning
double—Melb. and Williamstown Cups.


Anybody can be an agent for cherry
brandy ; but John Walker and Sons, Ltd., of 22
Bond-st., Sydney, have a unique cherry whiskey
calculated to throw the most festive kind of frag-
rance over Xmas. This already famous liqueur—
Fretny's Cherry Whiskey—is the result of the com-
bination of a new idea with the Montmorency
cherry and fine old Glenlivet whiskey, and it
makes your mouth water even to read a paragraph
about it, let alone write one. And the Sydney
agents are John Walker and Sons aforesaid.*
The ancient ship Republic—which has
twice set sail from N.S. W. with coal for Honolulu,
and twice put back for repairs—made a third
abortive attempt to get away last week. The new
crew was still unsatisfied of the vessel’s sea-
worthiness, and refused to bend sail or get up
anchor ; and there were strange rows and ructions
on board the aged, luckless tub, and the captain
was arrested on a charge of shooting one of the
sailors and committed for trial. At latest advices
the Republic still leaks tranquilly at her anchor-
age. There is no law in N.S.W. under which any
authority can prevent any ship, however awful,
going to sea if the crew will take her there and
provided she doesn’t carry passengers, and the
proposed new navigation law of Premier Reid
leaves things in this respect exactly as they were.
The N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing Co., Ltd.,
of Moore Park, Sydney, has placed upon the mar-
ket a splendid Lager Bier called Sydney Lager
Bier. This bier is the choicest thing ever offered
to the people of N.S.W. Analysts praise it most
highly ; doctors recommend it to convalescents,
and connoisseurs at the last Sydney Agricultural
Show gave it first prize against all competitors.
What has long troubled the rational people of
N.S.W. is “what to drink.” There is no fun
in summer weather in drinking fiery alcoholio
liquors that break up the nerves, affect the diges-
tion, heart, liver and kidneys; and on the other
hand there is only an intense vagueness and total
dissatisfaction in drinking wish-wash. Sydney
Lager Bier is the happy mean between these ex-
tremes, and people in the country who desire to
have the newest and best drink going should com-
municate with the N.S.W. Lager Bier Brewing
Co., Moore Park, Sydney, for particulars. Al-
though the new lager is so much superior to all
others, it is the most reasonable in cost upon this
market.*
What enterprise, combined with quality,
will do for a business firm in Australia is exempli-
fied by the success here of Holbrook and Co., of
London and Birmingham. This firm began busi-
ness in Sydney only two years ago, yet its popu-
larity has already reached such a stage that during
last week 100,000 bottles —that is, 1680 cases of
Holbrook’s sauce—were sold from the Sydney
depOt. Such success proves that no English house
need have the least misgiving in sending a first-class
article into the Australian market. Holbrook’s, too,
have done wonderfully good business with their
pickles and “ pure malt ” vinegar ; proving again
that sterling quality brings Australian money into
a firm’s exchequer.

“Ligmolliene,” the new specific discovered
by W. H. Malyon, chemist, of Victoria, is a
wonderful remedy for skin diseases. Mr. Malyon
is constantly receiving testimonials from people
who have been cured by his preparation ; even
doctors have been cured by it, after all the other
known remedies had failed. When such authori-
ties testimonialise Ligmolliene its specific virtues
are beyond all question.*
A cool hundred dead cats and dogs and
such small deer were recently taken out of the
Yarra at Hawthorn bridge ; and the odors of
Smellbourne’s glorious sewer have become so warm
with the approach of summer that the authorities
have almost made up their minds to “ really do
something.” Down in Smellbourne, the way to
dispose of everything objectionable is to “chuck it
in the Yarra.” The corpse of a lady in a boot-
trunk was chucked in the other evening in the
usual course, and apparently sank or floated about
for some days before the police noticed it. Dr.
Neild, who post-mortemed, thinks the lady was
chloroformed as a preliminary to illegal operation,
and died of the chloroform. Anyway, Smell-
bourne has a fine large Xmas sensation, and the
Yarra continues to stink proudly to the sea.

ASSISTANTS Engaged for the Country.
“Malle”: Moreprenatal influence. Writer’s
grandfather, when a very young man, received, at
the battle of Assaye, a severe sword-cut on the
forehead ; the mark of which was reproduced on
his son (writer’s father) and was also very ap-
parent on writer himself in infancy—a plainly
distinguishable dent still remaining.
“0.Q.” : The patriarchal yarn about the
furniture-polish man who could not sell the stuff
he made until he labelled it “cough mixture,” is
practically the history of the late Joseph Bosisto.
Baron von Mueller had little or nothing to do with
B.’s early experiments. An educated but dilapi-
dated character, known as “Jack the Buster,”
first brought under Bosisto’s notice the subject of
the distillation of essential oils from the leaves of
indigenous trees. Many a time I have talked
with Jack on his way to Bosisto’s shop, with a
bag of leaves on his shoulder. Bosisto experi-
mented for a long time, his aim being to produce
varnish; and very excellent varnish it was, too.
But he could not get it to dry hard, so he bottled
it up and sold it as liniment. Poor old “ Jack the
Buster” died happily in the midst of one of his
periodical “ bursts.” His surname, I believe, was
Blackwell.

Queer letter received by N.S. Wales M.P.:
Sir, —I have been detained in Gladesville and Kenmore
Lunatic Asylums for about 4 years, though I am quite
sane. I wrote to the Minister for Justice some days ago,
asking for an inquiry into my case, but, so far, have not
received a reply. I wish you would favor me by mention-
ing my case to him when you find an opportunity. The
law that regulates my life is as follows : Moral, code—Do
not kill, murder, or shed blood. Do not commit adultery
or adulterate. Do not bear false witness or lie. Do not
steal. Marry one wife (mate). Six children, born at
three-vear intervals —are a lawful family. Marriage should
take place when the wife is 18 years of age, or at any sub-
sequent 3 year period. 40 acres of good agricultural land,
to be heldas joint property of husband and wife ; should
constitute home. While the male animals and birds should
be the property of the husband, the females should belong
to the wife. The common, intercommunicating through-
out the whole seaboard, should be free to all, subject to
timber regulations; sea and ports should be free. No
mining should take place on private or common lands,
except by permission of all the people. Naturally, my
diet law is vegetarian, which does not permit me to use
milk, butter, or eggs, or any dish into the composition of
which they enter. When the spirit of the above law is
enforced individually, the fall of man and all that fol-
lowed could almost be forgotten immediately.

AND IMPORTANT BRANCHES AS FOLLOWS :


Soul and Body.
On* Samuel Gibson, a confine* in N.S.W.
Trial Bay prison, had an eye destroyed by the
bursting of a steam gauge, and sued the Govern-
ment for compensation. Whereupon the Crown
raised the contention that, as Gibson was a con-
victed felon, there was no duty upon its part,
as between employer and to keep its
machinery in proper order so that it wouldn’t
explode and destroy Gibson’s eye. Judge
Owen, while admitting the Crown’s possible
moral responsibility in the matter, held that of
legal responsibility there was not a shred, and that
Gibson, ‘ 1 not being aservant of the Government,
and not being a member of the general public,
exercising equal rights with the public,” was,
in fact, nobody at all, and had no statutory
status for redress against the Government.
Now it seems that plaintiff, at the time of his
mishap, was very much indeed a servant of the
Government j inasmuch as he could not even
give up an employment which he might know
or apprehend to be dangerous. If Gibson, in
fact, had known that the steam-gauge was
liable to burst at any moment, he could not,
like a free Government have removed
himself from its dangerous vicinity. If this
isn’t being a servant of the most aggravated
type—well, what is it ? Judge Owen’s theory
is that the Crown isn’t liable because it didn’t
want its scalded to be there ; that he
was there voluntarily because he voluntarily
committed the act which brought him there.
But there are several large and obvious holes
in this logic. (1) Some men get there, not by
their voluntary act, but through miscarriage of
justice. (2) For a multitude of offences the
courts are authorised to allow the option of a
fine, and the man who is scalded by the
bursting of the Crown’s boiler often only gets
within reach of it because the law refused him
that option ; in other words, he is there partly
by his own act and partly by the court’s liver.
(3) The Crown virtually contracts itself out of
the impunity which Judge Owen claims for it.
It passes a definite sentence of, say, six months’
hard labor, not six months “hard” and any
other accidental damage which may accrue ;
and it should keep to the terms of its own sen-
tence. (4) Even if there is no option of a fine,
and the offence is one which must be expiated
by imprisonment, the law simply says, “ hard
labor,” and tending a rotten boiler or anything
of the kind isn’t specified in the statute. Tho
prisoner doesn’t voluntarily, by his own crimi-
nal act, put himself in danger of the Crown’s
unreliable gauge-glass, because the statute says
nothing about any such implement. Why
doesn’t the Crown keep to the letter of its bar-
gain, when it is so anxious to hold the prisoner
t o the letter of his ?
Let’s see : this year’s ’9B ; and next year’s
99 ; and the year after —’00 ?
The Bulletin hears from various quarters
that if, or when, N.S.W. Navigation Acts
Amendment Bill passes, an attempt will be
made to dump the ancient fossils of the Marine
Board into the new places of Navigation Super-
intendent and Assessors created by the Bill.
It is to be hoped there is enough grit in
the Assembly to block this move. The in-
capacity of many of the members of the
Marine Board has been proved over and over ;
most of them are too old for active and efficient
service, and turn their posts into sinecures ; and
all are parts of the bad existing system which it is
the business of the Amending Bill to abolish root
and branch. If the transfer is really accomplished,
and the old Board continues to rule the roost
under new names, it will be one of the worst jobs
which the Reid Cabinet has perpetrated.
The story of the loss of the N.S.W. Govern-
ment steamer, Little Nell, as told at the inquest:
Vessel narrow and rather crank ; captain drunk ;
had often been drunk, but still retained in Govern-
ment employ ; steered the little vessel in all direc-
tions till he got her broadside on to the sea and
she went under ; one boat on board—so securely
lashed down that it couldn’t be moved ; no life-
belts ; two life-buoys for five people. The Little
Nell was convoyed for safety by the larger and
slower vessel, Dawn, but the drunken captain
raced away from her, and she was far astern when
wanted. Two lives lost. Marine Board fast
asleep.
A beautiful example of the worthlessness
of N.S.W. Assembly’s check on Treasurer Reid’s
expenditure was given in the debate on the
“Greater Britain Exhibition” vote. This exhi-
bition is being run by a private Hungarian com-
pany in London with the assistance of English
guinea-pigs, and the only reason for spending
money on it is to keep Ministerial supporters
sweet with the pickings. Reid asked the House
to authorise the expenditure of .£5OOO. When it
was objected to he coolly said that he had already
spent an unauthorised .£4OOO, which would
be money thrown away if it wasn’t supple-
mented. Then he said that the total expense
would not exceed ,£Booo—which meant that
he foresaw the unauthorised expenditure of
-83000 beyond the .£5OOO asked for. Later still he
offered to guarantee the expense wouldn’t be more
than _i!10,000 so, as long as he secured some
sanction, he was going to stretch it like elastic.
Finally, the vote was reduced to .£4000; and Reid
said he would take that as an intimation to pay
outstanding liabilities and let the matter drop.
So that apparently the Ministry has spent .£4OOO
without the authority of Parliament; and nothing
at all results—it is money thrown away.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buver of all other Australian gems.*
The smash-up of Cervera’s Spanish ships
off Santiago de Cuba bore out the British deduc-
tion from the battle of Yalu River, that it is the
medium quick-firing gun that does the damage.
American naval experts who have examined the
pulverised Spanish hulls report that the big 12
and 13in. guns did little damage, whereas the
blizzard of quick-fired projectiles swept completely
through the ships and utterly demoralised the
men it didn’t kill.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

THE VILLAGE BLACKSMITH.
Gives up His Work.
SICK, SUFFERED and SAVED.
The Case of
Mr. Win. McCarthy.
(From the Windsor and Richmond Gazette).
BY OUR SPECIAL REPORTER.
Information having been conveyed to the
Gazette that Mr. McCarthy had recovered from
A Very Serious illness,
we at once detailed a reporter to make fall
enquiries into the matter, and also to per-
sonally interview McCarthy himself on the
subject. When our reporter arrived at
McGrath’s Hill, near Windsor, he found
whom he sought, powerfully wielding a
sledge hammer in his forge. The way that
Mr. McCarthy lifted up the heavy sledge
as though it weighed but a few ounces quite
astonished our reporter, for he had expected
to find the blacksmith in
A Weak State of Health.
“ How do you feel, asked our reporter ?”
“ Never felt better in my life,” replied the
blacksmith, and he put down his hammer
and entered straight away into a conver-
sation.
“You ask me what was the matter with
me,” said Mr. McCarthy, “ well, that is
what no one could tell me. My illness
came upon me as unexpectedly
As a Thunderbolt.
One day I was working in the forge when I
was seized with a violent headache. I had
to at once put down my tools and go into
the house.
From that day I date my sufferings. I
could no longer enjoy my food. In fact, in
a very short time eating caused me intense
pain, and I only took what food was abso-
lutely necessary to prevent my dying of
sheer starvation. When my food (and that
always of the lightest and most easily
digestible kind) was eaten it caused a painful
Distension in My Stomach.
Sour gas would rise in my throat, together
with an intensely bitter fluid. This, I
believe, is what is commonly called heart-
burn. My skin began to get of a dirty
yellow colour, and I started to lose weight
rapidly.”
“ How about your work V*
** I had to entirely
Give Up My Work,
as I was too weak to do any. Sleep became
almost entirely out of the question. I was a
victim to the worst form of sleeplessness.
Night after night I passed walking up and
down my bedroom, getting into bed and
again rising to pace the room in the vain
hope of inducing sleep. In the morning I
would be so weary that I could scarcely
stand, and yet was quite unable to obtain
The Blessing of Sleep.
How I did long for it, if but for half-an-
hour. No one who has not suffered as I did
can have the faintest idea of the advantage
of proper sleep. ”
“ What effect had this want of sleep upon
your nerves ?”
“ The very worst. I began to get horribly
nervous. I couldn’t bear the sight of
strangers. The most trifling noises would
send me into
A Fit of IVAiYiblinfi:.
I became extremely irritable, and in spite of
myself, I used to fall into a terrible state of
excitement about nothing.”
“Any other symptoms, McCarthy?”
“ Yes; strange pains now came on in my
back and loins. They were doubtless kidney
and liver troubles and extremely agonising,
and used to fairly wear me out, and then
there arose another stage of my illness
which was so dreadful that even now I
hardly care to talk about it. As you under-
stand, although I couldn’t work I could
still walk, and was accustomed to take a
stroll in open air on fine days in the
hope that the fresh air would do me good.
One day, when some distance from home,
without any warning, I saw everything
going black before me, and I fell down in a
fit. It was epilepsy, or the falling sickness,
and I was
Taken Home Insensible
in a cart. The state I was in for some time
after this I can hardly describe, I vomited
and was so weak I could hardly move. All
day long I sat in a dull, lifeless sort of a
way. I seemed to possess no interest in the
world that waa moving round me. I be*
came vei'y despondent and low spirited.”
“ Were doctors called in to see you V
“Oh, yes, and they prescribed for me,
but with no result. 5 *
“ How long were you ill altogether ?”
“I was ill for two years. Nothing
seemed to hold out any hope.”
“Did you suffer from further seizures ?”
“Yes, most unfortunately, I did. Not
long after the first fit I had another,
and then another. Each fit was worse than
the previous one, and my illness seemed to
be rapidly progressing
To a Fatal Close.
I was now in a terrible plight. All my
symptoms were intensified. I suffered more
than ever from want of sleep. I became
extremely weak and thin, and in a very
short time I dropped from list to 9st 61b—a
loss of 221 b. This is a
Tremendous Loss of Flesh
for a light man, and will speak more plainly
of my condition than any mere words could
do. My irritability and excitability had
become worse since my seizures. I was hope-
less and in despair. I was laid up in bed, and
never expected to leave it again. Once a
little book was brought me by a friend.
‘ Read it carefully, 5 he said. 1 did so. The
book was descriptive of Clements Tonic and
how it had effected cures in the apparently
Most Hopeless Cases.” (
" What did you do ?”
“ I sent for some and commenced a course
of it. After about a dozen doses I began to
notice a distinct improvement in the general
tone of my nerves. They gradually became
better in tone and less liable to cause me to
shake suddenly. My nervous system
gathered vitality every day. That
Gloomy, Foreboding Feeling
which had hung over me for so long began
to lift like a cloud.”
“ Could you eat and digest your food 2”
“Yes; with better spirits came a
renewed appetite, and Clements Tonic, I
found, had a wonderfully stimulating effect
on my digestive organs, and lastly, but by no
means least, sleep once more came to me.
Deep and Refreshing Sleep
was now the rule with me every night I
was soon out of bed and in the open air.
The exercise did me good, and as I continued
Clements Tonic my strength continually
improved.”
“ And what about the other pains ?’\
“ Those in the back and head all left me,
and was soon able to do some light work.
Very shortly Clements Tonic put me on my
legs again, as
Strong and Able a Man
as ever I was in my life.”
“And you had no more seizures, Mr.
McCarthy ?”
“ No; I firmly believe the epilepsy has
been completely driven out of my system,
and I don’t fear it now.”
“ You look in good health, anyhow 1”
“Yes, and can do as hard a day’s work
as any blacksmith in Australia.”
“ And can I put this in the Gazette ?'
“Yes, you can, and as I am known all
the country round anyone who requires
further particulars of my case can easily find
me? As you may guess I am extremely
grateful for my cure from what seemed a
life-long, if not fatal illness.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION 1 ,
1, WiT.tiAu McCartuv, of McGrath’s Hill, near
Windsor, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have care-
fully read the annexed document, consisting of eleven*
folios, and consecutively numbered from one to eleven,
and that it contains and is a true and faithful account
cf my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish the same in
any way; and I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of an Act made and passed in
the ninth year of the reign of her present Majesty,
intituled “ An Act for the more effectual abolition of
'Oaths and Affirmations taken and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New South Wales,
and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof, and for
the suppression of voluntary and extra-judicial Oaths
and Affidavits.”
Declared at Windsor this 20th day of July, 1898,
before me.
JAMES J. FITZPATRICK,
Justice of the Peace.
CLEMENTS TONIC has a record of cures none can rival. Hundreds of base
substitutes come on the market, the plausible advertising resorted to attracts the attention
of the thoughtless and unwary, and they have a sale for a year or two till they are found
out. For a medicine to remain popular for the number of years Clements Tonic has, it
must have exceptional merit. The Australian public is too well educated and too discriminate
ing to be gulled for long even by clever, if false advertisements, and that is the reason all
the quack nostrums run to the length of their tether in leu than four years in this country.

J. G. Jaensch, the first man to take up
River Murray country in S.A. for farming, was
recently killed by accident in that province.
Jaensch was one of the first of the German em-
ployes of the S.A. Co. ; his wage (he was getting
2s. 6d. a day) and the possibilities of the country
brought scores more out. Before he died he was
one of the largest and most successful farmers in
the S.A. hill-country.
Caldwell, the very bad poet of S.A.
Assembly, got up on his two boots in the course
of the discussion re the bill for keeping out un-
desirable niggers, and waded in to p>rove that Pro-
vidence meant Australia to be a nigger country.
He was laboring badly in a heavy sea when Glynn
asked him how it was, in that case, that Eve had
red hair, and he broke up. Caldwell, being a
heavy ironclad, couldn’t see the irrelevance of it
in time, and he went away to seriously look up
Eve’s hair in the “ Encyclopedia Britannica.”

Leopold Fane De Salis, of Tharwa, near
Queanbeyan, who died the other day at 84, was
brother of William Pane De Salis, who died in
England a few years ago after a remarkable and
pecuniarily successful career in many phases of
early Australian life. Banking was his special
forte. A painting of him bv Millais hangs in the
board-room of London Chartered Bank. The
early soundness of Victorian banking is largely
attributed to his administration. He wandered
through the South Sea Islands, most of China,
and a good part of the then known Australia, and
collected valuable curios as he went along. When
dying he made liberal bequests to several hospitals
in Australia, as well as in England. He was so re-
markably like Gladstone in appearance that people
often followed him in the streets of London. To
Leopold, now deceased, he left <£3oo a year, with
an interest in <£20,000 and blocks of land in various
countries.

THE UNWELCOME GUEST-
WHERE HE COMES. HOW HE COMES.
WHY HE COMES.
ħ
Death
comes a n
unw e 1 -
come guest
to all ; yet
many
people un-
conscious-
ly invite
him.
They are
the people
who neg -
lect them -
selves;
who, be -
coming ill,
go on day
to day till
disease
overtakes
them, and
death that
is prema -
ture be-
comes in -
evitable.
Perhaps you are one of those people, and are not
aware of it. You have something the matter
with you, but, not having any experience, treat it
lightly, while it gets worse and worse, untimately
destroying you.
The wise man will at once put himself in the
witness-box, and ask himself the following ques-
tions, as will the wise woman :
Do I sleep badly ; do I wake up in the morning,
even when I appear to have slept all night, fagged
and jaded, with lack-lustre eyes and an inclination
only to go to bed again. Do I eat badly, or, when
Ido eat “well,” does my food agree with and
nourish me ? Do Igo about my work dejectedly—
do I suffer from headaches, pains in either the
limbs or body, and indulge in depressing retros-
pections ? Do I, in short, suffer from a loss of
mental and physical elasticity—that exultant
appreciation of life which all healthy people must
have ?
If to even one of these questions the answer be
“ Yes,” then that man realises that he wants a
tonic—something to give him an appetite, to
steady his nerves, and to make him strong and
active in the daytime, and afford him calm and
refreshing sleep at night.
Such a tonic is Minerva Tonic, the world’s
greatest and only permanent restorative, the
praise of which is general and unstinted.
Professor Macadam, Consulting Chemist to the
British Government, writes : —“ Minerva is a
judicious combination of iron, phosphorus, and
alkaloid compounds, which will be found highly

dose is generally sufficient, and a complete cure is certain.
“ Mr. W. G. HEARNE. —Dear Sir, —I had a had attack
of Bronchitis for six months, with dreadful hacking cough

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

On Widows and Other Women.
A few words by a woman on the maid, the widow,
and the Girl that is conventionally Lost:—
In her relation to the other sex, the widow is at the
greatest advantage. Why ? Because she is maid, wife,
widow in one. She carries into wifehood something of
the maid ; she carries into widowhood something of the
wife. She is Woman intensified. The virgin poetically
extolled as the one true type of womanhood, is, in reality,
something of the man-type. Like him, she is timid
socially, brave physically. Ask any woman that has
borne children (someone you knew as an Amazonic, out-
of-doors damsel), and she will tell you that though she is
as strong mentally and bodily as before, she could not do
what she used to do. Her horse takes her the safe, long
path now, not the short cut by the gully ; she is afraid to
be alone at night, etc., etc. Marriage has deepened in her
all that is essentially feminine, and completely obliterated
the masculine fibre of her maiden days.
* * • •
There are two sexes in the human world: men and
women are one, widows the other.
• • * *
The conventional idea is that, when a man has
had experiences that should be marital, and are not, his
prospects in the matrimonial world are by no means in-
jured ; but that, when a woman has ventured to rehearse
beforehand, her chance of shortening the mistress to the
Mrs. are nil. Theoretically, they are nil; practically,
they are not. Observe, there is always a halo of romance
about the girl who falls. She may be everything else, but
she is never ridiculous. The spinster, after a certain age,
is essentially ridiculous—she receives men’s conventional
esteem, but the other gets the real thing. The more
respectable the less respected. Miss Slimmens, with her
virginal 40-year-old curls, who keeps a boarding-house to
beguile the Government-clerk into lawful matrimony—
that’s comedy. But who ever brings Phryne on to
the stage in order to laugh at her ? The girl who can fall
naturally and gracefully has a future before her. Litera-
ture, which is masculine, is tear-sodden with beautiful-
snow allusions. No woman ever writes lovely, sad poems
about the fallen man. There are no masculine equiva-
lents for the “ringless bride,” “hunted huntress,”
“high priestess of humanity,” and so forth. In reality
there is as much romance about the one as the other.
That is to say, there is none at all. Delilah, who pleads
for custom at the corner of the street, is merely an ordi-
nary young woman who wants to get better clothes and
better food without troubling to work for them. I do
not say there is anything wrong in this; it is very wrong
to say anything is wrong nowadays. She may be a very
nice girl for all I know ; but I deny that there is any
romance about her. At the risk of being considered
narrow-minded, I maintain chat she is no better than the
virtuous woman.
• « * *
It is popularly supposed that what man admires most
in woman is chastity. And if chastity made the hair
grow and likewise acted as a complexion-restorer doubt-
less this would be true enough. As it is, in practice
though not in theory, he likes other things a great
deal better. In fact, if he has to choose between the
two, he will cheerfully let the chastity slide. A man does
not actuallv prefer a woman to be fallen, but he likes her
to be a little bit slanting, especially if she slants in his
direction. Even if she has slanted in somebody else’s
direction before he came on the scene, he doesn't mind so
much as is generally imagined. So long as there is no
baby, that is. A baby makes things too real. Sometimes
he will even stand the baby, and that brings us back to
the widow.
» * » «
Oobbett in his “ Advice to Young Men ” speaks strongly
and coarsely against the re-marriages of widows—“ For
after all, who can look at her without reflecting that she
has for a second time undergone that surrender,” &c.,
&c. This is how you should imagine a man would reflect.
But then a man never does reflect. Man, more imagina-
tive than woman, uses his imagination far afield. He
does not waste it in gilding his own life. He lives his life
but once. Woman flies backwards and forwards on
Wells’ Time Machine; she lives her honeymoon ere it
arrives, and has named all her children long before she
has received her first proposal. Man’s jealousy is purely
for the present; it is never retrospective. The jilted
hero leaves his sweetheart because he “ will not see her
in the arms of another,’’ which is ridiculous, by the
way, for he isn’t expected to be there when she
is in the arms of another. But he can bear to
think that she has been in the arms of another, ten, five,
or two years ago—so long as the Church has blessed the
union. * The thing itself does not appal him ; it is merely
his conventionality that is liable to be shocked. For man
loves to be virtuous by proxy ; and his wife is his God-
given whipping-girl.
* * * *
And to conclude—ln the eyes of man, the greatest vir-
tue about a woman is the fact that some other man wants
her. Man is not attracted by woman, but by the attrac-
tion of the other man. This is not true of women.
Nothing sickens a sensible woman sooner of her love for
a man than the knowledge that other women, whom
she despises, have the audacity to share her emo-
tions. But man likes to be one of a crowd. Now the
widow shows by the mere fact of her widowhood that
someone has wanted her. There is the fact patent to all
men that someone has asked her, someone has had her,
someone has lived with her, and hasn’t died. At least he
is dead, but he didn’t die of that And to know that
some other man has gone through it before, is a great
consolation toMan—less ready to plunge into the Unknown
than Woman, probably because he knows more about it.
And now you see why the Girl that is Conventionally
Lost is placed, as far' as matrimonial chances are con-
cerned, above the maid and next the widow. She, too,
in her simple, humble way has been Preferred.
The inferior Judge isn’t always quite the
stupendous potentate that he looks, and some-
times things happen to him in the disrespectful
country Out Back that are better left unreported.
Out in the wilderness you hear strange stories at
times about His ’Onah, and the things he did, and
the things that were done to him, and his drinks,
and his girls, and other matters. About the
wildest episode of the lot happened many
years ago to a certain Australian inferior
Judge who is still alive. His ’Onah was
sour and aggressive and cantankerous; but
he always put his tail down in the end if the
other party got really angry. He was staying at
a bakblox hotel once while out on duty, and the
first night of his arrival the house was full of
navvies all drinking themselves into the horrors.
The noise was terrific, and every now and then
His ’Onah came to the door of his sitting-room to
make loud, sour remarks about it. Consequently
he grew steadily more and more unpopular with
the crowd of road-menders, and several times
they yelled up the stair to him to “ shut up.” He
went to bed, but the racket still continued. Then
he arose again, and came to the top of the stair in
his nightshirt, and denounced the pick-and-shovel
men down below in such scathing language that a
riot broke out on the instant. Three of them
rushed the stair. They were all woefully unsteady,
and His ’Onah was a good-sized man, with a
strong position at the head of a narrow stairway,
and, if he had defended the situation with a
water-jug, he might possibly have routed the
enemy. But, instead, he gave a short, sudden
yelp, and fled into his bedroom, where he tripped
over a chair and fell across the bed. The
attitude was so convenient that two ribald
road-menders promptly held His ’Onah there,
face-downwards, while the third flicked him
severely with a wet towel. The distressed
landlady stood outside the door and implored
them to stop, but she couldn’t come in and
actually interfere owing to the Judge not being in
a state to receive company. Then the road-
menders poured a jug of beer over His
’Onah as a siirn of disrespect, and retired
shrieking with mirth and whisky. Nothing
came of the outrage When the crowd sobered-up
it cleared out hurriedly for fear of getting three
years And as His’Onah couldn’t well prosecute
fa Judge who went into the witness-box and de-
tailed how he had been swished with a wet
towel and had beer poured over him would
be a laughing-stock for ever afterwards) he
also cleared out as soon as possible and said
nothing But sentences were very large on that
particular circuit and people noticed that His
’Onah’s liver was something awful.

The law-trouble over the M‘Kellar tomb-
stone (Vic.), and the disinclination of the legatees
to part with their siller in payment thereof, re-
calls the sensible will of the late bediamonded Dr.
Beaney, who left a large amount to be expended
on his own marble tablet. Some people say he
left the inscription as well, to render the romantic
affair complete. He knew his way about, and
didn’t trust to the dubious affection of his heirs
and successors in such post-mortem reminiscences.
Any man who wants to lie under a good tomb-
stone, and to be properly lied-over by it, had best
buy it himself.

The Geological SpieleTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Steelman and Smith struck the new railway-line
just above the last embankment. They had been
battling round, with very moderate success, for
some months, down there in the region of “Juno ”
tobacco and square, japanned tin matchboxes.
Their luck was running just a few inches above
sea-level, as it were; they were “just living ”■—
“ knocking round and hanging out.’' They were
making for Wanganui, where Steelman hoped to
“get on to something,’’ and working the likely
towns by the way. Steelman had a scheme —for
“ raising stuff ” —in connection with certain local
affairs and persons in Wanganui, with which town
he was very well, though cautiously and discreetly,
acquainted.
• • • *
Steelman carried a black bag containing half-a-
dozen clean collars, a packet of official-looking
envelopes and documents—for the sake of certain
appearances ; three union tickets, one “ loyalty ”
strike ticket, and eight loaded dice; a leather
dice-box of his own invention and one on which
he could depend, yet not differing in appearance
from the boxes in common use round there ; a
pack of carefully-marked cards, a Bible, a copy of
“Innocents Abroad,” and “A Journey to the
Centre of the Earth,” by Jules Verne, a few
printed sermons clipped from a religious periodical,
a book of recitations, a song-book, a collection of
“ good yarns ” in manuscript—in a pocketbook,
and other various, curious, and mysterious
small properties, necessary to ths profession of a
gentleman-spieler, which it would be an unpardon-
able breach of faith to particularise.
# * - * ♦
Smith had a bulky, bulgy carpet bag with some
clothing in it; a couple of caps, a spare hat,
about a pound of “Juno” tobacco—all in little
bits and wrapped in a towel to keep it moist;
spare pipes, shaving tackle, and a Maoriland
directory. He also had a billy full of small
groceries, a couple of blankets, some more clothes,
spare boots, and various little camp belongings
usually found in swags—needles, thread, pens and
ink, soap and towels, pocket-book and note-paper,
soap, comb and brush, boot laces, string, &c.—
the whole in a canvas bag—like a small sailor’s
bag—which he carried swag fashion on his back.
Steelman also carried it sometimes, but not too
far nor often. In the baggage were also a long
rusty black coat of clerical cut, and a wide-
brimmed, black, soft felt hat, such as might be
worn by an unemployed laborer in the Lord’s
vineyard. Steelman labored in the Lord’s vine-
yard when things were slack in the other.
m m * *
The men were at work in the cutting and on the
embankment with their drays, barrows and
“ jacks.” It was a fine sunny day as yet, and the
smoke rose faintly blue from the “ burning off.”
Down through the cutting and across a curve at
the far end the railway camp was located.
Steelman stepped off the track, out of sight of
the workmen, sat down on a log, and dropped his
swag on the grass. Smith did likewise. Steelman
rested his head on his hand, his elbow on his knee,
and got ready to think.
“ Fill my pipe, Smith,” he said wearily, draw-
ing the pipe from his pocket in a tired, abstracted
way, with his disengaged hand, and handing the
briar to Smith without looking at it or at him.
“ Fill my pipe, Smith, and don’t talk now—l

beautiful complexion, and made me most strong and always
energetic. Formerly, I was very thin and fearfully depressed in
spirits ; whilst my appetite failed me altogether. My sleep used to
be restless and disturbed, and I was so tired in the mornings that
I could hardly get out of bed, and when I did I used to lie on the
sofa, feeling I hardly cared what happened. I saw a doctor, who
said I needed a strong
tonic, and I had several
bottles of physio from
him, besides tonics from
chemists ; but I grew no
better. I was in this
miserable and hopeless
condition when I heard

Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,

Pale Girls Made Pretty.
Upon parents rests a great responsibility at the time their
daughters are budding into womanhood. If your daughter is pale,
complains of weakness, is “ tired out ” upon the slightest exertion ;
if she is troubled with headache or backache, pain in the side, if
her temper is fitful and her appetite poor, she is in a condition of
extreme peril, a fit subject for the most dreaded of all diseases—
Consumption. If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They will assist the
patient to develop properly and regularly ; they will enrich the
blood and restore health’s roses to the cheeks ; bright eyes and
lightness of step will surely follow their use, and all danger of con-
sumption and premature death will be averted.

“A Mere Skeleton-”
A DEBILITATED MAN FILLED WITH LIFE AND VIGOR.
Blood disorders and blood impoverishments (weakness) lead to a
host of specific diseases, some fatal, others not fatal. Diarrhoea and
dysentery are two of the vvorst results of this weakness, and deaths
from this source are of daily occurrence. Hence every precaution
should be taken to avoid the initial weakness, and thus ward off its
invariably fearful consequences. Mr. W. Filmer, of Abbott-street,
West Maitland, N.S.W. where he is a highly-respected resident—
thus tells of his narrow escape from death :
“ For from twenty-five to thirty years I suffered from ehronio
diarrhoea,” he says. “At times I was affected in a most alarming
manner, and notwithstanding all my care with regard to diet, etc.,
I suffered terribly. I fell away to a mere skeleton, grew weak, losb
all confidence in my powers, and, finally, got so debilitated that
after an attack I have been seized with fainting fits which left me
in a prostrate and helpless condition. My medical adviser ordered
a change last November, ’97, as a last resource. During my absence
from Maitland I met a friend in Newcastle who had been a sufferer
in the same manner as myself for 10 years, and, on his advice, I re-
solved to give Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills a trial. In January, ’9B, I
procured the pills, and the result was most satisfactory from the
outset. My head grew clearer, my appetite increased, and I gradu-
ally grew stronger, and now, after all my years of suffering, I can
most conscientiously say that 3§ or 4 boxes of these famous pills
have brought about my ultimate recovery ; to-day I actually feel
better than I have done for the past 30 years, and during the last
nine months my weight has increased thirteen pounds. In sending
this testimonial testifying to the good qualities of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, I am performing my duty, so that other sufferers can
know for a.surety that a remedy is procurable that will make their
troubles things of the past. Should I ever again be attacked in a
similar manner, I would have no hesitation in taking these pills,
and would feel quite certain of their giving me almost instant
relief.”
