The pipes were filled. It was part of Smith’s
duty to keep the firm supplied with tobacco ; but
h°w he did it, under Steelman’s training is
another story. That the tobacco was all m little
bits might ?ive a hint.
They smoked awhile in a silence which was
sterMy thoughtful on Steelman s part, and
patiently discreet on the part of Smith He had
trained himself to appear unconscious of his near
surroundings on such an occasion. He sat looking
before him with as vacant an expression as he
could assume H.s chief anxiety was not to
look resigned, for Steelman hated to see a re-
signed expression on Smiths face—“an injured
look ” he called it. Smith found it a good plan
to try to look interested in the background of the
scenery sometimes. He found it a very bad plan
to take the slightest notice of Steelman, or even
appear aware of his presence when he fSteelman)
was thinking writing, or mending his clothe*
Smith knew Steelman, and Steelman understood
human nature.
„*,* , * * ,
Fresently Steelman rose, took up his bag, retraced
his steps along the track a little way, and turned
off into the edge of a belt of dead timber—green
with parasites. Smith followed. Steelman opened
his bag, took out two clean collars, handed one
to Smith, and put the other on himself.
“Now the cap, Smith,” he said. “No—hold
011 J— the wideawake.”
Smith opened the carpet-bag and handed out
the broad-brimmed hat, with which Steelman
replaced the hard-hitter he had been wearing.
“You’d better get out the billy, make a fire,
and heat a drop of water. I. want to have a
shave.”
femith started to gather some sticks. Steelman
meanwhile regarded himself critically in a second-
hand glass, which he carried in the black bag.
“ Never mind, Smith,” he said. “ I’ll do better
with ( the stubble on. Give me your grey sao-
coat -”
Smith obeyed without words, though he had
fully expected to be asked for the clerical gar-
ITien t.
“ Now then. Fix up and come along.”
He started back down the edge of the cutting,
Smith followed, still discreetly silent. His be-
haviour seemed to meet with the approval of
Steelman, who presently said :
“ Walk up alongside me, Smith.”
Smith did so, although he had to walk over
stones and tussocks, for the path was not wide
enough for two.
“ Lend me your lug, Smith.” >
Smith intimated, by a slight sideway movement
of his head, that his “lug”—otherwise his ear—
was at Steelman’s disposal.
“ Now, the main thing is to get some tucker and
shelter for a few days. There’s bad weather com-
ing on, I think”—pointing to the south-east—
“ there’s more weather down there than the moon
can swallow up. And, besides, I don’t want to
strike Wanganui for a fortnight yet. We want
all the money we’ve got—it’s only a mug that
parts with his money unless absolutely forced to
it-” 4 _
“ Y es, I see,” said Smith.
“ Well, now, bring both your lugs to bear on
mB <; m n B’ eo i°S' s t-”
“I see.” t
“ But you don’t see. I’ll show yon. Now
listen—get that vacant look off your face ! ”
Smith got as much of it off as ha could on such
abrupt notice.
“ I’m a geologist, travelling in the interests of
science and for my own amusement. See? ”
“ Y ee t RPft »
“ No,’you don’t Give me a show, for God’s
sake. I’m well off-well enough off to settle down
and b e an independent man for the rest of my
life. But I couldn’t settle down and rest con-
tented, for my heart and soul’s in geology, and if
I had a million pounds cash I wouldn’t rest-I’d
have to travel round and poke round amongst old
rocks and rubbish ; and the more I had, the more
I’d root round. I’m a member-vice-president
and half-a-dozen other things, in fact-of a
learned (learn-ned, remember), learn-ned society
in Sydney ” J
“ Why not London, Steely ? ”
“ Because,” said Steelman, rather impatiently,
“ Sydney is the London of these Maorilanders
You see,” he continued, condescendingly, “ you
seldom hear of London or Melbourne over in
these parts, but you always hear of Sydney.
Every second yokel’s ambition is to see Sydney
before he dies. . . . You’re my man—mysorvant,
or valet, or whatever you like to call it.”
“I see.”
“Well, don’t you forget it. You don’t know
much about me; you’ve only been travelling with
me for about six or seven months. I’m an Oxford
University man you think, and not long out. I’m
a bit eccentric, I’m cracked on geology” but that’s
only to be expected. I’m gone on science right
enough, but you’ve got no call to complain against
me as a boss. I always treated you square and
straight. You’ve got an easy berth—it just suits
you to loaf round and see the country, and live on
the fat of the land, and get paid for it. You’ve
got nothing to do except to arrange for accommo-
dation, carry a bag or two when off the coach or
rail route, and dig up a stone now and then,
You get your salary, and all expenses paid.’
You got this billet through answering an adver-
tisement in Wellington. Your boss doesn’t
like to talk much about himself, but he’s all there
on rocks. That will about do to begin with.
They won’t expect you to talk much about your
boss’s business—they’ll respect you all the more
for being a bit close about it. We’ve been
travelling round pretty near by the same route
that we did actually come by—only we took the
train and coach whenever we could. We’ll
arrange all about that to-night if we catch-on. If
they seem to want to know any more about it
you’ll have to think it up, or ask me on the quiet;
but always give me the office either before or im-
mediately after you commit yourself to anything,
for it won’t do for us to have different opinions
about our recent travels. We might run against
some brains down there amongst those navvies,
You know your own pitch about yourself beforo
you met me.”
“ That’s alright.”
“ By-the-way, have you got that Jules Verne’s
1 Journey to the Centre of the Earth ’ safe in your
ba ff—j lO . IVe got it; I might want to look through
again to-night.”
Smith now saw where Steelman got his idea
and confidence from ; but he knew better than say
anything.
“You see,” continued Steelman, good-
naturedly—or perhaps he hadn’t the fullest confi-
dence in his own scheme, and sought comfort
from begging the question, even with Smith—
“ you see, geology can always be made interest-
ing, even to ignorant men, especially if you have
a wall to illustrate by, like the side of that cutting
there, that’s studded with fossil mussels and petri-
fied oysters and fish that were stale in Noah’s
time and fresh about the year one million 8.A.”
Pause.
“ What’s * 8.A.,’ Steely? ” asked Smith.
“ ‘ Before Adam,’ ” said Steelman with a short
grin.
Smith smiled his sickly smile and asked :
“ What society do you belong to, Steelman ? ”
“ Oh, yes. I’d alvwst forgot that. There’s
more in your head than I thought, Smithy. Let’s
see—l wish we had an Australian Directory with
us.”
I. wish we had,” said Smith. But he didn’t
wish it. He formed an idea of the size of an
Australian directory as compared with the bulk
and weight of the Maoriland one he carried from
the extent of Australia on the map, as compared
with the size of Maoriland. He had enough to
carry already.
“ Let’s see,” said Steelman. “The Royal
Geolo No—the Royal Geographical Society
of Australia.’ I’ve seen the name somewhere, and
there’s sure to be a branch in Geology. I’d better
be vice-president, or chairman, or something—the
* vice ’ always sounds better than the real thing.
. . . No; now I come to think of it, ‘ honorary
member ’ will suit best and be safest, in case we
run against some brains down there amongst those
navvies.
“ And another thing, Smith. If you happen to
get merry with any of those chaps some night,
you might let your tongue loose a bit, and make
out that geology’s all bosh, and you’re sick of
hearing about it, and, if you had half my money,
you’d be damned if you’d go rooting round in the
mud, like a blessed old sunstruck ant-eater. And
you might refer to me, when I’m not in hearing,
as ‘ Old Rocks over there ’ —it’ll amuse the chaps,
and make the thing look more natural—but don’t
let me hear you at it, or I’ll give you the waddle
(the sack) on the spot, and stoush you afterwards.”
He grinned good-naturedly, and Smith smiled
his tired smile.
“ By-the-way, what’s your name, Steely ? ” he
asked.
“ Oh, yes, that’s right. Glad you reminded me
of it. You see, I can’t be expected to do all the
brain-work —there’s a limit to the biggest heads.
. . . I’m beginning to think,” he continued,
absently, but with a quiet grin for himself, “ I’m
beginning to think that men who are struck with
great ideas are often missed by small ones.” And
he mused awhile.
“I once heard a fellovv say,” murmured Smith,
innocently—“ or I seen it in a book, I’m not sure
which—that big ideas are always simple, and,
consequently, they strike fools first, and that the
fools get to be wise men afterwards through think-
ing over them.”
Steelman turned slowly, and regarded him with
astonishment, but Smith’s face was about as
idiotio as it usually was ; he wondered what he’d
done now.
“You should have been an educated man,
Smith,” said Steelman; then, after a pause, “I
don’t know whether to be proud of you or not.
It’s a good job you haven’t got brains enough to
see where you’re driving. But never mind; let’s
to business. My name’s Stoneleigh, 8.A.,
F.R. A.G. ; but you can’t remember all that; say
that you reckon there’s a dozen letters sticking up
after your boss’s name, like a funeral in wet
weather.”
“ Hold on ! What’s my name ? ” asked Smith,
as they stepped down on to the level.
“Smith—same as your own—James Smith,”
whispered Steelman, hurriedly. “ I’ll call you
James. Now hold your tongue and keep your
wits about you.”
* * * *
“ We had a geologist staying in our camp once,
for a week,” said a man who had worked with a
co-operative party on a new railway-line down
yonder in Maoriland. “He was a queer card.
He'd sent his traps on by coach and was tramping
it along the line looking at the country. He
seemed mighty interested in that piece of country,
too. He had his man with him, and vve got ’em
to stay with us a few days. He could read the
side of a cutting like a book, and tell us what
period every ‘ strarter ’ or ‘ layer,’ as he called it,
belonged to. It was raining most of the time,
and we had nothing to read, and Lord knows
how we’d have got on without that geologist
joker to keep us alive. Every time the rain
held up for an hour or so he’d call ‘James,’ and
go out and poke round the line, come back with a
bag full of old rocks, and lecture on ’em. He
could do it, too. Some of the boys were never
tired of listening to him. And he’d plenty of
good yarns if you could only get him away from
geology for awhile. He was worth any amount of
money, too, I believe; but, you know, those
scientific men will follow up their fads to their
dying days. I reckon we learnt a lot from that
chap—we got lessons and lectures every day for a
week and gave nothing for ’em—only a share of
the whare and the bit of grub him and his man
eat. You ought to have seen the chaps sitting
round the fire and listening to him all eyes and
mouths and ears, when he got right back to the
times when there were frogs as big as cows, and
when men went naked and lived in caves, and
knocked each other’s brains out with lumps of
stone tied onto sticks. The ‘ stone age ’ he called
it.”
HENRY LAWSON.
Two Sonnets.
I.
Ah me ! that quiet grave-yard on the hill !
Thither the mourners with their burdens go,
Their heads bow’d down, their footsteps sad and
slow ;
There lie my dead—and yet beside the rill
The children laugh their loudest; as they will
Those happy lovers loiter to and fro.
Life hath no break in its continuous flow,
And oti those mounds the sunshine streameth still.
Yet what avails it that the glory lies
Upon the hill ? What comfort can it give
To my dear dead, or unto me who live
Bereav’d ? Alas, there is no solace there )
Indeed, I find no comfort anywhere,—
The race endures —the individuai dies 1
ii.
The lilies bud and blossom, droop and die ;
The apples redden, mellowing to decay ;
The noiseless years steal on and glide away,
And here the living leave their dead,—while I
I know full well mine own time draweth nigh.
Yet welcome we the New Year as we may,
And Spring shows fairer flowers day by day,
And fresh fruits ripen as the months roll by.
Ah ! what avails it that we leave our love
With our lone dead upon the quiet hill ?
Time has a sharp, sweet medicine that cures
The heartache; and the wide world’s life is still
Eternal, as the silent stars above, —
The individual dies —the race endures !
DORA WILCOX.


Oh, the Multitude Who Say It!
Speaking of another world than this, a certain
Book, from which so many draw comfort in times
of trouble, says : “ The inhabitant never saith, £
am sick.” But in this world the air is full of the
weary, wailing cry, “I am sick,” “I suffer so,”
“ Can nothing be done to rid me of pain ?
Here is another case of it another out of a
number beyond all counting or imagining. Yet
this one got well. Do you want to hear about it ?
or do you know of any pain-racked mortal who
might like to hear about it? Then read, either
for yourself or for someone else. We give the facts
exactly as they were given to us.
“ In the early part of 1889 I began to suffer from
illness. I had at first a bad taste in my mouth,
and belched up a sour, gaseous fluid. My appetite
was poor, and, after eating, I had a burning pain
at my chest, and palpitation of the heart. I had
also a gnawing, grinding sensation at the pit of the
stomach, with a feeling of sinking, as though I
were being bodily lowered into a pit. My food
never seemed to settle, but repeated, and I had to
spit it out; this going on until my stomach was
empty.
“I lost a deal of sleep at night. I got very weak
and thin, losing over a stone in weight; I was so
feeble that I could hardly drag myself along. As
time went on I grew irritable and nervous, and
took no interest or pleasure in anything.
“ Sometimes better and at other times worse, I
continued in this way until August of last year
(1893), when I had to give up my employment as
collier at the Waterloo Main Colliery, where I
had been at work for seven years. At this time I
had got so bad that I sat in my chair from morn-
ing till night, too weak and prostrated to go about.
When I did venture out I dare not go alone for
fear I should fall down in the street.
“ At times a strange feeling came over me, as if
my henrt had stopped beating; and on one of
these occasions I felt as if I were dying, and said
tomy wife, ‘Lass, it's all up with me
“ During my long illness I consulted several
doctors, and they all said my heart was sound,
and that the trouble was all from my stomach and
liver. They gave jjne medicines and recommended
a change of air. I therefore went to Blackpool,
Scarborough, and Whitby, but was no better either
from the change or frpm the doctor’s physic.
‘ ‘ One day a book was left at my house telling
about Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and I
thought it might possibly help me. So I got a
bottle from Mr. Webster, chemist, Green Road,
and when 1 had finished that bottle I found relief,
for my food no longer repeated and the pain at
my heart was easier. I kept on with the medicine
and soon my appetite returned, and I could eat
without feeling any pain or distress. Gradually I
improved, getting stronger and stronger every
day. I can now eat anything, and do my work
with pleasure. I feel quite a new man, and con-
sider that Mother Seigel’s Syrup has saved my
life. Yours truly, (Signed) George Hopton, 25
Bayswater Terrace, Roundhay Road, Leeds,
February Bth, 1894.”
No words of ours can add an ounce to the
weight of this plain and manly letter. From it,
as from a hilltop, we can see the sad picture of
those four years of misery. The discouraged and
suffering husband, the patient, burden-bearing,
helpful wife. Heaven be praised that it all ended
as it did—in renewed health—instead of as it
might have ended. The disease was that insatiate
fiend and monster —indigestion and dyspepsia;
cunning as a fox, destructive as a man-eating
tiger. In this ease, as in so many others, Mother
Seigel’s Syrup demonstrated that power which has
made it celebrated all over the world—in palace
and in hut, wherever the inhabitant saith, I am
sick. If you are one of these, try it now. If you
are healthy and hearty, thank God for that, and
pass the news of George Hopton’s illness and re-
covery on to any who need to hear it.
